    **Title:** GLP-1/Notch Signaling Facilitates Cell-Fate Reprogramming in C. elegans Germline through Antagonism with Polycomb Repressive Complex 2

    **Research Question:** How does the GLP-1/Notch signaling pathway contribute to cell-fate reprogramming in the C. elegans germline, and what are the underlying molecular mechanisms?

    **Methods:** We employed a combination of genetics, tissue-specific transcriptome analysis, and functional studies of candidate genes to investigate the role of GLP-1/Notch signaling in cell-fate reprogramming in the C. elegans germline.

    **Main Findings:** Contrary to its established role in maintaining undifferentiated germline stem cells/progenitors, we discovered that GLP-1/Notch signaling promotes cell-fate reprogramming by activating specific genes silenced by the Polycomb Repressive Complex 2 (PRC2). We identified the conserved histone demethylase UTX-1 as a crucial GLP-1/Notch target facilitating reprograming. Notably, we found that GLP-1/Notch and PRC2 have an antagonistic effect on germ cell-fate decisions, with GLP-1/Notch activating genes repressed by PRC2.

    **Implications:** These findings have significant implications for our understanding of development and diseases associated with abnormal Notch signaling. The discovery of GLP-1/Notch's role in promoting cell-fate reprogramming highlights the complex interplay between signaling pathways and chromatin regulators in regulating cell fate decisions.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals a novel function of the GLP-1/Notch signaling pathway in facilitating cell-fate reprogramming in the C. elegans germline through antagonism with PRC2. The identification of UTX-1 as a critical GLP-1/Notch target provides insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying this process. These findings contribute to our understanding of the intricate relationships between signaling pathways and chromatin regulators in regulating cell fate decisions, with potential implications for the development of novel therapeutic strategies for diseases associated with abnormal Notch signaling.

    **Title:** SKAP2-dependent Signaling in Neutrophils is Essential for Clearance of Klebsiella pneumoniae Infections

    **Research Question:** What is the role of the adaptor protein SKAP2 in host defense against Klebsiella pneumoniae (K. pneumoniae) pulmonary infections?

    **Methods:** We used a murine model of K. pneumoniae 43816 infection to investigate the role of SKAP2 in pulmonary host defense. SKAP2-deficient (Skap2-/-) mice were compared to wild-type mice in terms of bacterial burden, neutrophil function, and reactive oxygen species (ROS) production.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that Skap2-/- mice had a 100-fold higher bacterial burden compared to wild-type mice, indicating that SKAP2 is essential for protection against K. pneumoniae infections. SKAP2-dependent signaling in neutrophils was found to be critical for K. pneumoniae-induced ROS production, which is a key mechanism for bacterial clearance. In contrast, neutrophil degranulation and phagocytosis were not affected by SKAP2 expression. Furthermore, we identified a signaling cascade involving Src family kinases (SFKs), Syk, Btk, PLCγ2, and PKC that is required for K. pneumoniae-induced ROS production, and SKAP2 was found to be proximal to their activation.

    **Implications:** Our study highlights the importance of SKAP2-dependent signaling in neutrophils for host defense against K. pneumoniae infections. The loss of SKAP2 significantly hindered the K. pneumoniae-induced phosphorylation of SFKs, Syk, and Pyk2, indicating that SKAP2 plays a critical role in K. pneumoniae-induced signaling pathways. The sensitivity of K. pneumoniae to ROS is supported by the increased bacterial burden in Cybb-/- mice and observations in chronic granulomatous disease (CGD) patients.

    **Conclusions:** In conclusion, our study demonstrates that SKAP2-dependent signaling in neutrophils is essential for K. pneumoniae-activated ROS production and for promoting bacterial clearance during infection. These findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms of host defense against K. pneumoniae infections and highlight the potential of SKAP2 as a therapeutic target for the treatment of K. pneumoniae infections.

    **Title:** Modeling the Hypnozoite Reservoir in Plasmodium vivax Malaria: Implications for Transmission and Control

    **Research Question:** How does the hypnozoite reservoir in the liver contribute to the transmission dynamics of Plasmodium vivax malaria, and what are the implications for malaria control interventions?

    **Methods:** We developed a within-host mathematical model of liver-stage hypnozoites, which was validated against data from tropical strains of P. vivax. The model was then embedded in a P. vivax transmission model to simulate the build-up of the hypnozoite reservoir following new infections and its depletion through hypnozoite activation and death.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that the hypnozoite reservoir is over-dispersed, with many individuals having few or no hypnozoites, while others have intensely infected livers. Individuals with more hypnozoites are predicted to experience more relapses and contribute more to onwards P. vivax transmission. Incorporating hypnozoite-killing drugs, such as primaquine, into first-line treatment regimens is predicted to cause substantial reductions in P. vivax transmission, as individuals with the most hypnozoites are more likely to relapse and be targeted for treatment.

    **Implications:** The hypnozoite reservoir is a critical component of P. vivax transmission dynamics, and its inclusion in malaria transmission models can provide valuable insights into the impact of malaria control interventions on the parasite's reservoirs in the vector, blood, and liver. Our results suggest that targeting the hypnozoite reservoir through primaquine treatment can be an effective strategy for reducing P. vivax transmission.

    **Conclusions:** This study demonstrates the importance of considering the hypnozoite reservoir in P. vivax malaria transmission models and highlights the potential benefits of incorporating hypnozoite-killing drugs into malaria control strategies. Our findings have important implications for the development of effective interventions to control and eliminate P. vivax malaria.

**Title:** Predicting Carbon Recovery in Amazonian Forests after Selective Logging: A Regional Analysis

**Research Question:** How do regional differences in climate, soil, and initial biomass influence carbon (C) recovery in Amazonian forests after selective logging?

**Methods:** We employed a novel modeling approach, calibrated on a network of 133 permanent forest plots (175 ha total) across Amazonia, to link regional differences in climate, soil, and initial biomass with survivors' and recruits' C fluxes. We analyzed long-term (8-30 year) inventory data from 13 experimentally-disturbed sites in the Amazon Basin and the Guiana Shield.

**Main Findings:** Our results show that net aboveground C recovery over 10 years is higher in the Guiana Shield and in the west (21 ± 3 Mg C ha-1) than in the south (12 ± 3 Mg C ha-1), where environmental stress is high (low rainfall, high seasonality). We found that survivors play a crucial role in forest regrowth and that the main drivers of C recovery are the growth and recruitment of trees ≥20 cm DBH.

**Implications:** Our study highlights the importance of considering regional differences in climate, soil, and initial biomass when predicting C recovery in Amazonian forests after selective logging. We provide a comprehensive map of post-disturbance C recovery potential in Amazonia, which can inform forest management and conservation efforts.

**Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the regional patterns of C recovery in Amazonian forests after selective logging. By understanding the key drivers of C recovery, we can develop more effective strategies for mitigating the impacts of human activities on C cycles in tropical forests. Our results have important implications for the management of Amazonian forests, which are a critical component of the global C cycle.

    **Title:** Distinguishing Neural Stem Cells from Neurosphere-Initiating Cells in the Adult Mouse Subventricular Zone

    **Research Question:** What is the relationship between neurosphere-initiating cells (NICs) and neural stem cells (NSCs) in the adult mouse subventricular zone (SVZ)?

    **Methods:** We prospectively identified and isolated NICs from the adult mouse SVZ using flow cytometry, and characterized them as GlastmidEGFRhighPlexinB2highCD24−/lowO4/PSA-NCAM−/lowTer119/CD45− (GEPCOT) cells. We also isolated pre-GEPCOT cells, which were quiescent and expressed higher levels of Glast and lower levels of EGFR and PlexinB2.

    **Main Findings:** GEPCOT cells were highly mitotic and short-lived in vivo, whereas pre-GEPCOT cells were quiescent and long-lived. GEPCOT cells were able to form neurospheres and undergo multilineage differentiation, whereas pre-GEPCOT cells were unable to form neurospheres but expressed stem cell markers such as Slc1a3-CreERT, GFAP-CreERT2, Sox2CreERT2, and Gli1CreERT2. Conditional deletion of the Bmi-1 polycomb protein depleted both GEPCOT and pre-GEPCOT cells, although pre-GEPCOT cells were more dependent on Bmi-1 for Cdkn2a (p16Ink4a) repression.

    **Implications:** Our data distinguish quiescent NSCs from NICs and provide a framework for studying their properties in vivo. The identification of GEPCOT cells as NICs highlights their role in neurogenesis, while the characterization of pre-GEPCOT cells as quiescent NSCs underscores their importance in maintaining the SVZ stem cell niche.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the relationship between NICs and NSCs in the adult mouse SVZ, and highlights the importance of distinguishing between these two cell populations. The identification of GEPCOT cells as NICs and pre-GEPCOT cells as quiescent NSCs has significant implications for our understanding of neurogenesis and the development of novel therapeutic strategies for neurological disorders.

    **Title:** Piezo1 Channels Function as Independent Mechanotransducers at Physiologically Relevant Densities

    **Research Question:** Do Piezo1 ion channels interact functionally with each other at different densities, and if so, how does this impact their pressure sensitivity and open probability?

    **Methods:** We employed cell-attached patch-clamp electrophysiology and pressure-clamp stimulation to investigate Piezo1 ion channels in cells expressing Piezo1 endogenously at low levels and cells overexpressing Piezo1 at high levels. Stochastic simulations of Piezo1 spatial distributions were also used to complement the electrophysiological data.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that Piezo1 density has no effect on its pressure sensitivity or open probability, even at non-physiological densities predicted to cause substantial footprint overlap. This suggests that Piezo channels, at densities likely to be physiologically relevant, inherently behave as independent mechanotransducers.

    **Implications:** These findings are crucial for understanding how cells transduce forces homogeneously across the entire cell membrane. The ability of Piezo channels to function independently is essential for cells to detect and respond to mechanical forces in a spatially homogeneous manner.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates that Piezo1 channels, at densities likely to be physiologically relevant, do not exhibit strong intrinsic tendencies to spatially concentrate and functionally modulate each other's mechanical sensitivity. This property enables cells to transduce forces with high spatial homogeneity across the entire cell membrane. These results have significant implications for our understanding of mechanotransduction in cells and highlight the importance of Piezo channels in detecting and responding to mechanical forces in various physiological contexts.

    **Title:** Investigating the Effects of Volatility on Decision-Making: A Bounded Evidence Accumulation Model

    **Research Question:** How does an increase in noise, or volatility, affect decision speed, accuracy, and confidence in motion-selective regions of the visual cortex?

    **Methods:** We employed a novel sensory manipulation to mimic the addition of unbiased noise to motion-selective regions of the visual cortex in macaque areas MT/MST. This manipulation was verified with neuronal recordings from macaque areas MT/MST. We used a reaction-time version of the random-dot motion discrimination task to study the relationship between volatility and decision speed in both humans and monkeys.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that increasing the noise induced faster decisions and greater confidence over a range of stimuli for which accuracy was minimally impaired. The magnitude of the effects was in agreement with predictions of a bounded evidence accumulation model. Specifically, we found that an increase in noise led to faster decisions, reduced accuracy, and paradoxically, higher confidence in both humans and monkeys.

    **Implications:** Our findings provide evidence for the bounded evidence accumulation model, which suggests that decisions arise via the accumulation of noisy evidence to a threshold or bound. This model makes intriguing predictions about the noise itself, including that an increase in noise should lead to faster decisions, reduced accuracy, and higher confidence. Our results support these predictions and have implications for our understanding of the neural mechanisms underlying decision-making.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates that a stimulus manipulation that increases the variance of the momentary evidence bearing on a decision, or volatility, increases both the speed of the decision and the confidence associated with it. These findings have important implications for our understanding of the neural mechanisms underlying decision-making and provide a framework for understanding the joint effect of volatility on choice, reaction time, and confidence. Our results support the bounded evidence accumulation model and highlight the importance of considering the role of noise in decision-making processes.

    **Title:** Regulation of Caenorhabditis elegans Lifespan by the Insulin-like Signaling Pathway and Calcineurin

    **Research Question:** How do the insulin-like signaling pathway and Calcineurin regulate lifespan in Caenorhabditis elegans?

    **Methods:** We investigated the role of the insulin-like signaling pathway and Calcineurin in regulating lifespan in C. elegans using a combination of genetic and biochemical approaches. We used RNA interference (RNAi) to knockdown genes involved in the insulin-like signaling pathway and Calcineurin, and examined the effects on DAF-16, the ortholog of the FOXO transcription factors in mammalian cells.

    **Main Findings:** We found that the insulin-like signaling pathway maintains a relatively short wild-type lifespan in C. elegans by phosphorylating and inactivating DAF-16. Calcineurin (TAX-6•CNB-1) also limits lifespan, but the mechanism was unknown. We discovered that TAX-6•CNB-1 and UNC-43, the C. elegans Calcineurin and Ca2+/calmodulin-dependent kinase type II (CAMKII) orthologs, respectively, regulate lifespan through DAF-16. However, unlike insulin signaling, UNC-43 phosphorylates and activates DAF-16, promoting its nuclear localization. The phosphorylation of DAF-16 at S286 by UNC-43 is removed by TAX-6•CNB-1, leading to DAF-16 inactivation.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that CAMKII (UNC-43) and Calcineurin (TAX-6•CNB-1) regulate C. elegans lifespan through the reversible phosphorylation of DAF-16. The direct, antagonistic nature of these two enzymes raises the possibility that they would constitutively negate each other and have essentially no control over DAF-16. However, our data suggest that insulin signaling dominates over CAMKII/Calcineurin signaling in regulating DAF-16 localization.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the regulation of lifespan in C. elegans and highlights the complex interplay between the insulin-like signaling pathway and Calcineurin in regulating DAF-16. These findings have implications for our understanding of aging and age-related diseases in mammals, where FOXO transcription factors, including FOXO3, are also regulated by CAMKII and Calcineurin.

    **Title:** Hierarchical Suppression of Motor Programs Underlies the Serial Organization of Grooming Behavior in Fruit Flies

    **Research Question:** How do nervous systems implement the serial execution of different movements to form motor sequences?

    **Methods:** We used genetically targeted activation of neural subsets in fruit flies to drive distinct motor programs that clean individual body parts. This enabled competition experiments to reveal the organizational principles governing the grooming sequence.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that the motor programs are organized into a suppression hierarchy, where motor programs that occur first suppress those that occur later. Cleaning one body part reduces the sensory drive to its motor program, which relieves suppression of the next movement, allowing the grooming sequence to progress down the hierarchy.

    **Implications:** This study provides the first example of an innate motor sequence implemented by the prevailing model for generating human action sequences. Our findings suggest that the evolutionarily ancient ability to select among competing behaviors through hierarchical suppression may have provided the necessary infrastructure to perform motor actions in sequence.

    **Conclusions:** We propose a model featuring independently evoked cleaning movements activated in parallel, but selected serially through hierarchical suppression, which successfully reproduces the grooming sequence. Our results demonstrate that hierarchical suppression among many behaviors leads to the emergence of a sequence, and we anticipate that identifying the neural circuitry for each cleaning module and determining how these circuitries interact will reveal how the serial organization of grooming behavior is encoded in the nervous system through a hierarchical suppression mechanism.

    **Significance:** This study contributes to our understanding of the neural control of serial action selection in an innate behavior common to most limbed animals—grooming. Our findings have implications for understanding the neural basis of motor sequence generation and may shed light on the evolution of complex behaviors in animals.

    **Title:** Shugoshin-like protein 2 (Sgol2) coordinates chromosome segregation in mammalian oocytes by regulating cohesion resolution, kinetochore-microtubule interactions, and the spindle assembly checkpoint.

    **Research Question:** How do diverse processes, including cohesion resolution, kinetochore-microtubule interactions, and the spindle assembly checkpoint (SAC), coordinate to ensure accurate chromosome segregation in mammalian oocytes?

    **Methods:** We used a combination of biochemical and cell biological approaches to investigate the role of Shugoshin-like protein 2 (Sgol2) in regulating chromosome segregation in mammalian oocytes.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that Sgol2 plays a crucial role in turning off the SAC, promoting the congression and bi-orientation of bivalents on meiosis I spindles, facilitating the formation of kinetochore-microtubule attachments (K-fibers), and limiting bivalent stretching. Sgol2's ability to protect cohesin and silence the SAC depends on its interaction with protein phosphatase 2A (PP2A). In contrast, its effect on bivalent stretching and K-fiber formation is independent of PP2A and mediated by the recruitment of MCAK and inhibition of Aurora C kinase activity, respectively.

    **Implications:** Our findings demonstrate that Sgol2 is a key regulator of chromosome segregation in mammalian oocytes, linking multiple processes essential for faithful chromosome segregation. The pleiotropic consequences of Sgol2 depletion, including inefficient SAC silencing, increased bivalent stretching, and altered kinetochore-microtubule attachments, highlight the importance of Sgol2 in ensuring accurate chromosome segregation.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals that Sgol2 is a multifunctional protein that regulates sister chromatid cohesion, chromosome traction, the spindle assembly checkpoint, and Aurora B/C kinases. By demonstrating that different point mutations alter the regulation of some but not other processes, we show that the pleiotropy observed in Sgol2-deficient oocytes is not due to secondary or tertiary pathology. Our findings provide new insights into the mechanisms of chromosome segregation in mammalian oocytes and highlight the importance of Sgol2 as a key regulator of this complex process.

    **Title:** Experience-Dependent Changes in Brain Connectivity Require RNA Localization and Protein Synthesis: A Role for Netrin-1/DCC Signaling

    **Research Question:** Do local stimulation and synaptic activity regulate the trafficking of RNAs from the nucleus to specific subcellular sites, and what is the role of netrin-1/DCC signaling in this process?

    **Methods:** This study employed a combination of imaging and biochemical techniques to investigate RNA localization and translation in cultured Aplysia sensory-motor neurons. Imaging of RNA localization and translation revealed that RNAs were delivered throughout the arbor of the sensory neuron, but translation was enriched only at sites of synaptic contact and/or synaptic stimulation. Investigation of the mechanisms that trigger local translation revealed a role for calcium-dependent retrograde netrin-1/DCC receptor signaling.

    **Main Findings:** Our results indicate that spatially restricting gene expression by regulating local translation rather than by directing the delivery of mRNAs from nucleus to stimulated synapses maximizes the readiness of the entire neuronal arbor to respond to local cues. We found that rRNA, three specific mRNAs, ribosomal proteins, and translation factors were delivered throughout the sensory neuron, but translation was significantly enriched at sites of synaptic contact and at sites of synaptic stimulation. Netrin-1/DCC signaling was identified as a key regulator of local translation at synapses and/or stimulated synapses.

    **Implications:** These findings suggest that the spatial regulation of gene expression in Aplysia sensory-motor neurons is mediated primarily at the level of translation rather than at the level of RNA targeting from the nucleus. The release of netrin-1 from either the sensory neuron (SN) or motor neuron (MN) during synapse formation and synaptic plasticity triggers translation at sites of synaptic but not non-synaptic contacts.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates that local stimulation and synaptic activity regulate the trafficking of RNAs from the nucleus to specific subcellular sites, and that netrin-1/DCC signaling plays a critical role in this process. These findings have important implications for our understanding of experience-dependent changes in brain connectivity and synaptic plasticity, and highlight the importance of local translation in regulating gene expression in neurons.

    **Title:** Mosaic Knockdown of Molecular Regulators Reveals Cell-Driven Self-Organization in Human Induced Pluripotent Stem Cell Colonies

    **Research Question:** How do cell-cell interactions and multicellular organization emerge in human induced pluripotent stem cell (hiPSC) colonies, and can we genetically control the emergence of asymmetric cell phenotypes and development of multicellular patterns?

    **Methods:** We employed an inducible CRISPR interference (CRISPRi) system in hiPSCs to silence key proteins that regulate cell adhesion (CDH1) and cortical tension (ROCK1). We created mixed populations of hiPSCs with and without the CRISPRi system and induced mosaic knockdown (KD) to interrogate how the creation of physical asymmetries in an otherwise homogeneous population leads to multicellular organization and pattern formation.

    **Main Findings:** Mosaic KD of ROCK1 or CDH1 resulted in differential patterning within hiPSC colonies due to cellular self-organization, while retaining an epithelial pluripotent phenotype. Knockdown induction stimulated a transient wave of differential gene expression within the mixed populations that stabilized in coordination with observed self-organization.

    **Implications:** Our study reveals a novel strategy to direct the emergence of heterotypic cell populations to control multicellular organization in pluripotent stem cells. This approach enables genetic interrogation of emergent multicellular properties, which can facilitate better understanding of the molecular pathways that regulate symmetry-breaking during morphogenesis.

    **Conclusions:** Our results demonstrate that mechanical manipulation of hiPSC sub-populations can result in controllable cell-driven self-organization into repeatable patterns. By silencing target genes by CRISPRi within only subpopulations of cells, we provide multiple avenues to genetically control the emergence of asymmetric cell phenotypes and development of multicellular patterns. Ultimately, this study identifies a novel strategy to direct the emergence of heterotypic cell populations to control multicellular organization in pluripotent stem cells, and subsequently facilitates the creation of robust models of morphogenesis necessary for the mechanistic study of human developmental tissue patterning and formation.

    **Title:** A Normative Theory for Set Size Effects in Visual Working Memory

    **Research Question:** What is the underlying mechanism driving the decrease in encoding precision in visual working memory as the number of encoded items increases?

    **Methods:** We propose a normative theory that posits the brain minimizes a weighted sum of an error-based behavioral cost and a neural encoding cost. We construct a model based on this theory and test its predictions using data from nine delayed-estimation experiments.

    **Main Findings:** Our model accounts well for the effects of both set size and probing probability on encoding precision, aligning with previous empirical findings. Notably, we find that the total amount of invested resource can vary non-monotonically with set size, suggesting that it is sometimes optimal to encode only a subset or even none of the relevant items in a task.

    **Implications:** Our findings raise the possibility that cognitive "limitations" arise from rational cost minimization rather than from constraints. This theory may also apply beyond working memory tasks, providing a normative formalism to explain set size effects in attention tasks. Furthermore, our model predicts a non-monotonic relation between population-level spiking activity and set size, which can be tested using neuroimaging experiments.

    **Conclusions:** Our normative theory provides a principled explanation for the decrease in encoding precision with increasing set size in visual working memory. The model's ability to account for both set size and probing probability effects, as well as the non-monotonic relation between set size and resource investment, supports its validity. These findings have implications for our understanding of cognitive limitations and the neural mechanisms underlying working memory and attention. Future studies can test the model's predictions and explore its applicability to other cognitive tasks.

    **Title:** A Plant Pathogen Effector Exploits Host Autophagy to Promote Disease Susceptibility

    **Research Question:** How do plant pathogens interfere with autophagy-related processes to counteract host defenses?

    **Methods:** Using the Irish potato famine pathogen Phytophthora infestans as a model, we investigated the interaction between the pathogen effector PexRD54 and the host autophagy protein ATG8CL.

    **Main Findings:** We show that PexRD54 binds to ATG8CL with high affinity and stimulates the formation of ATG8CL-marked autophagosomes. Furthermore, PexRD54 depletes the autophagy cargo receptor Joka2 out of ATG8CL complexes, thereby interfering with Joka2's positive effect on pathogen defense.

    **Implications:** Our results reveal a novel mechanism by which a plant pathogen effector has evolved to antagonize a host autophagy cargo receptor to counteract host defenses. This finding highlights the complex interplay between plant pathogens and host autophagy pathways.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates that, in addition to disrupting autophagy, pathogens can also activate autophagy for their own benefit. Specifically, PexRD54 competes with the host cargo receptor Joka2 and depletes it out of ATG8CL autophagosomes to promote disease susceptibility. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying plant-pathogen interactions and highlight the importance of autophagy in plant defense against pathogens.

    **Future Directions:** Future studies will need to consider the spatio-temporal dynamics of autophagy and subcellular trafficking during host infection. Additionally, it will be interesting to determine whether other plant pathogens also secrete effectors that evolved an ATG8 interacting motif or target autophagy in other ways. These effectors would serve as valuable tools to dissect the mechanisms of defense-related selective autophagy.

    **Keywords:** autophagy, plant-pathogen interaction, PexRD54, ATG8CL, Joka2, Phytophthora infestans.

    **Title:** Elucidating Cardiac Fibroblast Responses to Simultaneous Environmental Stimuli: A Novel In Vitro Model of Cardiac Injury

    **Research Question:** How do cardiac fibroblasts (CFs) respond to a combination of mechanical strain and oxygen tension changes, mimicking early environmental changes in cardiac injury?

    **Methods:** We employed a multi-stimulus microdevice to apply cyclic mechanical strain and controlled oxygen tension to human primary CFs. We examined the effects of individual and combined stimuli on CF behavior, including proliferation, collagen production, and cytokine release.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that individual stimuli, such as mechanical strain or hypoxia, induce distinct CF responses, including proliferation, YAP translocation, and PDGF secretion. However, only the combination of hypoxia and reduced contractility (2% strain) triggers the release of inflammatory and pro-fibrotic cytokines and matrix metalloproteinases. This synergistic effect was not observed with individual stimuli.

    **Implications:** Our findings provide new insights into the complex interactions between environmental stimuli and CF responses in cardiac injury. The combination of hypoxia and reduced contractility may play a crucial role in triggering pathological responses in CFs, leading to fibrotic remodeling. This understanding has significant implications for the development of in vitro models of cardiac disease and the design of reparative strategies.

    **Conclusions:** Our study presents a novel in vitro model that recreates a pathological environment for understanding CF responses during cardiac injury. By finely controlling mechanical strain and oxygen regimes, we have elucidated the individual and synergistic contributions of environmental signals in the activation of early CF responses relevant to cardiac fibrotic disease. This model provides a valuable tool for future studies of pathological myocardial remodeling and the development of therapeutic interventions. Our findings highlight the importance of considering the complex interactions between environmental stimuli and CF responses in the context of cardiac injury, and suggest that the combination of hypoxia and reduced contractility may be a critical trigger for pathological responses in CFs.

    **Title:** Alternative Isoforms of the DOMINO Nucleosome Remodeling ATPase Specify Distinct Chromatin Regulators in Drosophila

    **Research Question:** How do the alternative isoforms of the DOMINO nucleosome remodeling ATPase (DOM-A and DOM-B) contribute to transcriptional regulation in Drosophila, and what are the mechanisms underlying their distinct functions?

    **Methods:** We employed a combination of biochemical and genetic approaches to investigate the functions of DOM-A and DOM-B in Drosophila cells. We used RNA sequencing (RNAseq) to analyze the effects of knockdowns of DOM-A, DOM-B, TIP60, and H2A.V on transcription. We also performed biochemical assays to examine the ATPase activity of DOM-A and its ability to acetylate histone H4.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that DOM-A and DOM-B form distinct multi-subunit complexes that are necessary for transcriptional regulation. The DOM-B complex incorporates H2A.V into chromatin in an ATP-dependent manner, similar to the yeast SWR1 complex. In contrast, the DOM-A complex functions as an ATP-independent histone acetyltransferase complex, targeting lysine 12 of histone H4, similar to the yeast NuA4 complex. Our data also suggest that the ATPase activity of DOM-A is dispensable for H4K12 acetylation.

    **Implications:** Our findings provide new insights into the mechanisms of chromatin regulation in Drosophila and highlight the importance of alternative splicing in generating functional diversity amongst chromatin regulators. The distinct functions of DOM-A and DOM-B illustrate how different evolutionary strategies can lead to similar functional separation, as seen in yeast and humans, where nucleosome remodeling and histone acetyltransferase complexes originate from gene duplication and paralog specification.

    **Conclusions:** Our work demonstrates that the alternative isoforms of the DOMINO nucleosome remodeling ATPase specify distinct chromatin regulators in Drosophila, with DOM-A functioning as an ATP-independent histone acetyltransferase complex and DOM-B incorporating H2A.V into chromatin in an ATP-dependent manner. These findings have implications for our understanding of chromatin regulation and highlight the importance of alternative splicing in generating functional diversity amongst chromatin regulators.

    **Title:** Muscle β-Catenin Regulates Presynaptic Differentiation via Transcriptional Regulation of Slit2

    **Research Question:** What functions of muscle β-Catenin (Ctnnb1) are critical for neuromuscular junction (NMJ) formation, and how does it regulate presynaptic differentiation?

    **Methods:** We employed an in vivo transgenic approach to dissect the functions of muscle Ctnnb1 in NMJ formation. We generated transgenic mice expressing wild-type or Ctnnb1 mutants that were impaired in transcriptional regulation or cell-adhesion signaling.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that the transcriptional activity of muscle Ctnnb1 is essential for presynaptic differentiation, as a Ctnnb1 mutant lacking the transactivation domain (TAD) was unable to rescue presynaptic deficits caused by Ctnnb1 mutation. In contrast, the cell-adhesion function of Ctnnb1 is dispensable. We identified Slit2 as a potential retrograde factor that mediates the effects of muscle Ctnnb1 on presynaptic differentiation. Transgenic expression of Slit2 in muscle cells was able to diminish presynaptic deficits caused by Ctnnb1 mutation, including poor innervation, reduced vesicle density, and depressed miniature endplate potentials (mEPPs).

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that muscle Ctnnb1 regulates presynaptic differentiation via transcriptional regulation of Slit2, a protein that repels or collapses neuronal axons. This retrograde pathway is critical for NMJ formation and maintenance. The identification of Slit2 as a key mediator of muscle Ctnnb1's effects on presynaptic differentiation provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying NMJ formation.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates that the transcriptional activity of muscle Ctnnb1 is essential for presynaptic differentiation, and that Slit2 is a critical downstream effector of muscle Ctnnb1 in this process. These findings have important implications for our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying NMJ formation and maintenance, and may provide new targets for therapeutic interventions in neuromuscular disorders.

    **Title:** Force Transduction at Cell-Cell Adhesions Regulates Tissue Development, Maintenance, and Adaptation

    **Research Question:** How do mechanical forces transmitted through cell-cell adhesions regulate tissue development, maintenance, and adaptation in dynamic epithelial tissues?

    **Methods:** We developed a novel computational and experimental approach to quantify force transmission in cell clusters with sub-cellular and multi-cellular resolution. We applied this technology to spontaneously-forming adherent epithelial cell clusters and analyzed the dynamics of force transmission at individual cell-cell junctions and the multi-cellular scale.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that basal force fluctuations are coupled to E-cadherin localization at the level of individual cell-cell junctions. At the multi-cellular scale, cell-cell force exchange depends on the cell position within a cluster and is adaptive to reconfigurations due to cell divisions or positional rearrangements. Importantly, force transmission through a cell requires coordinated modulation of cell-matrix adhesion and actomyosin contractility in the cell and its neighbors.

    **Implications:** These findings provide insights into the mechanisms that control mechanical stress homeostasis in dynamic epithelial tissues. Our results highlight the importance of force transmission through cell-cell adhesions in regulating tissue development, maintenance, and adaptation.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates that force transduction at cell-cell adhesions plays a crucial role in regulating tissue mechanics. The development of our computational and experimental approach provides a valuable resource for the study of mechanotransduction in cell-cell adhesions. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of tissue development, maintenance, and adaptation, and may contribute to the development of novel therapeutic strategies for tissue-related diseases.

    **Key Takeaways:**

    * Force transmission through cell-cell adhesions is essential for tissue development, maintenance, and adaptation.
    * Basal force fluctuations are coupled to E-cadherin localization at individual cell-cell junctions.
    * Cell-cell force exchange depends on cell position within a cluster and is adaptive to reconfigurations.
    * Coordinated modulation of cell-matrix adhesion and actomyosin contractility is required for force transmission through a cell.

    **Title:** NCOA4-mediated Ferritinophagy: Unveiling the Molecular Mechanisms of Iron-Dependent Regulation

    **Research Question:** How does NCOA4, a selective cargo receptor, regulate the autophagic turnover of ferritin in response to changes in intracellular iron levels?

    **Methods:** We employed a combination of biochemical, cell biological, and genetic approaches to investigate the interaction between NCOA4 and ferritin (FTH1), as well as the role of the ubiquitin-proteasome system in regulating NCOA4 abundance. We used zebrafish and cultured cells to study the physiological relevance of NCOA4-mediated ferritinophagy.

    **Main Findings:** We demonstrate that NCOA4 directly interacts with FTH1 via a conserved C-terminal domain and a key arginine residue on FTH1.     **Implications:** Our findings reveal the molecular mechanisms underlying NCOA4-mediated ferritinophagy and explain how intracellular iron levels modulate NCOA4 activity. We show that NCOA4 plays an essential role in erythroid differentiation in zebrafish and cultured cells, highlighting the importance of NCOA4-mediated ferritinophagy in maintaining cellular iron homeostasis.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the regulation of ferritinophagy and the role of NCOA4 in iron-dependent processes. The identification of the NCOA4-FTH1 interaction and the involvement of the ubiquitin-proteasome system in regulating NCOA4 abundance sheds light on the molecular mechanisms underlying ferritinophagy. These findings have important implications for our understanding of iron metabolism and the development of therapeutic strategies for iron-related disorders.

    Title: Hierarchical Organization of the Lateral Prefrontal Cortex: Unveiling the Apex of Higher-Level Cognition

    Research Question: How is the lateral prefrontal cortex (LPFC) functionally organized to support higher-level cognition?

    Methods: We employed functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) and dynamic causal modeling to investigate the hierarchical organization of the LPFC in humans. We examined the rostral/caudal axis of the LPFC and modeled interactions among LPFC sub-regions to determine whether and how cognitive control demands elicit asymmetrical influences indicative of hierarchical processing.

    Main Findings: Our results revealed rostral/caudal gradients of abstraction in the LPFC, with progressively rostral areas processing increasingly abstract levels of cognitive control. Dynamic causal modeling revealed asymmetrical LPFC interactions indicative of hierarchical processing, with the mid LPFC positioned as the apex of the hierarchy. We found that cognitive demands induced connectivity modulations towards mid LPFC, consistent with a role in integrating information for control operations. Furthermore, the strengths of these dynamics were related to trait-measured higher-level cognitive ability.

    Implications: Our findings suggest that the LPFC is hierarchically organized, with the mid LPFC positioned to synthesize abstract and concrete information to control behavior. The relative strength of efferent versus afferent connections positioned mid LPFC as the apex of the hierarchy, contrary to dominant assumptions. Our results also indicate that cognitive demands elicit top-down influences from rostral to mid LPFC and bottom-up influences from caudal to mid LPFC, suggesting that mid LPFC forms a nexus where information converges to influence action.

    Conclusions: Our study provides evidence for a hierarchical organization of the LPFC, with the mid LPFC playing a critical role in integrating information for control operations. The strengths of these dynamics were related to higher-level cognitive ability, suggesting that the functional organization of the LPFC is intimately tied to cognitive capacity. Our findings have implications for understanding the neural basis of higher-level cognition and may inform the development of novel therapeutic interventions for cognitive disorders.

    Abstract:
    The evolution of higher intelligence in hominoids is often linked to cortical expansion and folding. However, the molecular and cellular mechanisms underlying cortical folding remain poorly understood. Here, we investigate the role of the hominoid-specific gene TBC1D3 in regulating cortical development. We found that expression of TBC1D3 in ventricular cortical progenitors of mice via in utero electroporation caused delamination of ventricular radial glia cells (vRGs) and promoted the generation of self-renewing basal progenitors with the morphology of outer radial glia (oRGs), which are abundant in primates. Furthermore, down-regulation of TBC1D3 in cultured human brain slices decreased the generation of oRGs. Notably, localized oRG proliferation resulting from either in utero electroporation or transgenic expression of TBC1D3 was often found to underlie cortical regions exhibiting folding. Our findings suggest that TBC1D3 is a hominoid gene required for oRG generation and regulation of cortical expansion and folding.

    Methods:
    We used in utero electroporation to express TBC1D3 in ventricular cortical progenitors of mice at embryonic day 13.5 (E13.5). We also examined the effect of TBC1D3 expression on the intrinsic stemness signaling pathways in basal progenitors (BPs) using flow cytometry-sorted cells at E15.

    Main Findings:
    We found that TBC1D3 expression in vRGs caused delamination and promoted the generation of self-renewing BPs with the morphology of oRGs. Down-regulation of TBC1D3 in cultured human brain slices decreased the generation of oRGs. Localized oRG proliferation resulting from either in utero electroporation or transgenic expression of TBC1D3 was often found to underlie cortical regions exhibiting folding.

    Implications:
    Our findings suggest that TBC1D3 is a hominoid gene required for oRG generation and regulation of cortical expansion and folding. This study provides proof-of-concept that brain development can be regulated by duplicated genes during hominoid evolution.

    Conclusions:
    We have identified TBC1D3 as a hominoid-specific gene that regulates cortical expansion and folding through the generation of oRGs. Our findings provide new insights into the molecular and cellular mechanisms underlying cortical folding and suggest that TBC1D3 is a candidate gene that controls many other genes involved in cortex expansion and folding.

    **Title:** Stepwise Ubiquitination of NPR1 Regulates Immune-Induced Transcriptome Reprogramming in Plants

    **Research Question:** Does ubiquitination of the immune coactivator NPR1 play a transcriptional role prior to its proteasomal degradation, and if so, what are the underlying mechanisms?

    **Methods:** We investigated the role of ubiquitination in NPR1-mediated immune responses in Arabidopsis thaliana using a combination of biochemical, genetic, and molecular biology approaches.

    **Main Findings:** Our study reveals that ubiquitination of NPR1 is a stepwise process, initiated by the Cullin-RING E3 ligase CRL3, which promotes chromatin association and expression of target genes. The subsequent extension of ubiquitin chains by the E4 ligase UBE4 leads to proteasomal degradation of NPR1. However, we also identified two proteasome-associated deubiquitinases, UBP6/7, which counteract UBE4 activity and enhance NPR1 longevity.

    **Implications:** Our findings indicate that the sequential actions of E3 and E4 ligases, balanced by opposing deubiquitinases, fine-tune the activity of NPR1 without requiring its sacrificial turnover. This suggests that ubiquitination can act as a transcriptional signal, allowing NPR1 to activate its target genes prior to degradation.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the mechanisms underlying immune-induced transcriptome reprogramming in plants. We propose that the initial ubiquitination of NPR1 by CRL3 enhances its transcriptional coactivator activity, while the subsequent extension of ubiquitin chains by UBE4 inactivates NPR1 and promotes its degradation. The opposing activities of UBP6/7 deubiquitinases further fine-tune NPR1 activity, allowing for a dynamic regulation of immune responses. These findings have important implications for our understanding of the complex interplay between ubiquitination, proteasomal degradation, and transcriptional regulation in plants.

    **Title:** Elucidating the Biogenesis of 24 Nucleotide Small Interfering RNAs in Arabidopsis thaliana

    **Research Question:** What are the molecular mechanisms underlying the biogenesis of 24 nucleotide small interfering RNAs (24 nt siRNAs) in Arabidopsis thaliana, and what are the roles of nuclear RNA polymerase IV (Pol IV), RNA-dependent RNA polymerase 2 (RDR2), and DICER-like 3 (DCL3) in this process?

    **Methods:** We employed a combination of genetic, biochemical, and bioinformatic approaches to investigate the biogenesis of 24 nt siRNAs in Arabidopsis thaliana. We analyzed the accumulation of Pol IV and RDR2-dependent RNAs (P4R2 RNAs) in dcl3 mutants and demonstrated that these RNAs are diced into 24 nt siRNAs by DCL3 in vitro.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that P4R2 RNAs are mostly 26-45 nt in length and initiate with a purine adjacent to a pyrimidine, characteristics shared by Pol IV transcripts generated in vitro. RDR2 terminal transferase activity may account for occasional non-templated nucleotides at P4R2 RNA 3’ termini. We also found that the 24 nt siRNAs primarily correspond to the 5’ or 3’ ends of P4R2 RNAs, suggesting a model whereby siRNAs are generated from either end of P4R2 duplexes by single dicing events.

    **Implications:** These findings provide new insights into the biogenesis of 24 nt siRNAs in Arabidopsis thaliana and highlight the essential roles of Pol IV, RDR2, and DCL3 in this process. Our results also suggest that P4R2 RNAs are the immediate precursors of siRNAs, which are diced into 24 nt siRNAs by DCL3.

    **Conclusions:** In summary, our study elucidates the molecular mechanisms underlying the biogenesis of 24 nt siRNAs in Arabidopsis thaliana and provides new insights into the roles of Pol IV, RDR2, and DCL3 in this process. These findings have important implications for our understanding of the regulation of gene expression and the maintenance of genome stability in plants.

**Title:** Emergence of Mature Cortical Network Dynamics: A Discrete Transition Period in Early Postnatal Development

**Research Question:** How do immature neural networks develop into their mature form, enabling complex computations underlying cognition, during early postnatal development?

**Methods:** We employed high spatiotemporal resolution in vivo electrophysiology in immature mice to investigate the progression of large-scale synaptic and cellular activity patterns across development.

**Main Findings:** Our study reveals that mature cortical processes emerge rapidly and simultaneously after a discrete but volatile transition period at the beginning of the second postnatal week of rodent development. This transition is characterized by relative neural quiescence, followed by the emergence of spatially distributed, temporally precise, and internally organized activity. We demonstrate a similar developmental trajectory in humans, suggesting an evolutionarily conserved mechanism that facilitates a transition in network operation.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that a transient quiescent period is a requisite for the subsequent emergence of coordinated cortical networks. This period of developmental perturbation may be an evolutionarily conserved indicator of a substantial shift in cortical network operations.

**Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the postnatal time course for neural networks to perform complex computations. We propose that the emergence of mature cortical network dynamics is preceded by a discrete, relatively quiescent, evolutionarily conserved transition period during early postnatal development. This period is characterized by a nadir in oscillations and neural spiking, signaling the shift from local, loosely correlated, putatively sensory-driven patterns to internally organized, spatially distributed, and temporally precise activity. Our findings have implications for understanding the development of cognitive functions and may inform the development of novel therapeutic strategies for neurological disorders.

    **Title:** Modeling the Coexistence of Multiple Strains in Plasmodium falciparum Populations: The Role of Immune Selection in Structuring Parasite Diversity

    **Research Question:** How does immune selection influence the coexistence of multiple strains in Plasmodium falciparum populations that undergo sexual recombination, and what are the implications for malaria control?

    **Methods:** We developed an agent-based model that extends earlier ‘strain theory’ to address the var gene family of Plasmodium falciparum, which encodes the major surface antigen PfEMP1. The model tracks the dynamics of all unique var repertoires in a population of hosts, considering the extensive diversity of multi-copy genes that undergo antigenic variation via sequential, mutually exclusive expression.

    **Main Findings:** Our model shows that even under high levels of sexual recombination, strain competition mediated through cross-immunity structures the parasite population into a subset of coexisting dominant repertoires of var genes. The degree of antigenic overlap between these repertoires depends on transmission intensity. Empirical comparison of patterns of genetic variation at antigenic and neutral sites supports the role of immune selection in structuring parasite diversity.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that immune selection plays a crucial role in shaping the diversity of Plasmodium falciparum populations, even in the presence of high levels of sexual recombination. This has significant implications for malaria control, as it implies that vector control strategies that reduce transmission may not always drastically reduce var gene diversity. A reservoir of parasite genomes with diverse, highly non-overlapping var repertoires will remain, potentially limiting the effectiveness of control measures.

    **Conclusions:** Our agent-based model provides new insights into the coexistence of multiple strains in Plasmodium falciparum populations and highlights the importance of immune selection in structuring parasite diversity. These findings have important implications for the development of effective malaria control strategies, emphasizing the need to consider the complex interactions between parasite populations and the host immune system.

    **Title:** Deamidation of UBC13 by Shigella flexneri Effector OspI Disrupts Polyubiquitination Activity and Silences NF-κB Inflammatory Response

    **Research Question:** How does the deamidase OspI from Shigella flexneri inhibit the polyubiquitination activity of the UBC13/TRAF6 complex, leading to silencing of the NF-κB inflammatory response?

    **Methods:** We employed biochemical and biophysical approaches to investigate the effects of OspI-mediated deamidation of UBC13 on its interaction with TRAF6 RING-domain (TRAF6RING) and subsequent polyubiquitination activity.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that OspI deamidates a glutamine residue (Q100) in UBC13, converting it to glutamate (E100), which disrupts the interaction between UBC13 and TRAF6RING.     **Implications:** These findings highlight a novel mechanism by which a post-translational modification (deamidation) can perturb the conformation and stability of transient protein complexes, leading to inhibition of protein-protein association. This mechanism may have broader implications for understanding how pathogens manipulate host cell signaling pathways to evade immune responses.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals that OspI-mediated deamidation of UBC13 is a key mechanism by which Shigella flexneri suppresses the NF-κB inflammatory response. By disrupting the UBC13/TRAF6 complex, OspI prevents the synthesis of K63-linked polyubiquitin chains, which are essential for NF-κB activation. These findings provide new insights into the molecular basis of Shigella flexneri pathogenesis and highlight the importance of post-translational modifications in regulating protein-protein interactions.

**Title:** Bidirectional Manipulation of Cortical Interneurons Reveals Asymmetry in Computational Functions

**Research Question:** Do bidirectional manipulations (activation and inactivation) of specific cortical interneuron types provide internally consistent insights into their computational roles?

**Methods:** We used single-unit recordings in the auditory cortex of awake mice to investigate the effects of optogenetic manipulation of somatostatin-positive (Sst+) and parvalbumin-positive (Pvalb+) interneurons on tone-responses.

**Main Findings:** Our results show that inactivation of Sst+ interneurons increased response gain, while inactivation of Pvalb+ interneurons weakened tuning and decreased information transfer. In contrast, activating Sst+ and Pvalb+ interneurons revealed no such differences. We used a simple network model to understand this asymmetry and found that small changes in key parameters, such as spontaneous activity or strength of the light manipulation, determined whether activation and inactivation would produce consistent or paradoxical conclusions regarding interneurons' computational functions.

**Implications:** Our findings challenge the assumption that bidirectional manipulations provide a straightforward way to identify the functions supported by specific cortical interneuron types. Instead, our results suggest that the effects of activation and inactivation can be context-dependent and influenced by various factors, such as the type of interneuron and the experimental conditions.

**Conclusions:** Our study highlights the importance of considering the complexities of interneuron function and the need for a more nuanced approach to understanding their computational roles. By using a combination of experimental and modeling approaches, we can gain a deeper understanding of the mechanisms underlying interneuron function and develop more effective strategies for manipulating and interpreting the results of bidirectional manipulations. Our findings have implications for the broader goal of establishing the ways in which we can use causal strategies to gain insight into interneuron function.

**Title:** Neural Basis of Compensatory Mechanisms in Congenital Amusia: A Functional Connectivity Study

**Conclusions:** Our study provides evidence for a neural basis of compensatory mechanisms in congenital amusia. The reduced functional connectivity between left prefrontal language-related regions and right hemisphere pitch-related regions in individuals with amusia suggests that they rely more heavily on other dimensions, such as duration, to process spoken language. These findings have implications for our understanding of the neural basis of speech perception and the development of compensatory strategies in individuals with congenital amusia.     **Methods:** We employed optogenetic circuit mapping in mice to investigate the neural codes underlying spatial and non-spatial behaviors in the RSG.

The anterior thalamus and dorsal subiculum, sources of spatial information, selectively recruit LR neurons, while claustral and anterior cingulate inputs, sources of mostly non-spatial information, preferentially control RS cells. Precise sublaminar axonal and dendritic arborization within RSG layer 1 enables this parallel processing.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that the distinct morphology and intrinsic properties of LR neurons make them ideally suited to integrate head direction input from the thalamus and spatial inputs from the hippocampal formation via the dorsal subiculum. The observed thalamocortical synaptic dynamics enable computational models of LR neurons to compute the speed of head rotation, despite receiving head direction inputs that do not explicitly encode speed.

    **Conclusions:** Our study highlights a superficial retrosplenial circuit enabled by the precise sublaminar organization of distinct principal neuronal subtypes and axonal afferents. The parallel input streams identify a distinct principal neuronal subtype, LR neurons, ideally positioned to support spatial orientation computations in the RSG. Future studies should investigate how these two parallel streams of information are integrated in downstream neurons within both the ipsilateral and contralateral granular and dysgranular retrosplenial cortices to gain a mechanistic understanding of retrosplenial computations.

    **Title:** Seizure Activity Promotes Prion-like Spreading of Tauopathy after Traumatic Brain Injury

    **Background:** Traumatic brain injury (TBI) is a significant risk factor for dementias, including tauopathies such as chronic traumatic encephalopathy (CTE). The mechanisms underlying the prion-like spreading of Tau aggregates after TBI are not fully understood, partly due to the lack of tractable animal models.

    **Research Question:** Do seizures play a role in promoting the spread of tauopathy after TBI?

    **Methods:** We employed a novel zebrafish model expressing a genetically encoded fluorescent Tau biosensor that reports accumulation of human Tau species when seeded via intraventricular brain injections. Zebrafish larvae were subjected to a novel TBI paradigm, which produced various TBI features, including cell death, post-traumatic seizures, and Tau inclusions.

    **Main Findings:** Bath application of dynamin inhibitors or anticonvulsant drugs rescued TBI-induced tauopathy and cell death, suggesting a role for seizure activity in the prion-like seeding and spreading of tauopathy following TBI. Our data reveal a substantial burst of neural activity occurs during TBI, and zebrafish larvae exposed to TBI exhibit a significantly higher propensity for spontaneous seizures.

    **Implications:** These findings suggest that post-traumatic seizures are a druggable mechanistic link between TBI and the prion-like progression of tauopathy. Manipulating seizure activity mitigated Tau aggregation, revealing a critical role for endocytosis in the prion-like spread of Tau seeds in vivo following TBI.

    **Conclusions:** Our novel in vivo TBI model implicates seizures and dynamin-dependent endocytosis in the spread of Tau seeds, offering potential therapeutic targets. These findings highlight the utility of zebrafish as a tractable model for studying TBI and tauopathy, and suggest that anti-convulsants that dampen post-traumatic seizures may be a route to moderating subsequent tauopathy. Further work is warranted to explore the therapeutic potential of these findings.

    Methods: This study employed resting-state functional magnetic resonance imaging (rfMRI) to investigate the relationship between the spatial configuration of functional brain regions and measures of brain connectivity.     **Title:** Revisiting the Role of TBP2 in Skeletal Muscle Gene Expression and Regeneration

    **Research Question:** Does TBP2 replace TBP in the transcription pre-initiation complex to control cell type-specific gene expression in skeletal muscle cells?

    **Methods:** We investigated the role of TBP2 in skeletal muscle gene expression and regeneration using TBP2 null mice, C2C12 myoblasts, and primary mouse muscle stem cells (MuSCs).     **Main Findings:** Our results show that TBP2 is not expressed in myotubes derived from C2C12 myoblasts or primary mouse MuSCs, and that TBP2-deficient mice have an intact regeneration potential after injury.     **Conclusions:** In summary, our study demonstrates that TBP, but not TBP2, is the essential component of the PIC that promotes muscle gene expression in differentiated skeletal muscles. Our findings have important implications for understanding the regulation of skeletal muscle gene expression and regeneration, and highlight the need for further investigation into the role of TFIID-TBP in muscle cell differentiation.

    **Title:** Stimulation-Induced Neuroplasticity in Large-Scale Cortical Networks

    **Research Question:** How does brain stimulation modulate the excitability of neural circuits and drive neuroplasticity in large-scale networks?

    **Methods:** We employed a large-scale optogenetic interface to simultaneously stimulate excitatory neurons and record electrocorticographic activity across primary somatosensory (S1) and motor (M1) cortex in two macaques. We tracked two measures of network connectivity: the network response to focal stimulation and baseline coherence between pairs of electrodes.

    **Main Findings:** Within minutes of stimulation in S1 or M1, we observed significant strengthening of gross functional connectivity between these areas. At a finer scale, stimulation led to heterogeneous connectivity changes across the network, reflecting correlations introduced by stimulation-evoked activity consistent with Hebbian plasticity models.

    **Implications:** Our findings demonstrate a robust relationship between stimulus-evoked correlations and changes in baseline connectivity across the network, supporting a Hebbian model of large-scale stimulation-induced plasticity. This work extends Hebbian plasticity models to large-scale circuits, with significant implications for stimulation-based neurorehabilitation strategies.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides evidence that brain stimulation can modulate the excitability of neural circuits and drive neuroplasticity in large-scale networks. The observed changes in network connectivity are consistent with Hebbian plasticity models, suggesting that stimulation-evoked activity drives large-scale network plasticity in a Hebbian manner. These findings have important implications for the development of novel neurorehabilitation strategies that leverage stimulation-based approaches to promote functional reorganization in the brain. Future studies should investigate intermediate time-frames and detailed cortical mapping to further elucidate the relationship between stimulation-induced plasticity and Hebbian learning.

    **Title:** Neural Consequences of Primary Visual Cortex Lesion in a Rhesus Macaque: Implications for Residual Visual Function

    **Research Question:** What are the neural consequences of a primary visual cortex (V1) lesion in a Rhesus macaque, and how do these consequences impact residual visual function?

    **Methods:** A rare case study of a Rhesus macaque (monkey S) born without V1 was conducted using multi-modal magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) and functional connectivity analysis. Visual stimulation under anesthesia with checkerboards and full-field moving dots was used to activate visual structures outside of the lesion.

    **Main Findings:** Despite the absence of V1, monkey S's in-group behavior was unremarkable, but visual task training was impaired. Visual cortical activation was sparse, but included face patches. Functional connectivity analysis revealed an intact network of bilateral dorsal visual areas temporally correlated with V5/MT activation, even without V1. Subcortical responses to visual stimulation were robust, but there was little evidence for strengthened subcortical input to V5/MT supporting residual visual function or blindsight-like phenomena.

    **Implications:** These findings suggest that residual visual function in the absence of V1 may be supported by an intact network of extrastriate cortical visual areas. The lack of significant differences between monkey S and controls in the pathways between LGN-V5/MT and pulvinar-V5/MT suggests that either or both could support residual visual abilities. However, the lack of consistent visual cortex activation, possibly due to anesthesia, makes this question difficult to answer.

    **Conclusions:** This study provides new insights into the neural consequences of a V1 lesion in a Rhesus macaque. The findings suggest that residual visual function may be supported by an intact network of extrastriate cortical visual areas, rather than strengthened subcortical input to V5/MT. These results have implications for the development of rehabilitation methods for human patients with V1 lesions. Further research is needed to fully understand the neural mechanisms underlying residual visual function in the absence of V1.

    **Title:** Unveiling the Molecular Mechanism of IFI16-Mediated Recognition of Foreign dsDNA in the Host Nucleus

    **Research Question:** How does the innate immune sensor IFI16 distinguish between foreign double-stranded (ds) DNA of pathogenic origin and host genomic material inside the nucleus?

    **Methods:** We employed a combination of biochemical assays and single-molecule techniques, including time-resolved bulk and single-molecule fluorescence assays, to investigate the molecular mechanisms underlying IFI16-mediated recognition of foreign dsDNA.

    **Main Findings:** Our results demonstrate that IFI16 one-dimensionally tracks long stretches of exposed foreign dsDNA to assemble into supramolecular signaling platforms. Notably, we show that nucleosomes act as barriers that prevent IFI16 from targeting host DNA by directly interfering with these one-dimensional movements. This unique scanning-assisted assembly mechanism enables IFI16 to distinguish between friend and foe and assemble into oligomers efficiently and selectively on foreign DNA.

    **Implications:** Our findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanism of IFI16-mediated recognition of foreign dsDNA in the host nucleus. We propose that the 1D-diffusion mediated assembly plays a key role in regulating the overall IFI16-mediated immune responses. Furthermore, our results suggest that chromatinization acts as a key feature that allows IFI16 to distinguish host from foreign DNA in the nucleus.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals a unifying molecular mechanism that explains how IFI16 resolves the central issues in initiating its foreign-dsDNA sensing pathways. The 1D scanning mechanism of IFI16 allows a limited number of IFI16 molecules to allocate each other on large genomes of invading pathogens. Our findings have significant implications for understanding the molecular basis of innate immunity and may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies to combat infectious diseases.
Title: Spatially Targeted Optical Microproteomics (STOMP): A Novel Technique for Interrogating Micron-Scale Regions of Interest in Mammalian Tissue

    Abstract:

    Research Question: Can a novel proteomics technique, Spatially Targeted Optical Microproteomics (STOMP), be developed to interrogate micron-scale regions of interest (ROIs) in mammalian tissue without genetic manipulation?

    Methods: STOMP combines two-photon laser scanning microscopy with photochemical affinity labeling and mass spectrometry. Methanol or formalin-fixed specimens are stained with fluorescent dyes or antibodies to visualize ROIs, then soaked in solutions containing the photo-tag: 4-benzoylbenzyl-glycyl-hexahistidine. Confocal imaging along with two-photon excitation are used to covalently couple photo-tags to all proteins within each ROI, to a resolution of 0.67 µm in the xy-plane and 1.48 µm axially. After tissue solubilization, photo-tagged proteins are isolated and identified by mass spectrometry.

    Main Findings: As a test case, we examined amyloid plaques in an Alzheimer's disease (AD) mouse model and a post-mortem AD case, confirming known plaque constituents and discovering new ones. Gel electrophoresis of the indiscriminately photo-tagged proteins reveals a very different pattern of protein bands compared to the specimen in which amyloid plaques were specifically targeted for STOMP analysis. The predominant protein photo-tagged in the STOMP sample is Aβ, which is not visible in the non-plaque experiment due to its low overall abundance.

    Implications: STOMP has the potential to advance research in cell biology by yielding the composition of small features that are not amenable to biochemical purification. This technique can be applied to various biological samples, including cell lines, primary cell cultures, ex vivo specimens, biopsy samples, and fixed post-mortem tissue.

    Conclusions: STOMP represents the first application of two-photon microscopy to affinity photolabeling, offering single-micron-scale three-dimensional resolution that is an order of magnitude better than the current state of the art LCM. This novel technique has great potential to provide clues to the etiology of neurodegenerative disease and advance research in cell biology.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates that Fz3 plays a crucial role in axonal growth and survival of cranial and spinal motor neurons during embryonic development. The absence of Fz3 leads to stalling of motor axons and subsequent cell death, suggesting that Fz3 is essential for the proper growth and guidance of motor axons. These findings have important implications for our understanding of PCP signaling and its role in axonal growth and development.     **Title:** Elucidating the Regulatory Roles of Transposable Elements in Early Mouse Development

    **Research Question:** Do transposable elements (TEs) play significant roles in establishing gene regulatory networks in early mouse embryonic and trophoblast stem cells?

These findings have significant implications for our understanding of the evolution and development of gene regulatory networks in mammals.

    **Title:** Unraveling the Evolutionary Paths of C4 Photosynthesis: A Meta-Analysis and Bayesian Approach

    **Research Question:** How did C4 photosynthesis independently evolve in at least 60 plant lineages, and what are the underlying evolutionary drivers of this complex trait?

    **Methods:** We conducted a meta-analysis of 18 lineages containing species that use C3, C4, or intermediate C3–C4 forms of photosynthesis to parameterize a 16-dimensional phenotypic landscape. We developed and experimentally verified a novel Bayesian approach based on a hidden Markov model to predict how the C4 phenotype evolved.

    **Main Findings:** Our analysis revealed that the polyphyletic appearance of C4 photosynthesis is associated with diverse and flexible evolutionary paths that group into four major trajectories. We found that the order of C4 trait acquisition is flexible and driven by non-photosynthetic drivers, and that alternative evolutionary histories underlying the appearance of C4 photosynthesis were determined by ancestral lineage and initial phenotypic alterations unrelated to photosynthesis.

    **Implications:** Our study provides insights into the evolution of one of the most complex traits to have arisen in multiple lineages, C4 photosynthesis. We propose that early evolutionary events determined the downstream phenotypes of C4 sub-types by restricting lineages to independent pathways across phenotype space. Our findings suggest that mechanistic diversity underlies the multiple evolutionary pathways generating C4 photosynthesis, which may be a key factor in facilitating the convergent evolution of complex traits.

    **Conclusions:** This study demonstrates the power of a novel Bayesian approach to infer likely evolutionary trajectories connecting phenotypes from sparsely sampled, highly stochastic data. Our results have significant implications for understanding the evolution of complex traits and may benefit efforts to recapitulate the acquisition of C4 photosynthesis through genetic engineering of C3 species.

    **Title:** Revisiting the Evolution of Nucleotide Specificity in the Bacterial Cytoskeletal Protein SpoIVA

    **Research Question:** What drove the evolution of SpoIVA, a bacterial cytoskeletal protein, from a GTPase to an ATPase, and what is the role of nucleotide specificity in its function?

    **Methods:** We reengineered the nucleotide-binding pocket of SpoIVA to mimic its ancestral GTPase activity, creating SpoIVAGTPase, and examined its function in vitro and in vivo. We also investigated the effects of varying ATP and GTP concentrations on SpoIVA and SpoIVAGTPase polymerization.

    **Implications:** Our study reveals that the nucleotide base, not just the energy released from hydrolysis, plays a crucial role in specific biological functions.     **Conclusions:** Our results demonstrate that the evolution of SpoIVA from a GTPase to an ATPase was driven by the selective pressure of increased ATP levels at the end of sporulation. Our findings have implications for understanding the evolution of protein function and the development of novel therapeutic strategies targeting protein-nucleotide interactions.

    **Title:** Unveiling the Spatial Structure of the Arabidopsis Circadian Clock: Single-Cell Oscillations and Cell-to-Cell Coupling

    **Research Question:** How robust is the Arabidopsis circadian clock at the single-cell level, and how is clock timing coordinated across the plant?

    **Methods:** We used a reporter for the core clock gene CIRCADIAN CLOCK ASSOCIATED 1 (CCA1) to examine the dynamics of the Arabidopsis clock across the whole plant at the single-cell level over several days under constant environmental conditions.

    **Main Findings:** Our data reveal robust single-cell oscillations, albeit desynchronized, with two waves of clock activity observed: one going down and one up the root. We also find evidence of cell-to-cell coupling of the clock, especially in the root tip. A simple model suggests that cell-to-cell coupling and measured period differences between cells can generate the observed waves in clock gene expression.

    **Implications:** Our results reveal the spatial structure of the plant clock and suggest that unlike the centralized mammalian clock, the Arabidopsis clock has multiple coordination points. We estimate the coupling strength between cells and find evidence of coupling, especially strong in the root tip. Our findings support a more decentralized model of clock coordination in plants.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates that the Arabidopsis circadian clock is robust at the single-cell level and that cell-to-cell coupling plays a crucial role in coordinating clock timing across the plant. The observed waves of clock gene expression and variable clock periods at each position in the root contribute to desynchronization of oscillating single cells, leading to damping of rhythms at the whole plant level. Our results provide new insights into the spatial structure and coordination of the plant circadian clock system, challenging previous hierarchical models and supporting a more decentralized model of clock coordination in plants.

    **Title:** Neural Biomarkers for Multi-Talker Speech Intelligibility in Adults with Clinically Normal Hearing

    **Title:** DeepPoseKit: A Novel Software Toolkit for Fast and Accurate Animal Pose Estimation using Deep Learning

    **Research Question:** How can we develop a user-friendly and efficient software toolkit for estimating animal posture from images and videos, addressing the limitations of current methods in speed and robustness?

    **Methods:** We introduce DeepPoseKit, a novel software toolkit that leverages an efficient multi-scale deep-learning model, Stacked DenseNet, and a fast GPU-based peak-detection algorithm to estimate keypoint locations with subpixel precision.     **Main Findings:** We demonstrate the versatility of DeepPoseKit in multiple challenging animal pose estimation tasks in laboratory and field settings, including groups of interacting individuals.
