    **Implications:** DeepPoseKit reduces barriers to using advanced tools for measuring behavior, enabling researchers to collect larger, higher-resolution, and more reliable behavioral datasets. This, in turn, has the potential to revolutionize our understanding of relationships between behavior, the brain, and the environment.

    **Conclusions:** We present DeepPoseKit as a user-friendly and efficient software toolkit for estimating animal posture from images and videos. Our approach addresses the limitations of current methods in speed and robustness, making it a valuable resource for researchers across the behavioral sciences. The code, documentation, and examples for DeepPoseKit are freely available under a permissive open-source license, facilitating widespread adoption and further development. By providing a fast and accurate method for animal pose estimation, DeepPoseKit has the potential to transform our understanding of animal behavior and its underlying mechanisms.

    **Title:** Disruption of Glycolytic Flux Triggers NLRP3-Mediated Inflammatory Signaling and Pyroptosis in Innate Immune Cells

Supplementation of the glycolytic product pyruvate resulted in significant reductions in caspase-1 activation, IL-1β secretion, and cell death induced by S. typhimurium.

    **Title:** Aberrant Proinsulin Misfolding and Disulfide-Linked Complexes in Prediabetes and Type 2 Diabetes

    **Research Question:** What is the role of proinsulin misfolding and disulfide-linked complexes in the development of prediabetes and type 2 diabetes?

Our findings highlight the importance of ER folding environment and β-cell function in the development of insulin deficiency and diabetes. Further research is needed to explore the therapeutic potential of targeting proinsulin misfolding and disulfide-linked complexes in the prevention and treatment of type 2 diabetes.

**Title:** Covert Attention Activates Spatially Structured Receptive Fields in Entorhinal Cortex

**Research Question:** Do neurons in the entorhinal cortex exhibit spatially structured receptive fields in response to movements of covert attention, independent of physical movement?

**Methods:** We recorded the activity of 141 neurons in the entorhinal cortex of two rhesus macaque monkeys performing a covert attention tracking task. The monkeys were trained to maintain central fixation while attending to a small dot moving around a computer monitor. We evaluated the standard gridness measure to assess the spatial structure of the receptive fields.

**Main Findings:** Our results show that a significant population (14%) of entorhinal cells exhibited spatially structured receptive fields with a triangular tiling of space in response to movements of covert attention, without any physical movement. This is in contrast to recordings obtained in the hippocampus, where grid-like representations were not observed.

**Implications:** Our findings provide strong evidence that the activation of cells in entorhinal cortex with spatially structured firing fields does not require physical movement through an environment. This suggests that grid cells in the entorhinal cortex may participate in a range of cognitive functions, including attentional processes. The overlap between the neural substrate for overt and covert attention (Corbetta et al., 1998) supports the hypothesis that grid cells can represent not only gaze location but also the locus of attention in general.

**Conclusions:** Our results demonstrate that entorhinal cortex neurons can exhibit spatially structured receptive fields in response to movements of covert attention, independent of physical movement. This finding has significant implications for our understanding of the neural basis of spatial cognition and attentional processes. The ability of entorhinal cortex neurons to represent spatial information in the absence of physical movement suggests a more general role for grid cells in cognitive functions beyond navigation. Further research is needed to fully elucidate the mechanisms underlying this phenomenon and its implications for our understanding of the neural basis of cognition.

    Title: Achieving Reproducibility in Rodent Behavior: A Standardized Task for Decision-Making in Mice

    Research Question: Can a complex mouse behavior be reproduced across multiple laboratories, and what are the implications for achieving reproducibility in neuroscience?

    Methods: We developed and implemented a standardized task to probe decision-making in head-fixed mice, which assays perceptual and value-based decision-making. We standardized training protocols, experimental hardware, software, and procedures across seven laboratories in three countries. A total of 140 mice were trained, and 5 million mouse choices were collected into a publicly available database.

    Main Findings: Despite variable learning speeds across mice and laboratories, once training was complete, there were no significant differences in behavior across laboratories. Mice in different laboratories adopted similar reliance on visual stimuli, past successes and failures, and estimates of stimulus prior probability to guide their choices. These results demonstrate that a complex mouse behavior can be reproduced across multiple laboratories.

    Implications: Our findings establish a standard for reproducible rodent behavior and provide an unprecedented dataset and open-access tools to study decision-making in mice. This study suggests a path toward achieving reproducibility in neuroscience through collaborative open-science approaches. By making data and techniques fully available to the broader community, we can accelerate the development of new adaptations and variations of our approach, and improve the quality and reproducibility of neural correlates of decision-making studies.

    Conclusions: This study demonstrates that a complex mouse behavior can be successfully reproduced across laboratories, and highlights the importance of collaborative open-science approaches in achieving reproducibility in neuroscience. By providing a standardized task and open-access tools, we aim to catalyze the use of mice in high-quality, reproducible studies of neural correlates of decision-making, and ultimately advance our understanding of the neural basis of behavior.

    **Title:** Divergent Sensory Coding Mechanisms in Mormyrid Fish Reveal Adaptations for Social Communication

    **Research Question:** How do evolutionary differences in sensory perception relate to the physiological coding of stimuli in Mormyrid fish, and what are the implications for social communication?

    **Methods:** We investigated the peripheral sensory coding mechanisms of Mormyrid fish, which communicate through electric organ discharges (EODs) with variable interpulse intervals (IPIs). We compared the responses of two types of sensory receptors: spiking receptors, which encode signal waveform into spike-timing differences, and oscillating receptors, which produce spontaneously oscillating potentials.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that oscillating receptors respond to electric pulses by resetting their phase, resulting in transient synchrony among receptors that encodes signal timing and location, but not waveform. These receptors are most sensitive to frequencies found only in the collective signals of groups of conspecifics and are correlated with increased behavioral responses to these frequencies. In contrast, spiking receptors are specialized for detecting signals from individuals.

    **Implications:** Our findings provide the first evidence for sensory coding through oscillatory synchrony and suggest that different perceptual capabilities correspond to different receptor physiologies. We hypothesize that these divergent mechanisms represent adaptations for different social environments, with oscillating receptors facilitating detection of communication signals from large groups of fish.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals a novel mechanism for peripheral sensory coding in Mormyrid fish, where oscillating receptors respond to stimuli with phase resets, resulting in transient synchrony that encodes signal timing and location. This mechanism is specialized for detecting signals from groups of conspecifics and is correlated with increased behavioral responses to these frequencies. Our findings have important implications for understanding the evolution of sensory perception and social communication in animals.

    **Title:** Atomic Structure of Bacteriophage BPP-1 Reveals Novel Fold Topology and Insights into Phage-Display Engineering

Our findings have significant implications for phage-display engineering, as the termini of both CP and MCP are ideally positioned for DGR-based phage-display engineering.

    **Conclusions:** This study provides the first atomic structure of BPP-1 at 3.5 Å resolution, revealing a novel fold topology of the Johnson fold in BPP-1 MCP. Our results highlight the importance of non-covalent interactions in the formation of the BPP-1 capsid and demonstrate the potential of BPP-1 as a model system for phage-display engineering. The atomic structure of BPP-1 will facilitate further research into the mechanisms of phage-display engineering and the development of novel biotechnological applications.

Title: **Adaptation of Dengue Virus Populations to Host Environments Reveals Balancing Act between Beneficial and Deleterious Mutations**

**Research Question:** How do dengue virus (DENV) populations adapt to the distinct environments of human and mosquito hosts, and what are the relative contributions of beneficial and deleterious mutations to this adaptation?

**Methods:** We employed serial passage of DENV in human and mosquito cell lines, followed by ultra-deep sequencing to estimate the fitness effects of all single-nucleotide variants in these populations. This approach allowed us to quantify the contributions of beneficial and deleterious mutations to the collective fitness of the population.

**Main Findings:** Our analysis revealed that the continuous influx of deleterious mutations counterbalances the effect of rare, host-specific beneficial mutations, shaping the path of adaptation. Beneficial mutations preferentially mapped to intrinsically disordered domains in the viral proteome and clustered to defined regions in the genome. These phenotypically redundant adaptive alleles may facilitate host-specific DENV adaptation. Notably, the evolutionary constraints observed in our simple system mirrored trends observed across DENV and Zika strains, indicating that our findings recapitulate key biophysical and biological constraints shaping long-term viral evolution.

**Implications:** Our study highlights the importance of understanding the interplay between beneficial and deleterious mutations in shaping the evolution of viral populations. The identification of host-specific beneficial mutations and their clustering in specific regions of the DENV genome provides insights into the molecular mechanisms of selection and adaptation operating on DENV populations in cells and host populations.

**Conclusions:** Our results demonstrate that adaptation of DENV populations relies on a balance between the dynamics of rare beneficial mutations and the significant and constantly replenished load of deleterious alleles. The identification of "hotspots" of host-specific selection in the DENV genome and proteome provides a framework for understanding the evolutionary paths of DENV in distinct cellular environments. These findings have important implications for the development of effective strategies to combat DENV infection and transmission.

Title: Elucidating the Mechanism of Bone Morphogenetic Protein (BMP) Signaling Gradient Formation in Zebrafish Embryos: A Source-Sink Mechanism Revealed

    Abstract:

    Background: Bone Morphogenetic Proteins (BMPs) play a crucial role in patterning the dorsoventral (DV) embryonic axis of vertebrates and invertebrates. The prevailing view in vertebrates suggests that BMP gradient formation is achieved through a counter-gradient of BMP antagonists, often accompanied by ligand shuttling to generate peak signaling levels. However, the exact mechanism remains unclear.

    Research Question: What is the mechanism of BMP signaling gradient formation in zebrafish embryos?

    Methods: We employed a robust quantitative imaging method to directly measure the BMP signaling gradient in wild-type and mutant zebrafish embryos. We combined these data with a mathematical model-based computational screen to test hypotheses for gradient formation.

    Main Findings: Our analysis ruled out a BMP shuttling mechanism and a bmp transcriptionally-informed gradient mechanism.     Implications: Our findings provide new insights into the mechanism of BMP signaling gradient formation in zebrafish embryos, challenging the prevailing view of a counter-gradient mechanism. The source-sink mechanism highlights the importance of BMP antagonist distribution in shaping the BMP signaling gradient.

    Conclusions: Our study reveals a novel mechanism of BMP signaling gradient formation in zebrafish embryos, shedding light on the complex interactions between BMP ligands and antagonists during development. The source-sink mechanism provides a new framework for understanding the patterning of the DV axis in vertebrates and invertebrates, with implications for our understanding of developmental biology and disease.

    **Title:** Characterization of MFSD1, a Novel Lysosomal Transporter, and Its Essential Accessory Subunit GLMP

Title: Elucidating the Mechanism of Kinesin’s Microtubule-Attached Cycle: Insights from High-Resolution Cryo-EM Reconstructions

    Abstract:

    Background: Microtubule-based transport by kinesin motors, powered by ATP hydrolysis, is essential for various cellular processes in eukaryotes. Understanding the molecular mechanism of kinesin’s microtubule-attached cycle is crucial for elucidating its function.

    Research Question: What is the structural basis of kinesin’s nucleotide-binding behavior on and off microtubules, and how do microtubules regulate kinesin’s affinities for ADP and ATP?

    Methods: We obtained high-resolution cryo-EM reconstructions of the human monomeric kinesin motor domain (K349) attached to microtubules in two different chemical states: no-nucleotide and ADP•aluminum fluoride (ADP•Al•Fx).     Main Findings: Our results reveal a mechanistic scheme in which microtubule attachment, mediated by a universally conserved ‘linchpin’ residue in kinesin (N255), triggers a clamshell opening of the nucleotide cleft and accompanying release of ADP. This structural transition is reminiscent of the analogous nucleotide-exchange steps in myosin and F1-ATPase motors.

    Implications: Our findings provide a structural basis for kinesin’s nucleotide-binding behavior on and off microtubules, revealing how microtubules regulate kinesin’s affinities for ADP and ATP by modulating the conformation of the switch loops. This uncouples the motor’s nucleotide affinity from its microtubule affinity, allowing microtubules to regulate kinesin’s activity.

    Conclusions: Our study elucidates the mechanism of kinesin’s microtubule-attached cycle, providing insights into the structural basis of kinesin’s nucleotide-binding behavior on and off microtubules. These findings have significant implications for understanding the regulation of kinesin’s activity and its role in various cellular processes.

    **Title:** Spatial Propagation of Radial ERK Activity Distribution (SPREAD) in the Epidermis: A Novel Mechanism of Cell Proliferation Regulation

    **Research Question:** How is extracellular signal-regulated kinase (ERK) activity regulated and propagated in the epidermis, and what is its role in cell cycle progression?

    **Methods:** We used transgenic mice expressing a FRET biosensor of ERK to visualize ERK activity in living epidermal cells at single-cell resolution. Long-term imaging of intact epidermis revealed radial propagation of ERK activity, which we termed Spatial Propagation of Radial ERK Activity Distribution (SPREAD). We investigated the mechanism of ERK propagation and its role in cell cycle progression.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that SPREADs are bursts of ERK activation that originate from a few cells and propagate radially up to 50 μm over 30 minutes. The frequency of SPREADs correlates with the rate of epidermal cell division. SPREADs are stimulated by 12-O-tetradecanoylphorbol 13-acetate (TPA) in a manner dependent on epidermal growth factor receptors (EGFRs) and their cognate ligands. At the wounded skin, ERK activation propagates as a trigger wave in parallel to the wound edge, suggesting that ERK activation propagation can be superimposed. Furthermore, we found that SPREADs are associated with G2/M cell cycle progression.

    **Implications:** Our findings provide new insights into how cell proliferation and transient ERK activity are synchronized in a living tissue. The discovery of SPREADs reveals a novel mechanism of ERK activity regulation, which is essential for homeostasis in the epidermis and other tissues.

    **Conclusions:** In conclusion, our study demonstrates that ERK activity is dynamically regulated in the epidermis through SPREADs, which are bursts of ERK activation that propagate radially and are associated with cell cycle progression. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of how growth signal activation is generated and propagated throughout the tissue, and highlight the importance of ERK activity regulation in maintaining tissue homeostasis.

    **Title:** Elucidating the Principles of Circadian Rhythm Entrainment: A Mathematical Framework for Predicting Clock Behavior in Varying Environments

    **Research Question:** What are the underlying principles that govern the entrainment of circadian rhythms in response to external cues, and can a mathematical framework be developed to predict clock behavior in various environmental conditions?

    **Methods:** This study employed a combination of in vivo and in vitro experiments using the cyanobacterial circadian clock as a model system. The authors reconstituted the core oscillator responsible for driving genome-wide transcriptional rhythms using purified Kai proteins and simulated seasonal effects by driving the Kai proteins with pulses of nucleotides on a 24-hour cycle.

    **Main Findings:** The results show that the phase of the circadian rhythm follows a simple scaling law in light-dark cycles, tracking midday across conditions with variable day length. The core biochemical oscillator comprised of the Kai proteins behaves similarly when driven by metabolic pulses in vitro, indicating that such dynamics are intrinsic to these proteins. A general mathematical framework based on instantaneous transformation of the clock cycle by external cues was developed, which successfully predicts clock behavior under many cycling environments.

    **Implications:** The findings of this study provide new insights into the principles of circadian rhythm entrainment and suggest that the ability to track midday is an intrinsic property of the core oscillator. The mathematical framework developed in this study can be used to guide the design of experiments that probe the molecular origins of key mathematical features and may be applicable to clocks in many organisms.

    **Conclusions:** This study demonstrates that the entrained behavior of the circadian clock can be captured quantitatively by a mathematical framework with a small number of parameters. The precisely defined nature of the cyanobacterial clock facilitates such experiments, and the model is general, making it useful for other organisms as well. The results of this study have significant implications for our understanding of circadian biology and may lead to the development of new approaches for predicting and manipulating clock behavior in various environmental conditions.

    **Title:** Meis1 Inactivation Disrupts Sympatho-Vagal Regulation of Cardiac Rhythmicity and Increases Susceptibility to Sudden Cardiac Death

    **Research Question:** Does Meis1 inactivation in the mouse neural crest lead to altered sympatho-vagal regulation of cardiac rhythmicity and increased susceptibility to SCD?

    **Conclusions:** This study highlights the importance of Meis1 in the regulation of cardiac rhythmicity and increases our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying SCD. Our findings suggest that Meis1 mutations could mediate susceptibility to unexplained SCD by disrupting sympatho-vagal regulation of cardiac functions. These results have significant implications for the development of novel therapeutic strategies to prevent SCD.

    **Title:** Dynamic Regulation of NRSF Target Genes Contributes to Epileptogenesis

    **Title:** Emergence of Feature Selectivity in Parallel Olfactory Pathways via Distinct Lateral Inhibitory Circuits

The distinct lateral inhibitory circuits in mitral and tufted cells allow them to encode different aspects of the stimulus, enabling the olfactory system to process complex odor information more efficiently. These findings contribute to our understanding of the neural mechanisms underlying sensory processing and may have implications for the development of novel computational models of sensory systems.
Title: Elucidating the Molecular and Supramolecular Determinants of Allostery in the M2 Muscarinic Receptor: A Comparative Study of Monomers and Oligomers

    Abstract:
    The M2 muscarinic receptor, a prototypic model of allostery in G protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs), has been extensively studied, yet the molecular and supramolecular determinants of its allosteric effects remain unknown. To address this knowledge gap, we compared the allosteric properties of monomers and oligomers of the M2 muscarinic receptor using a FRET-based sensor of conformation at the allosteric site and pharmacological assays involving mutants engineered to preclude intramolecular effects. Our results reveal that allosteric effects in monomers are exclusively negative and arise from intramolecular electrostatic repulsion between the allosteric and orthosteric ligands. In contrast, allosteric effects in oligomers can be positive or negative, depending on the allosteric-orthosteric pair, and arise from interactions within and between the constituent protomers. Molecular dynamics simulations at the atomic level further elucidated the complex behavior of oligomers, which is characteristic of muscarinic receptors in myocardial preparations. These findings provide new insights into the molecular and supramolecular determinants of allostery in the M2 muscarinic receptor and highlight the importance of considering the oligomeric nature of GPCRs in understanding their allosteric behavior.

    Research Question:
    What are the molecular and supramolecular determinants of allostery in the M2 muscarinic receptor, and how do they differ between monomers and oligomers?

    Methods:
    We employed a FRET-based sensor of conformation at the allosteric site and pharmacological assays involving mutants engineered to preclude intramolecular effects to compare the allosteric properties of monomers and oligomers of the M2 muscarinic receptor. Molecular dynamics simulations at the atomic level were used to examine the electrostatic, steric, and conformational determinants of allostery.

    Main Findings:
    Our results show that allosteric effects in monomers are exclusively negative and arise from intramolecular electrostatic repulsion between the allosteric and orthosteric ligands. In contrast, allosteric effects in oligomers can be positive or negative, depending on the allosteric-orthosteric pair, and arise from interactions within and between the constituent protomers.

    Implications:
    These findings have significant implications for our understanding of the molecular and supramolecular determinants of allostery in the M2 muscarinic receptor and highlight the importance of considering the oligomeric nature of GPCRs in understanding their allosteric behavior.

    Conclusions:
    Our study provides new insights into the molecular and supramolecular determinants of allostery in the M2 muscarinic receptor and demonstrates the complex behavior of oligomers, which is characteristic of muscarinic receptors in myocardial preparations. These findings have important implications for the development of novel therapeutic strategies targeting GPCRs and highlight the need for further research into the oligomeric nature of these receptors.

    **Title:** Histone Modification by SETD2 Facilitates DNA Damage Response and Repair

    **Title:** A Missense Variant in Rasgrp1 Disrupts mTOR Signaling and Triggers Autoimmune Dysregulation in CD4+ T Cells

Title: Sex-Specific Effects of Social Isolation on Corticotropin-Releasing Hormone (CRH) Neurons in Mice

Background: Social networks play a crucial role in buffering the effects of stress, but the absence of a social network can also be stressful. We investigated the effects of social isolation on the intrinsic properties of corticotropin-releasing hormone (CRH) neurons in the paraventricular nucleus (PVN) of the hypothalamus in mice.

Research Question: Do social isolation and acute physical stress have sex-specific effects on the biophysical properties of CRH neurons in mice?

Methods: We isolated preadolescent female and male mice from their littermates for 24 hours and examined the intrinsic properties of CRH neurons in the PVN using acute brain slices. We also subjected group-housed females and single-housed males to acute physical stress (swim stress) and examined the effects on CRH neurons.

Main Findings: We found that social isolation increased first spike latency (FSL) and decreased excitability of CRH neurons in female mice, but not in age-matched male mice. In contrast, acute physical stress increased FSL in group-housed females and single-housed males. The increase in FSL following social isolation or acute physical stress was blocked by the glucocorticoid synthesis inhibitor, metyrapone, and mimicked by exogenous corticosterone.

Implications: Our findings demonstrate that social isolation, but not acute physical stress, has sex-specific effects on PVN CRH neurons. These results suggest that female mice may interpret social isolation as a stress, leading to changes in the intrinsic properties of CRH neurons.

Conclusions: Our study provides new insights into the sex-specific effects of social isolation and acute physical stress on CRH neurons in mice. These findings have implications for our understanding of the neural mechanisms underlying sex differences in stress responses and may inform the development of sex-specific treatments for stress-related disorders.

    **Title:** Evolutionary Optimization of Hemoglobin Affinity and Cooperativity for Tissue Oxygen Delivery in Mammals

    **Background:** Hemoglobin (Hb) is a model protein for studying molecular adaptation in vertebrates. While both affinity and cooperativity of oxygen binding to Hb affect tissue oxygen delivery, only affinity was previously thought to determine molecular adaptations of Hb.

    **Research Question:** Do Hb affinity and cooperativity reflect evolutionary and physiological adaptations that optimize tissue oxygen delivery in mammals?

    **Methods:** We derived the relationship between the Hill coefficient and relative affinity and conformational changes parameters of the Monod-Wymann-Changeux allosteric model and graphed the ‘biophysical Hill landscape’ describing this relation.

    **Main Findings:** We found that mammalian Hb cooperativity values reside on a ridge of maximum cooperativity along this landscape, allowing for both gross- and fine-tuning of tissue oxygen unloading to meet the distinct metabolic requirements of mammalian tissues for oxygen. Our findings reveal the mechanism underlying body size-related adaptation of mammalian Hb.

    **Implications:** The generality and implications of our findings suggest that evolutionary tuning of mammalian Hb maximum cooperativity and affinity points, achieved by co-varying the relative affinity and conformational ratio of its quaternary states, regulates tissue oxygen delivery by Hb and serves as one means to meet the distinct needs of different animal tissues for oxygen.

    **Conclusions:** Our results support the hypothesis that changes in mammalian Hb binding and conformation have been tuned during evolution, giving rise to close to maximum cooperativity in oxygen binding, albeit yielding a different maximum nH value in each case. Furthermore, we found that the greater an animal’s metabolic rate, the higher was the observed maximum Hill cooperativity value of oxygen binding by Hb, and an inverse linear correlation was found between the metabolic rate and Hb affinity.

    **Title:** Evolutionary Robustness of Cells to Physiologically Destructive Perturbations: A Case Study in Saccharomyces cerevisiae

    **Research Question:** How do cells adapt to the removal of a crucial component of a functional module, and what are the implications for our understanding of evolutionary robustness?

    **Methods:** We conducted an evolutionary experiment in Saccharomyces cerevisiae, deleting the polarity gene BEM1 and allowing the cells to evolve for 1000 generations under strong selective pressure. We sequenced the genomes of the evolved lineages and monitored their polarization dynamics.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that the bem1∆ lineages rapidly increased in fitness and eventually reached >90% of the fitness of their BEM1 ancestors. These mutations led to a substantial recovery in the speed and accuracy of cell polarization.

    **Implications:** Our study demonstrates that cells can be evolutionarily robust to physiologically destructive perturbations, and that recovery by gene inactivation can lead to rapid divergence in the parts list for cell biologically important functions. The larger target size for mutations that inactivate proteins favors evolutionary trajectories that remove components from modules over those that quantitatively alter their properties.

    **Conclusions:** Our findings have important implications for our understanding of the evolution of cellular functions and the robustness of cells to perturbations. They suggest that cells can evolve to perform the same biological functions with different proteins, and that gene inactivation can be a key mechanism for adaptation to strong selective pressure. This study provides new insights into the evolution of Saccharomyces cerevisiae and has broader implications for our understanding of the evolution of cellular functions in general.

    **Title:** Neural Mechanisms of Economic Commitment: A Study of Value Encoding in the Medial Prefrontal Cortex

    **Research Question:** How do humans integrate value signals over time when making economic commitments, and what neural mechanisms are involved in this process?

    **Methods:** In this study, we used a multi-alternative choice task to investigate the neural mechanisms of economic commitment in humans. We used functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) to examine the activity in the rostral medial prefrontal cortex (rmPFC) and dorsal anterior cingulate cortex (dACC) while participants made these decisions.

The results suggest that the rmPFC plays a critical role in integrating value signals over time, while the dACC is involved in encoding value when decisions involve rejection or deferral.     **Conclusions:** This study provides novel insights into the neural mechanisms of economic commitment and the computation of value in the medial prefrontal cortex. Our results highlight the importance of considering the temporal dynamics of value encoding in economic decision-making and the role of the dACC in encoding value when decisions involve rejection or deferral. These findings have important implications for our understanding of economic behavior and the neural mechanisms that underlie it.

    **Title:** Puzzle-Shaped Cells in Plant Epidermis: A Mechanistic Link between Cell Shape and Mechanical Stress

We demonstrate that puzzle-shaped cells in isodiametric plant organs provide a means to avoid large open areas in the cell and the high stresses that they induce. Our results suggest that plant cells regulate their shape to prevent excessive mechanical stress, which is a critical factor in plant growth and development. This study contributes to our understanding of the complex relationships between cell shape, mechanical stress, and tissue growth in plants.

    **Title:** Synergistic Interactions between Cross-Intron and Cross-Exon Processes Promote Efficient Pre-Spliceosome Assembly in Human Cells

    **Research Question:** How do cross-intron and cross-exon interactions jointly contribute to the assembly of pre-spliceosomes on multi-intron pre-mRNA molecules in human cells?

    **Methods:** We employed single-molecule fluorescence microscopy (CoSMoS) to investigate the dynamics of spliceosomal subcomplexes on individual pre-mRNA molecules in human nuclear extracts. Fluorescently tagged spliceosomal subcomplexes were used to visualize interactions between U1 and U2 subcomplexes and the 5' splice sites of introns and exons.

Our results highlight the importance of cross-intron and cross-exon interactions in promoting correct and efficient splicing of multi-intron pre-mRNAs, and provide a framework for understanding the molecular mechanisms contributing to this remarkable accuracy.

    **Title:** A Novel Ubiquitination-Dependent Folding System for Membrane Proteins on the Cytosolic Side of the Endoplasmic Reticulum

    **Research Question:** How do membrane proteins fold efficiently on the cytosolic side of the endoplasmic reticulum (ER), and what role does ubiquitination play in this process?

    **Methods:** We investigated the folding of the Wnt signaling coreceptor LRP6 and other membrane proteins, including CFTR and anthrax toxin receptor 2, using a combination of biochemical and cell biological approaches.

    **Main Findings:** We discovered a novel ubiquitination-dependent folding system that operates on the cytosolic side of the ER. Upon synthesis, LRP6 undergoes site-specific ubiquitination on a specific lysine residue (Lys-1403), which retains it in the ER and prevents degradation. Subsequent removal of ubiquitin from this lysine by the deubiquitinating enzyme USP19 allows LRP6 to exit the ER. This ubiquitination-deubiquitination cycle is reminiscent of the glucosylation-deglucosylation cycle in the ER lumen. Folded LRP6 molecules undergo palmitoylation and ER export, while unsuccessfully folded proteins are polyubiquitinated on other lysines and targeted to degradation.

    **Implications:** This ubiquitin-dependent folding system not only controls the proteostasis of LRP6 but also regulates the folding of other membrane proteins, including CFTR and anthrax toxin receptor 2, which are involved in severe human diseases.

    **Conclusions:** Our findings reveal a novel assisted-folding system that operates on the cytosolic side of the ER and depends on site-specific cycles of ubiquitination-deubiquitination. This system provides a mechanism for the efficient folding of membrane proteins, which is essential for maintaining cellular homeostasis and preventing protein misfolding diseases. The discovery of this ubiquitin-dependent folding system has important implications for understanding the regulation of membrane protein folding and the development of therapeutic strategies for protein misfolding diseases.

**Title:** Sex-based modulation of cognitive processes in value-based decision making: A computational analysis of exploration and exploitation in mice

**Research Question:** Do sex differences exist in the latent variables underlying behavior in a classic value-based decision making task, and if so, how do these differences contribute to individual differences in vulnerability to neuropsychiatric disorders?

**Methods:** We used a restless two-armed bandit task to investigate sex differences in exploration and exploitation in mice. Male and female mice were trained on the task, and their behavior was analyzed using computational tools, including Hidden Markov Models (HMM) and Reinforcement Learning (RL) models.

**Main Findings:** Our results show that male and female mice had similar accuracy in the task, but they achieved this performance via different patterns of exploration. Male mice tended to make more exploratory choices overall, whereas female mice explored less but learned more quickly during exploration. Post-hoc win-stay lose-shift analyses revealed that female mice displayed more win-stay behaviors, indicating that they were more likely to repeat behaviors that produced reward on the previous trial.

**Implications:** These findings suggest that sex exerts stronger influences on decision making during periods of learning and exploration than during stable choices. Exploration during decision making is altered in people diagnosed with addictions, depression, and neurodevelopmental disabilities, pinpointing the neural mechanisms of exploration as a highly translational avenue for conferring sex-modulated vulnerability to neuropsychiatric diagnoses.

**Conclusions:** Our study provides evidence for sex differences in the latent variables underlying behavior in a classic value-based decision making task. These differences in exploration and exploitation strategies may contribute to individual differences in vulnerability to neuropsychiatric disorders. Further research is needed to understand how sex, explore-exploit states, and reward outcomes interact to influence animal choices, and how these findings can be translated to humans.

    **Title:** Mechanistic Insights into Membrane Protein Extraction by the Ubiquitin Ligase Doa10

    **Research Question:** How do hydrophobic transmembrane (TM) segments of membrane proteins move from the phospholipid bilayer into the cytosol during endoplasmic reticulum-associated protein degradation (ERAD)?

    **Methods:** We employed a reconstituted system with purified proteins from Saccharomyces cerevisiae to investigate the role of the ubiquitin ligase Doa10 in membrane protein extraction.

    **Conclusions:** Our study resolves a long-standing question regarding the folding state of luminal domains during retrotranslocation, demonstrating that they are extracted in an unfolded state. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of ERAD and its role in maintaining protein homeostasis in the cell. Furthermore, our results highlight the importance of Doa10 as a retrotranslocase in the ERAD pathway, providing a framework for future studies on the regulation of membrane protein quality control.

    Title: Epigenetic Memory of Gene Expression States is Stored in Cis at the Polycomb Target Gene FLOWERING LOCUS C (FLC) in Arabidopsis

    Background: The Polycomb Repressive Complex 2 (PRC2) maintains epigenetic repression of many genes in animals and plants by modifying chromatin at its targets.     Research Question: Can Polycomb-repressed chromatin at a single gene instruct its own inheritance and constitute a cis memory system?

    Methods: To address this question, we exploited the classic epigenetic process of vernalization in Arabidopsis. We crossed FLC-mCherry to FLC-Venus plants to generate F1 hybrids carrying a single copy of each transgene and monitored the expression of FLC in the same plants.

    Main Findings: We show that one copy of FLC can be repressed while the other is active, and this'mixed' expression state is inherited through many cell divisions as plants develop. Furthermore, we demonstrate that epigenetic memory of FLC expression is stored not in trans but in cis.

    Implications: Our findings suggest that Polycomb-repressed chromatin at a single gene can instruct its own inheritance and constitute a cis memory system. This challenges the prevailing view that epigenetic memory is stored in the concentrations of diffusible regulatory factors (trans memory).

    Conclusions: Our study provides evidence that epigenetic memory of gene expression states is stored in cis at the Polycomb target gene FLC in Arabidopsis. This has significant implications for our understanding of how cells'remember' past events, such as environmental or developmental cues, and how epigenetic information is inherited through cell division. Our findings highlight the importance of cis memory in the regulation of gene expression and provide new insights into the mechanisms of epigenetic inheritance.

    **Title:** Oligomerization of Endoplasmic Reticulum Hsp70 Chaperone BiP: A Mechanism for Buffering Unfolded Protein Load in the Endoplasmic Reticulum

    **Research Question:** What is the structure and functional significance of oligomers formed by the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) Hsp70 chaperone BiP, and how do these oligomers contribute to the regulation of unfolded protein load in the ER?

    **Methods:** Site-specific proteolysis and crosslinking were used to probe the architecture of BiP oligomers, while native gel electrophoresis was employed to investigate the relationship between unfolded protein load and BiP oligomerization.

    **Main Findings:** BiP oligomers were found to consist of adjacent protomers engaging the interdomain linker of one molecule in the substrate binding site of another, which attenuates the chaperone function of oligomeric BiP. Lumenal ER calcium depletion caused rapid oligomerization of mammalian BiP and a coincidental diminution in substrate binding, indicating the relative inertness of the oligomers. Native gel electrophoresis revealed a rapidly-modulated reciprocal relationship between the burden of unfolded proteins and BiP oligomers, as well as slower equilibration between oligomers and inactive, covalently-modified BiP.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that BiP oligomerization is a mechanism for buffering fluctuations in ER unfolded protein load on a rapid timescale, which is attainable neither by inter-conversion of active and covalently-modified BiP nor by the conventional unfolded protein response. The oligomeric form of BiP is a relatively inactive storage form of the chaperone that is accessible on a short timescale.

    **Conclusions:** Our results paint a coherent picture of how enhanced production of unfolded proteins rapidly draws on the pool of inactive, oligomeric BiP to service its growing clientele. The inverse correlation between unfolded protein burden and BiP oligomerization appears to break down in the context of ER calcium depletion, which is both a strong inducer of the UPR and promotes rapid oligomerization of BiP. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of the regulation of unfolded protein load in the ER and the role of Hsp70 chaperones in maintaining protein homeostasis.

    **Title:** Structural Insights into the Coat Protein of Pepino Mosaic Virus: Implications for Flexuous Filamentous Viruses

    **Research Question:** What is the three-dimensional structure of the coat protein (CP) of Pepino mosaic virus (PepMV), a representative of the genus Potexvirus, and how does it interact with viral RNA?

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides the first high-resolution structure of a potexvirus CP and sheds light on its interactions with viral RNA. The structural homology between potexvirus CP and phlebovirus NP highlights the possibility of gene transfer between eukaryotic (+) and (-) ssRNA viruses. These findings have important implications for our understanding of the evolution and diversity of flexuous filamentous viruses and may inform the development of novel strategies for controlling these economically important plant pathogens.

    **Title:** mRNA:ncRNA Avoidance: A Novel Global Factor Influencing Protein Abundance in Prokaryotes

**Title:** Asynchronous Denervation of Neuromuscular Junctions in Amyotrophic Lateral Sclerosis: A Complex and Dynamic Process

**Background:** Amyotrophic Lateral Sclerosis (ALS) is a devastating neurodegenerative disease characterized by the progressive loss of motor neurons and neuromuscular junctions (NMJs). Despite its early onset, the mechanisms underlying NMJ denervation in ALS remain poorly understood.

**Research Question:** Is NMJ denervation in ALS a manifestation of global motor neuron degeneration or a complex and dynamic process involving local branch-specific events?

**Methods:** We employed in vivo imaging of single axons and their NMJs in SOD1G37R mice over a three-month period to investigate the time course and dynamics of structural changes during ALS progression.

**Main Findings:** Our results reveal that single motor-units are dismantled asynchronously in SOD1G37R mice, with weeks prior to complete axonal degeneration, the dismantling of axonal branches is accompanied by contemporaneous new axonal sprouting resulting in synapse formation onto nearby NMJs. **Implications:** Our findings suggest that NMJ denervation in ALS is a complex and dynamic process of continuous denervation and new innervation, rather than a manifestation of sudden global motor neuron degeneration. This temporal window between early branch-specific dismantling and global motor axon degeneration could be therapeutically exploited in ALS.

**Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the mechanisms underlying NMJ denervation in ALS, highlighting the importance of local branch-specific events in the disease progression. These findings have significant implications for the development of novel therapeutic strategies aimed at preserving NMJs and slowing disease progression in ALS patients.

Our results suggest that tamoxifen treatment modulates ERα signaling in the central nervous system to alter fundamental aspects of physiology and health. Given the known roles of ERα in mediating many aspects of physiology, these findings have important implications for understanding the side effects of tamoxifen therapy in breast cancer patients.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates the utility of mice as a model to study the physiological effects of tamoxifen and highlights the critical role of ERα in mediating these effects. These findings provide a foundation for further research into the cellular and molecular mechanisms underlying tamoxifen-induced side effects and may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies to mitigate these effects in breast cancer patients.

    **Title:** DeepFRET: An Automated, Open-Source Solution for Objective Analysis of Single-Molecule Förster Resonance Energy Transfer (smFRET) Data

    **Research Question:** Can a deep learning-based approach be developed to automate the analysis of single-molecule Förster Resonance Energy Transfer (smFRET) data, enabling rapid, objective, and accurate quantification of biomolecular dynamics?

    **Methods:** We developed DeepFRET, an open-source, standalone software that utilizes a pre-trained deep neural network (DNN) to analyze smFRET data. The software operates with minimal human intervention, requiring only a user-adjustable quality threshold to output detailed quantification of structural dynamics from raw images.

    **Main Findings:** Our results demonstrate that DeepFRET achieves >95% classification accuracy on ground truth data, outperforming human operators and commonly used threshold methods, while requiring ~1% of the time. The software's precision and speed were further validated on real data, showcasing its capacity to objectively quantify biomolecular dynamics.

    **Implications:** The development of DeepFRET represents a paradigm shift in smFRET analysis, enabling fully automated methodologies for biomolecular recognition, protein folding, and dynamics. This advance has the potential to increase the breadth and impact of single-molecule studies in structural biology.

    **Conclusions:** DeepFRET offers a rapid, objective, and accurate assessment of smFRET data, making it an invaluable tool for the scientific community. Its open-source nature and user-friendly interface enable end-users with limited programming skills to easily operate the software. The software's ability to recognize abstract patterns and learn useful features directly from raw input data eliminates the need for extensive data preprocessing or empirically defined rules, ensuring reproducible and less opinionated evaluation of single-molecule data. DeepFRET's potential to benchmark the reproducibility of smFRET data acquisition methods across laboratories highlights its significance in advancing the field of dynamic structural biology.

    **Title:** Unveiling the Molecular Mechanism of SecM-Dependent Translational Stalling in Escherichia coli

    **Research Question:** How does the C-terminal arrest sequence of SecM regulate the SecA-dependent secretion pathway in Escherichia coli by stalling ribosomes at distinct elongation steps of the translation cycle?

    **Methods:** We employed an in vitro translation system from E. coli to purify SecM-stalled ribosome nascent chain complexes (RNCs) and analyzed their structures using single-particle cryo-EM at 3.3-3.7 Å resolution.

    **Main Findings:** Our cryo-EM structures of SecM-stalled ribosomes reveal two distinct stalling mechanisms at different elongation steps of the translation cycle. The first mechanism involves the inactivation of the ribosomal peptidyl-transferase center (PTC), which inhibits peptide bond formation with the incoming prolyl-tRNA. The second mechanism involves the prolonged residence of the peptidyl-RNA at the hybrid A/P site, which prevents full-scale tRNA translocation. These findings demonstrate that SecM-dependent translational stalling is achieved through a dynamic reshaping of the ribosome's functional center.

    **Implications:** Our results provide new insights into the molecular mechanism of SecM-dependent translational stalling, shedding light on how regulatory peptides control the translation cycle. The identification of two distinct stalling mechanisms highlights the complexity of ribosome regulation and underscores the importance of ribosome- SecM interactions in modulating translation.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals the atomic-level details of SecM-dependent translational stalling, providing a framework for understanding how SecM regulates the SecA-dependent secretion pathway in E. coli. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of ribosome regulation and translation control, and may inform the development of novel therapeutic strategies targeting bacterial translation.
Title: Foveal Cone Density as a Function of Axial Length in Living Eyes: Implications for Visual Acuity in Myopes and Emmetropes

    Abstract:
    We investigated the relationship between foveal cone density and axial length in 28 eyes of 16 subjects using a novel Adaptive Optics Scanning Laser Ophthalmoscope. Our results show that foveal cone density increases with axial length, but not in proportion to eye length. Notably, myopes exhibit a higher angular sampling density in and around the fovea compared to emmetropes, despite retinal stretching. This finding contradicts previous reports of deficits in best-corrected foveal vision in myopes, suggesting that increased spacing between photoreceptors caused by retinal stretching is not the primary cause of these deficits. Our study provides new insights into the physical and visual implications of foveal cone density as a function of axial length, with potential implications for understanding visual acuity in myopes and emmetropes.

    Methods:
    We used a new generation Adaptive Optics Scanning Laser Ophthalmoscope to image cones at and near the fovea in 28 eyes of 16 subjects. Cone density and other metrics were computed in units of visual angle and linear retinal units.

    Main Findings:
    Our results show that foveal cone density increases with axial length, but not in proportion to eye length. Myopes exhibit a higher angular sampling density in and around the fovea compared to emmetropes, despite retinal stretching.

    Implications:
    Our findings suggest that increased spacing between photoreceptors caused by retinal stretching is not the primary cause of deficits in best-corrected foveal vision in myopes. Instead, myopes may have a higher angular sampling density in and around the fovea, potentially leading to better visual acuity.

    Conclusions:
    Our study provides new insights into the physical and visual implications of foveal cone density as a function of axial length, with potential implications for understanding visual acuity in myopes and emmetropes.     **Title:** Quantifying the Genetic Determinants of Streptococcus pneumoniae Carriage Duration in Infants

    **Background:** Streptococcus pneumoniae is a leading cause of invasive disease in infants, particularly in low-income settings. Asymptomatic carriage in the nasopharynx is a prerequisite for disease, but the variability in its duration is currently only understood at the serotype level.

    **Research Question:** What are the genetic determinants of Streptococcus pneumoniae carriage duration in infants?

    **Methods:** We developed a model to calculate the duration of carriage episodes from longitudinal swab data and combined these results with whole genome sequence data. We estimated the heritability of pneumococcal genomic variation and partitioned it into lineage and locus effects.

    **Main Findings:** Our analysis revealed that pneumococcal genomic variation accounted for 63% of the phenotype variation, while host traits (age and previous carriage) accounted for less than 5%. We further quantified the amount attributable to the largest sources of variation in carriage duration: serotype (17%), drug-resistance (9%), and other significant locus effects (7%). A pan-genome-wide association study identified prophage sequences as being associated with decreased carriage duration independent of serotype, potentially by disruption of the competence mechanism.

    **Implications:** Our findings support theoretical models of pneumococcal competition and antibiotic resistance. The identification of prophage sequences as a significant factor in carriage duration has interesting corollaries on pneumococcal genome diversification through frequent infection and loss of prophage, even during carriage episodes.

    **Conclusions:** This study provides a quantitative estimate of the genetic determinants of Streptococcus pneumoniae carriage duration in infants. Our results suggest that variants other than serotype affect carriage duration, consistent with recent theoretical work. We have identified candidates for the loci that fulfil this role and provide a framework for understanding the genetic contribution to pneumococcal carriage duration. These findings have important implications for the development of strategies to prevent and control pneumococcal disease, particularly in low-income settings.
Title: Elucidating the Stoichiometry of the KCNQ1-KCNE1 Complex: Implications for Cardiac Repolarization

    Abstract:
    The slow delayed rectifier current (IKs) plays a crucial role in stabilizing repolarization and shortening action potential duration in the heart. The tetrameric voltage-gated ion channel, KCNQ1, and its β-subunit, KCNE1, are key components of this current. However, the stoichiometry between α and β-subunits has been a subject of controversy, with studies reporting either a strict 2:4 or a variable ratio up to 4:4. To address this issue, we used IKs fusion proteins linking KCNE1 to one, two, or four KCNQ1 subunits to reproduce 4:4, 2:4, or 1:4 stoichiometries. Our whole-cell and single-channel recordings revealed that the 2:4 and 4:4 stoichiometries exhibited increasingly hyperpolarized activation, reduced conductance, and shorter first latency of opening compared to the 1:4 stoichiometry. Notably, these effects were abolished by the addition of KCNE1. Furthermore, using a UV-crosslinking unnatural amino acid in KCNE1, we found that the crosslinking rates of the 2:4 and 4:4 stoichiometries were progressively slowed compared to KCNQ1, indicating that no intrinsic mechanism limits the association of up to four β-subunits within the IKs complex. These findings suggest that flexibility in KCNQ1’s β-subunits association allows for a powerful mechanism to modulate repolarization in the heart through changes in expression. Our results have significant implications for understanding the regulation of IKs and its role in cardiac function and disease.

    Research Question:
    What is the stoichiometry of the KCNQ1-KCNE1 complex, and how does it affect the properties of the slow delayed rectifier current (IKs)?

    Methods:
    We used IKs fusion proteins linking KCNE1 to one, two, or four KCNQ1 subunits to reproduce 4:4, 2:4, or 1:4 stoichiometries. Whole-cell and single-channel recordings were performed in mammalian cells, and UV-crosslinking unnatural amino acid approaches were used to study the association of KCNE1 with KCNQ1.

    Main Findings:
    Our results show that the 2:4 and 4:4 stoichiometries exhibit increasingly hyperpolarized activation, reduced conductance, and shorter first latency of opening compared to the 1:4 stoichiometry. These effects were abolished by the addition of KCNE1. Furthermore, the crosslinking rates of the 2:4 and 4:4 stoichiometries were progressively slowed compared to KCNQ1, indicating that no intrinsic mechanism limits the association of up to four β-subunits within the IKs complex.

    Implications:
    Our findings suggest that flexibility in KCNQ1’s β-subunits association allows for a powerful mechanism to modulate repolarization in the heart through changes in expression. This has significant implications for understanding the regulation of IKs and its role in cardiac function and disease.

    Conclusions:
    Our study provides new insights into the stoichiometry of the KCNQ1-KCNE1 complex and its effects on the properties of IKs. The flexibility in KCNQ1’s β-subunits association allows for a powerful mechanism to modulate repolarization in the heart, which has significant implications for understanding the regulation of IKs and its role in cardiac function and disease.

    **Title:** Protein Mobility in Prokaryotic Cytoplasm: The Role of Surface Properties and Ionic Strength

    **Research Question:** How do protein surface properties and ionic strength influence protein mobility in the cytoplasm of prokaryotic cells, and what are the implications for cellular processes?

    **Methods:** We used surface-modified fluorescent proteins (FPs) to investigate the translational diffusion coefficients (D) of proteins in the cytoplasm of Escherichia coli, Lactococcus lactis, and Haloferax volcanii. We analyzed the effects of protein surface charge and ionic strength on protein mobility.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that protein mobility in E. coli depends on the net charge and its distribution over the protein surface, with positive proteins diffusing up to 100-fold slower than negative ones. This effect is weaker in L. lactis and Hfx. volcanii due to electrostatic screening. We also found that the decrease in mobility is primarily caused by the interaction of positive FPs with ribosomes, as confirmed by in vivo diffusion measurements and in vitro experiments with purified ribosomes.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that ribosome surface properties may limit the composition of the cytoplasmic proteome. The binding of positive proteins to ribosomes may inhibit protein synthesis by affecting the assembly or activity of ribosomes. This has significant implications for our understanding of prokaryotic cell biology, particularly in the context of endosymbiotic relationships.

    **Conclusions:** Our study highlights the importance of considering protein surface properties and ionic strength in understanding protein mobility in prokaryotic cytoplasm. The findings have significant implications for our understanding of cellular processes, including protein synthesis, and may shed light on the functioning of prokaryotic endosymbionts. Further research is needed to fully elucidate the consequences of our findings and to explore the broader implications for cellular biology.

    Abstract:
    Mismatch repair (MMR)-deficient tumors exhibit a distinct molecular fingerprint characterized by persistent DNA replication errors, which confer resistance to standard therapy. We employed whole-genome and whole-exome sequencing to investigate the mutation spectra in MMR-deficient tumors and their clinical relevance. Our results reveal a unique mutation signature in MMR-deficient tumors, surprisingly similar to germ-line DNA, indicating that a substantial fraction of human genetic variation arises from mutations escaping MMR. Consequently, MMR-deficient tumor cultures exhibit a dose-dependent sensitivity to DSB inducers.     Main Findings:

We identified a large set of recurrent indels that may serve as markers for MSI and optimized a novel marker set capable of detecting MSI with greater specificity and selectivity than standard MSI tests. Recurrent indels are enriched for the DNA double-strand break repair by homologous recombination pathway, leading to reduced DSB repair in MMR-deficient tumors.

    Implications:

    Title: Enhanced Magnets: Overcoming Limitations of Light-Inducible Dimerization Protein Modules for Precise Temporal and Spatial Control of Biological Processes

    Background: Light-inducible dimerization protein modules, such as Magnets, have revolutionized the field of optogenetics by enabling precise temporal and spatial control of biological processes in a non-invasive fashion.     Research Question: Can we engineer an optimized Magnets pair that requires neither concatemerization nor low-temperature preincubation for efficient responses?

    Methods: We employed a robust screen for reconstitution of Magnets dimerization using light-dependent accumulation of a protein at the outer mitochondrial membrane.     Implications: eMags represent a highly effective tool for optogenetically manipulating physiological processes over whole cells or in small subcellular volumes, offering major advantages over other systems in several different assays.     Conclusions: We have overcome the limitations of Magnets by engineering an optimized pair that requires neither concatemerization nor low-temperature preincubation for efficient responses. eMags are a valuable addition to the toolbox of optogenetic reagents, enabling precise temporal and spatial control of biological processes in a non-invasive fashion.

    **Title:** Ribosome Stalling Activates the Integrated Stress Response to Attenuate Neurodegeneration in Mammalian Neurons

    **Research Question:** What are the signaling pathways triggered by stalled elongation complexes in mammalian neurons, and how do they contribute to neurodegeneration?

    **Methods:** We used C57BL/6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice, in which neuronal elongation complexes are stalled at AGA codons due to deficiencies in a tRNAArgUCU tRNA and GTPBP2, a mammalian ribosome rescue factor.     **Implications:** Our results demonstrate the critical interplay between translation elongation and initiation in regulating neuron survival during cellular stress.     **Conclusions:** Our study reveals a novel feedback loop between elongation defects, particularly ribosome stalling, and translation initiation. These findings have important implications for understanding the mechanisms of neurodegeneration and the development of therapeutic strategies to prevent or treat neurodegenerative diseases.

    **Title:** Global Variations in Adult Height Over the Past Century: A Meta-Analysis of 18.6 Million Participants

    **Research Question:** How has adult height changed over the past century across different countries and populations?

    **Methods:** We conducted a meta-analysis of 1472 population-based studies, involving over 18.6 million participants, to estimate mean height for individuals born between 1896 and 1996 in 200 countries.

    **Main Findings:** Our analysis revealed substantial and uneven changes in adult height across countries over the past century. The largest gains in adult height were observed in South Korean women and Iranian men, who became 20.2 cm (95% CI: 17.5-22.7) and 16.5 cm (95% CI: 13.3-19.7) taller, respectively. In contrast, there was little change in adult height in some sub-Saharan African countries and South Asia. The tallest populations over the past century were men born in the Netherlands in the last quarter of the 20th century, with an average height of 182.5 cm, while the shortest were women born in Guatemala in 1896, with an average height of 140.3 cm (95% CI: 135.8-144.8).

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that the height differential between the tallest and shortest populations has remained relatively stable over the past century, with a gap of approximately 19-20 cm for women and 22-23 cm for men. However, the ranking of countries in terms of adult height has changed substantially, with some countries experiencing significant increases in height while others have seen little change.

    **Conclusions:** This study provides a comprehensive overview of global variations in adult height over the past century. Our results highlight the importance of considering the complex interplay between genetic, environmental, and socio-economic factors that influence human growth and development. The findings have implications for our understanding of population health, nutrition, and economic development, and underscore the need for continued research into the determinants of adult height and its relationship to health outcomes.

    **Title:** Sepsis-Induced Changes in Memory CD8 T Cells: Long-Term Alterations in Phenotype, Function, and Localization

    **Research Question:** What are the long-term effects of sepsis on memory CD8 T cells, and how do these changes impact the host's ability to respond to re-infection?

    **Methods:** We investigated the effects of sepsis on memory CD8 T cells in both patients and mice using a cecal ligation and puncture (CLP) model. We analyzed the proliferation, composition, and function of memory CD8 T cells in septic patients and mice, as well as their ability to control Listeria monocytogenes infection.

    **Main Findings:** We observed that central memory CD8 T cells (TCM) from septic patients and mice proliferate more than those from healthy individuals. This increased proliferation leads to changes in the composition of the memory CD8 T cell compartment and altered tissue localization. Furthermore, memory CD8 T cells from sepsis survivors have an altered transcriptional profile and chromatin accessibility, indicating long-lasting T cell intrinsic changes. These changes result in altered T cell function and reduced capacity to control L. monocytogenes infection.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that sepsis leads to long-term alterations in memory CD8 T cell phenotype, protective function, and localization, potentially changing the host's capacity to respond to re-infection. These changes may predispose individuals to re-infection and compromise their ability to mount an effective immune response.

    **Conclusions:** This study provides new insights into the long-term effects of sepsis on memory CD8 T cells and highlights the need for further research into the mechanisms underlying these changes. Understanding the impact of sepsis on memory CD8 T cells may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies to improve immune function and reduce the risk of re-infection in sepsis survivors.
Title: Elucidating the Mechanism of APOBEC3A-Mediated Inhibition of Long INterspersed Element-1 (LINE-1) Retrotransposition

    Abstract:
    Long INterspersed Element-1 (LINE-1 or L1) retrotransposition poses a significant mutagenic threat to human genomes. To counteract this threat, human cells have evolved strategies to regulate L1 retrotransposition, including the APOBEC3 (A3) gene family. Among these enzymes, APOBEC3A (A3A) is the most potent inhibitor of L1 retrotransposition in cultured cell assays. However, the molecular mechanism by which A3A inhibits L1 retrotransposition has remained enigmatic. Here, we employed in vitro and in vivo assays to elucidate the mechanism of A3A-mediated inhibition of L1 retrotransposition. Our results demonstrate that A3A can inhibit L1 retrotransposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA that arises during the process of L1 integration. Furthermore, we show that engineered L1 retrotransposition events derived from cultured human cells co-expressing A3A and the uracil DNA glycosylase inhibitor protein (UGI) contain strand-specific, A3A-mediated C-to-U deamination events. These findings provide a mechanistic explanation for how the A3A cytidine deaminase protein can inhibit L1 retrotransposition.

    Research Question: What is the molecular mechanism by which APOBEC3A (A3A) inhibits Long INterspersed Element-1 (LINE-1) retrotransposition?

    Methods: We employed in vitro and in vivo assays, including the L1 Element Amplification Protocol (LEAP), to investigate the mechanism of A3A-mediated inhibition of L1 retrotransposition.

    Main Findings: Our results demonstrate that A3A can inhibit L1 retrotransposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA that arises during the process of L1 integration. We also show that engineered L1 retrotransposition events derived from cultured human cells co-expressing A3A and UGI contain strand-specific, A3A-mediated C-to-U deamination events.

    Implications: These findings provide a mechanistic explanation for how the A3A cytidine deaminase protein can inhibit L1 retrotransposition, shedding light on the molecular mechanism of A3A-mediated inhibition of L1 retrotransposition.

    Conclusions: Our results demonstrate that A3A can inhibit L1 retrotransposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA that arises during the process of L1 integration. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying A3A-mediated inhibition of L1 retrotransposition and highlight the importance of A3A in regulating L1 retrotransposition in human cells.

    **Title:** Rem2 Regulates Experience-Dependent Plasticity in the Visual Cortex

    **Research Question:** What is the role of the activity-regulated, non-canonical Ras-like GTPase Rem2 in visual circuit plasticity?

    **Methods:** We investigated the function of Rem2 in visual circuit plasticity using Rem2 knockout (Rem2-/-) mice. We examined ocular dominance (OD) plasticity during the critical period and in adulthood, as well as the intrinsic excitability of layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons in vitro and in vivo.

    **Main Findings:** Our results demonstrate that Rem2-/- mice fail to exhibit normal OD plasticity during the critical period. At the cellular level, we found that Rem2 regulates intrinsic excitability of layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons, prior to changes in synaptic function. In vitro and in vivo recordings revealed increased spontaneous firing rates in the absence of Rem2. These findings indicate that Rem2 is a key molecule that regulates neuronal excitability and circuit function in response to changing sensory experience.

    **Implications:** Our data suggest that Rem2 functions at the nexus of a signaling network that senses and responds to changes in neuronal activity, preserving proper circuit function in the face of changing sensory experience. These results raise the possibility that Rem2 may play an important role in regulating the absolute responsiveness of the cortex to visual stimulation, such as through regulation of neuronal excitability or synaptic scaling.

    **Conclusions:** Our findings provide an important step forward in connecting molecular regulators of neuronal morphology with broader circuit function. Given that Rem2 expression is acutely regulated by visual experience, we propose that Rem2 plays a role in activity-dependent processes in the visual cortex following sensory deprivation. Further investigation of Rem2-dependent signaling mechanisms will elucidate the molecular mechanisms that instruct activity-dependent modifications of neural circuitry. Overall, our study highlights the critical role of Rem2 in regulating experience-dependent plasticity in the visual cortex.
Here is a 300-400 word abstract for the given biology research article:

    **Title:** Magnetic Fields and Biological Molecules: A Critical Reevaluation of Magnetogenetics and Magnetosensation

    **Research Question:** Can magnetic fields be used to control biological molecules and cells, as proposed by recent studies on magnetogenetics and magnetosensation?

    **Methods:** A critical analysis of existing literature on magnetogenetics and magnetosensation, focusing on the biophysical mechanisms proposed by Qin et al. (2016), Stanley et al. (2015), and Wheeler et al. (2016).

    **Main Findings:** Our analysis reveals significant discrepancies between the reported effects of magnetic fields on biological molecules and cells and the basic laws of physics. Specifically, the forces, torques, and temperatures produced by paramagnetic protein complexes are too small by many orders of magnitude to achieve the desired effects on molecular orientation or membrane channels.

    **Implications:** If the reported phenomena do occur, they must rely on entirely different mechanisms than those proposed by the authors. The paramagnetic nature of protein complexes severely limits their utility for engineering magnetically sensitive cells. Furthermore, the proposed routes to magnetogenetics, based on either pulling or heating a ferritin/channel complex with magnetic fields, have no chance of success without dramatic new discoveries about the structure of biological matter.

    **Conclusions:** Our reevaluation of magnetogenetics and magnetosensation highlights the need for a more nuanced understanding of the biophysical mechanisms underlying these phenomena. While the idea of controlling biological molecules and cells with magnetic fields is intriguing, the current evidence does not support the proposed mechanisms. Future research should focus on exploring alternative explanations for magnetosensation and magnetogenetics, such as the interaction of magnetic fields with single molecules in biochemical reactions (Ritz et al., 2010). Ultimately, a deeper understanding of the biophysical basis for magnetosensation and magnetogenetics is essential for the development of novel therapeutic and technological applications.

    **Title:** Prospero Regulates Quiescence in Drosophila Neural Progenitors

    **Research Question:** How do Drosophila neural progenitors (neuroblasts) switch between self-renewal, differentiation, and quiescence, and what is the role of the differentiation factor Prospero in this process?

    **Methods:** We used Drosophila neuroblasts as a model system to investigate the molecular mechanisms underlying quiescence. We analyzed the expression of Prospero, a known differentiation factor, in relation to neuroblast quiescence and proliferation.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that Prospero is transiently detected at low levels in the neuroblast nucleus just prior to entry into quiescence. We found that loss of Prospero prevents entry into quiescence, whereas a pulse of low-level nuclear Prospero drives proliferating larval neuroblasts into quiescence. Furthermore, we observed that nearly all quiescent neuroblasts reactivated proliferation and re-expressed progenitor markers after 24 hours. We also found that precocious low-level nuclear Prospero can advance the timing of neuroblast quiescence.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that Prospero levels distinguish three progenitor fates: absent for self-renewal, low for quiescence, and high for differentiation. This study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying quiescence in Drosophila neural progenitors and highlights the importance of Prospero in regulating this process.

    **Conclusions:** Our results demonstrate that transient, low-level nuclear Prospero is sufficient to induce neuroblast quiescence and that Prospero is required for neuroblast entry into quiescence. We propose that the pulse of low-level nuclear Prospero marks neuroblast entry into quiescence, suggesting a functional relationship between Prospero and quiescence. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of stem cell biology and the development of stem cell-based therapeutics.

    Title: Dissecting the Uncoating Kinetics of HIV-1 Capsids: A Single-Particle Fluorescence Microscopy Study

    Background: The uncoating of the HIV-1 capsid is a critical step in the viral life cycle, allowing for the release of the viral cDNA prior to nuclear import. However, the intrinsic disassembly pathway of the capsid and its regulation by capsid-binding molecules remain poorly understood.

    Research Question: What are the discrete steps involved in the uncoating of HIV-1 capsids, and how do capsid-binding molecules modulate this process?

    Methods: We developed a single-particle fluorescence microscopy method to follow the real-time uncoating kinetics of authentic HIV capsids in vitro immediately after permeabilizing the viral membrane. This approach allowed us to observe the effects of capsid-binding molecules on discrete steps in the capsid uncoating process.

    Main Findings: Our results show that the opening of the first defect in the lattice is the rate-limiting step of uncoating, followed by rapid and catastrophic collapse. The capsid-binding inhibitor PF74 accelerates capsid opening but stabilizes the remaining lattice, while binding of a polyanion to a conserved arginine cluster in the lattice strongly delays initiation of uncoating but does not prevent subsequent lattice disassembly.

    Implications: Our findings suggest that different stages of uncoating can be controlled independently, and the interplay between different capsid-binding regulators likely determines the overall uncoating kinetics. This has important implications for the development of antiviral therapies targeting the HIV-1 capsid.

    Conclusions: Our single-particle fluorescence microscopy study provides new insights into the uncoating kinetics of HIV-1 capsids and the effects of capsid-binding molecules on this process. By resolving intermediates in the disassembly pathway that are otherwise averaged out in traditional ensemble assays, our approach has allowed us to classify HIV-1 capsids according to their uncoating behaviors. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of the HIV-1 life cycle and the development of novel antiviral strategies.
