    **Title:** Unexpected Role of JNK Signaling in Breast Cancer Development: Loss-of-Function Mutations Promote Tumor Initiation

    **Research Question:** Do loss-of-function mutations in the cJUN NH2-terminal kinase (JNK) signaling pathway contribute to breast cancer development?

    **Methods:** We investigated the effect of JNK deficiency in the murine breast epithelium using a mouse model of breast cancer. We examined the impact of JNK signaling on genomic stability, tumor initiation, and progression.

    **Main Findings:** Contrary to our initial hypothesis, we found that loss of JNK signaling in mammary epithelial cells led to the development of breast cancer in mice. Furthermore, JNK deficiency accelerated tumor formation in a murine model of breast cancer, associated with widespread early neoplasia and genomic instability. Our data indicate that JNK plays a crucial role in preventing tumor initiation, rather than promoting tumor growth.

    **Implications:** These findings suggest that loss-of-function mutations in the JNK pathway may be "driver" mutations that promote genome instability and tumor initiation in breast cancer. The frequent occurrence of JNK pathway loss-of-function mutations in human breast cancer may contribute to the development of this malignancy.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals an unexpected role of JNK signaling in breast cancer development. We demonstrate that JNK deficiency promotes murine breast cancer development and accelerates tumor formation in a mouse model of breast cancer. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying breast cancer and highlight the importance of JNK signaling in preventing tumor initiation. Further research is needed to fully elucidate the role of JNK pathway mutations in breast cancer and to explore potential therapeutic strategies targeting this pathway.
Title: Elucidating the Role of Peptidylglycine α-Amidating Monooxygenase (PAM) in Cilium Biogenesis across Eukaryotic Cell Types

    Abstract:
    Cilium biogenesis, the process by which cilia form and mature, remains poorly understood in various cell types. Recent studies have implicated peptidylglycine α-amidating monooxygenase (PAM), an enzyme involved in generating amidated bioactive signaling peptides, in the assembly of cilia in Chlamydomonas and mammalian cells. Here, we investigate the role of PAM in ciliogenesis across multiple eukaryotic cell types, including Chlamydomonas, planaria, and mice. Our results demonstrate that PAM is essential for the normal assembly of motile and primary cilia in these organisms. Knockdown of PAM in Chlamydomonas resulted in cilia assembly defects beyond the transition zone, abnormal Golgi architecture, and altered levels of cilia assembly components. Similarly, decreased PAM gene expression in planaria reduced motile ciliary density and led to the formation of cytosolic axonemes lacking a ciliary membrane. In mice, PAM deficiency resulted in aberrant primary cilia architecture on neuroepithelial cells. Our findings suggest that PAM activity and post-Golgi trafficking contribute to ciliogenesis defects and reveal a highly conserved link between PAM, amidation, and ciliary assembly across eukaryotic cell types. These results highlight the importance of PAM in ciliogenesis and provide new insights into the evolutionarily conserved mechanisms underlying cilium biogenesis.

    Research Question:
    What is the role of peptidylglycine α-amidating monooxygenase (PAM) in ciliogenesis across multiple eukaryotic cell types?

    Methods:
    We used a combination of molecular biology, cell biology, and microscopy techniques to investigate the role of PAM in ciliogenesis in Chlamydomonas, planaria, and mice.

    Main Findings:
    Our results show that PAM is essential for the normal assembly of motile and primary cilia in Chlamydomonas, planaria, and mice. PAM knockdown or deficiency resulted in cilia assembly defects, abnormal Golgi architecture, and altered levels of cilia assembly components.

    Implications:
    Our findings suggest that PAM activity and post-Golgi trafficking contribute to ciliogenesis defects and reveal a highly conserved link between PAM, amidation, and ciliary assembly across eukaryotic cell types. These results highlight the importance of PAM in ciliogenesis and provide new insights into the evolutionarily conserved mechanisms underlying cilium biogenesis.

    Conclusions:
    Our study demonstrates that PAM is a crucial, evolutionarily conserved factor required for ciliogenesis in multiple cell types. The conserved role of PAM in ciliogenesis across eukaryotic cell types suggests that PAM may have a novel and highly conserved signaling or sensory function in eukaryotic cilia.

    **Title:** Unveiling the Mechanism of Myo3-Mediated Cross-Linking of Espin1 in Stereocilia Development and Maintenance

    **Research Question:** How does the Class III myosin Myo3 interact with the actin-bundling protein Espin1 to regulate the development and maintenance of stereocilia in vertebrate hair cells?

    **Methods:** We employed X-ray crystallography and biochemical assays to elucidate the molecular basis of the Myo3-Espin1 interaction and its role in actin bundle assembly and stabilization.

    **Main Findings:** We discovered that the tail homology I motif (THDI) of Myo3 contains two repeat sequences capable of independently binding to the ankyrin repeats of Espin1 with high affinity. The structures of Myo3 in complex with Espin1 reveal a Myo3-mediated cross-linking mechanism that promotes actin fiber bundling activity of Espin1. Furthermore, we found that Myo3 binding can release the auto-inhibition of Espin1, leading to an increase in the diameter of Espin1-promoted actin bundles.

    **Implications:** Our findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying the development and maintenance of stereocilia in vertebrate hair cells. The Myo3-Espin1 interaction is crucial for promoting the formation of thicker parallel actin bundle fibers in cellular structures such as stereocilia. Defects in this interaction have been linked to hereditary hearing impairments.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals a previously unrecognized direct myosin binding-induced actin bundling activity regulation mechanism of an actin filament cross-linker protein. The Myo3-Espin1 interaction is essential for the proper development and maintenance of stereocilia, and its dysregulation can lead to hearing impairments. Our findings have significant implications for understanding the molecular basis of hearing loss and may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies for treating hearing-related disorders.
Title: Elucidating the Molecular Mechanisms of Voltage Sensing in Eukaryotic Calcium Channels: A Structural and Functional Analysis of CaV1.1 Voltage-Sensing Domains

    Abstract:
    Voltage-gated calcium channels (CaVs) are essential for various cellular functions, including synaptic transmission, muscle contraction, and hormone secretion. To understand the molecular mechanisms governing their voltage-sensing properties, we employed a multidisciplinary approach combining structure modeling, mutagenesis, and electrophysiology to analyze the structures, free energy, and transition kinetics of the activated and resting states of two functionally distinct voltage-sensing domains (VSDs) of the eukaryotic calcium channel CaV1.1. Our results reveal that both VSDs exhibit the characteristic features of the sliding helix model, but differ significantly in ion-pair formation of the outer gating charges. Specifically, stabilization of the activated state enhances the voltage dependence of activation, while stabilization of resting states slows the kinetics. These findings provide a mechanistic model explaining how specific ion-pair formation in separate VSDs can realize the characteristic gating properties of voltage-gated cation channels.

    Research Question:
    What are the molecular mechanisms governing the specific voltage-sensing properties of eukaryotic calcium channels?

    Methods:
    We used a combination of structure modeling, mutagenesis, and electrophysiology to analyze the structures, free energy, and transition kinetics of the activated and resting states of two functionally distinct VSDs of the eukaryotic calcium channel CaV1.1.

    Main Findings:
    Our results show that both VSDs exhibit the characteristic features of the sliding helix model, but differ significantly in ion-pair formation of the outer gating charges. Stabilization of the activated state enhances the voltage dependence of activation, while stabilization of resting states slows the kinetics.

    Implications:
    These findings provide a mechanistic model explaining how specific ion-pair formation in separate VSDs can realize the characteristic gating properties of voltage-gated cation channels. This understanding has important implications for the development of novel therapeutic strategies targeting voltage-gated calcium channels in various diseases, including cardiovascular and neurological disorders.

    Conclusions:
    Our study fills an important gap in the understanding of the voltage-sensing mechanism in eukaryotic voltage-gated cation channels. The structural and functional analysis of CaV1.1 VSDs provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms governing the specific voltage-sensing properties of these channels, and highlights the importance of ion-pair formation in determining the characteristic gating properties of voltage-gated cation channels.

    **Title:** The Role of Matrix Metalloproteinases in Adult Visual Plasticity: A Critical Window for Recovery

    **Research Question:** Do matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) play a crucial role in adult visual plasticity, and can inhibition of their upregulation following cortical stroke rescue lost plasticity?

    **Methods:** We investigated the effects of MMP-inhibition on experience-induced plasticity in healthy and stroke-affected adult mice using a combination of in vivo optical imaging and behavioral vision tests.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that 7-day application of MMP-inhibitor prevented visual plasticity in healthy animals. However, treatment with MMP-inhibitor once, but not twice, following stroke rescued plasticity, which is normally lost under these conditions. These findings suggest that an optimal level of MMP-activity is essential for adult visual plasticity to occur.

    **Implications:** Our data highlight a critical role of MMPs in adult visual plasticity and suggest that upregulation of MMP-activity shortly after a cortical lesion compromises experience-induced visual plasticity, which can be rescued by brief MMP-inhibition. This implies that precise regulation of MMP-activity is essential for facilitating plasticity in the adult brain.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates a vital role of MMPs for adult visual cortex plasticity, particularly for the increase of open eye responses in V1 after monocular deprivation (MD). These findings expand previous studies from juvenile rats and suggest that MMP-activity has to be within a narrow window for experience-induced plasticity to occur. The results also provide new opportunities for treatment and recovery after stroke, as a single treatment with MMP-inhibitor after the stroke rescued plasticity in both paradigms. Overall, our study highlights the importance of MMPs in adult visual plasticity and the need for precise regulation of their activity to facilitate recovery after brain injury.
Here is a 300-400 word abstract for the given biology research article:

    **Title:** Conformational Dynamics of AMPA Receptors at Vertebrate Excitatory Synapses

    **Research Question:** What are the conformational dynamics of AMPA receptors at vertebrate excitatory synapses, particularly in the presence and absence of auxiliary subunits?

    **Methods:** We employed bifunctional methanethiosulfonate cross-linkers to calibrate the conformations of functional AMPA receptors in the presence and absence of the auxiliary subunit Stargazin. Cryo-electron microscopy structures of AMPA receptors in complex with auxiliary subunits were also analyzed.

    **Main Findings:** Our data indicate that the extracellular layer of AMPA receptors can get trapped in stable, opened-up conformations, especially upon long exposures to glutamate. However, the presence of Stargazin limits this conformational flexibility. In contrast, under synaptic conditions, where brief glutamate exposures and the presence of auxiliary proteins dominate, the extracellular domains of AMPA receptors likely remain compact during gating.

    **Implications:** These findings suggest that the extracellular domains of synaptic AMPA receptors are unlikely to undergo large structural rearrangements during synaptic transmission and instead work in a fairly compact conformational regime, unless faced with long exposures to glutamate in pathological conditions.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the conformational dynamics of AMPA receptors at vertebrate excitatory synapses. The presence of auxiliary subunits, such as Stargazin, plays a crucial role in regulating the conformational flexibility of AMPA receptors. These findings have important implications for our understanding of synaptic transmission and the development of novel therapeutic strategies for neurological disorders associated with AMPA receptor dysfunction.

    **Significance:** This study contributes to the growing body of research on the structural biology of AMPA receptors and highlights the importance of auxiliary subunits in regulating their conformational dynamics. The findings have significant implications for our understanding of synaptic transmission and the development of novel therapeutic strategies for neurological disorders associated with AMPA receptor dysfunction.

    **Title:** Systematic Optimization of Intestinal Digestion Protocol for Single-Cell Isolation from Helminth-Infected Murine Intestines

    **Research Question:** Can a standardized intestinal digestion protocol be optimized to facilitate the isolation of millions of immune cells from heavily infected murine duodena, and does this protocol provide an accurate representation of intestinal immune responses during helminth infection?

    **Methods:** We employed a systematic optimization of a standard intestinal digestion protocol to isolate immune cells from the duodena of mice infected with helminths. The isolated cells were then analyzed using a 23-color spectral flow cytometry panel and validated by confocal microscopy.

    **Main Findings:** Our optimized protocol enabled the isolation of millions of immune cells from the heavily infected duodena. High-dimensional spectral flow cytometry analysis revealed many known hallmarks of anti-parasite immune responses throughout the entire course of helminth infection, including changes in B220+, Siglec F+, CD3+ CD4+, CD3+ CD4-, and CD64+ cell populations. These findings were corroborated by confocal microscopy, demonstrating that our protocol provides an accurate representation of intestinal immune responses.

    **Implications:** Our optimized cell isolation protocol and high-dimensional analysis have the potential to accelerate single-cell discoveries of local helminth immune responses, which have previously been unfeasible due to the strong anti-parasite type 2 immune responses that drive mucus production, tissue remodeling, and immune cell infiltration in the intestines.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates the feasibility of isolating millions of immune cells from heavily infected murine intestines using a systematically optimized intestinal digestion protocol. The accuracy of our protocol was validated by high-dimensional spectral flow cytometry analysis and confocal microscopy, enabling the characterization of many known hallmarks of anti-parasite immune responses throughout the entire course of helminth infection. Our findings highlight the importance of good cell isolation techniques for investigating intestinal responses against helminths directly, and our protocol has the potential to accelerate single-cell discoveries in this field.

    **Title:** Elucidating Proteasomal Peptide-Hydrolysis Dynamics: An Integrated Experimental and Modeling Approach

    **Research Question:** What are the mechanisms regulating proteasome activity, and how do they impact protein half-life and cellular processes?

    **Methods:** We employed an iterative and tightly integrated experimental and modeling approach to develop, explore, and validate mechanistic models of proteasomal peptide-hydrolysis dynamics. We used carefully designed experimental assays and detailed modeling to shed light on the regulatory components of 20S proteasomes and the complex interactions between substrate/product transport and substrate hydrolysis over time for representative peptides.

    **Main Findings:** Our analysis suggests that the rate-limiting step of hydrolysis is the transport of substrates into the proteasome. The transport efficiency varies between human standard- and immuno-proteasomes, thereby impinging upon total degradation rate and substrate cleavage-site usage. We also found that the 20S proteasome is a dynamic enzyme, and its activity varies over time due to interactions between substrates and products and the proteolytic and regulatory sites. Our model predicts the locations of these sites and the interactions between them, which are experimentally supported.

    **Implications:** The findings of this study have significant implications for our understanding of proteasome function and its role in regulating protein half-life and cellular processes. The model developed in this study provides a comprehensive framework for describing the regulatory components of 20S proteasomes and the complex interactions between substrate/product transport and substrate hydrolysis over time. This knowledge is crucial for understanding the mechanisms of proteasomal action, particularly the causes and extent of differences in substrate-specific proteolysis between different proteasome isoforms.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the mechanisms regulating proteasome activity and highlights the importance of considering the structural characteristics of the proteasome in understanding its catalytic activity. The model developed in this study can be used to predict proteasome dynamics in more complex scenarios, such as those that exist inside the cellular environment. Overall, this study contributes to a deeper understanding of proteasome function and its role in regulating protein half-life and cellular processes.
Title: Identification of a Novel β-Alanine Transporter Required for Histamine Homeostasis and Visual Synaptic Transmission in Drosophila

    Abstract:

    Background: The recycling of neurotransmitters is essential for sustained synaptic transmission, and histamine recycling is critical for visual synaptic transmission in Drosophila. However, the mechanisms underlying β-alanine recycling, a key component of histamine homeostasis, remain poorly understood.

    Research Question: Do retinal pigment cells play a critical role in visual neurotransmission, and is a β-alanine transporter required for histamine homeostasis and visual synaptic transmission in Drosophila?

    Methods: We used a combination of genetic and biochemical approaches to identify a novel β-alanine transporter, BalaT, in Drosophila. We generated balat mutants and analyzed their effects on β-alanine levels, β-alanine accumulation in the retina, and visual synaptic transmission.

    Main Findings: We found that balat mutants exhibited lower levels of β-alanine and reduced β-alanine accumulation in the retina. Moreover, BalaT is expressed and required in retinal pigment cells for maintaining visual synaptic transmission and phototaxis behavior.

    Implications: Our results provide the first genetic evidence that retinal pigment cells play a critical role in visual neurotransmission and suggest that a BalaT-dependent β-alanine trafficking pathway is required for histamine homeostasis and visual synaptic transmission.

    Conclusions: We identified a novel β-alanine transporter, BalaT, which is essential for histamine homeostasis and visual synaptic transmission in Drosophila. Our findings highlight the importance of β-alanine recycling in maintaining histamine levels and suggest a conserved function for β-alanine trafficking in mammals and Drosophila. This study provides new insights into the mechanisms underlying histamine recycling and visual synaptic transmission, and has implications for our understanding of neurotransmitter homeostasis in the nervous system.
Here is a 300-400 word abstract for the given biology research article:

    **Title:** Redox Collapse in Chronologically Aging Yeast Cells: A Novel Insight into the Mechanisms of Aging

    **Research Question:** What are the precise roles of oxidants in lifespan and aging, and how do chronologically aging yeast cells undergo a sudden redox collapse?

    **Methods:** We used Saccharomyces cerevisiae as a model system to investigate the onset, extent, and targets of protein oxidation in postmitotic aging cells. We monitored the thiol oxidation status of almost 300 different protein thiols and discovered that yeast cells undergo a global redox collapse, leading to massive thiol oxidation in almost 80% of identified proteins several days prior to cell death.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that chronologically aging yeast cells undergo a sudden redox collapse, which is not triggered by an increase in endogenous oxidants, but rather by a substantial drop in cellular NADPH levels. This decrease in NADPH levels occurs very early during lifespan and sets into motion a cascade that down-regulates most cellular processes. Caloric restriction, a near-universal lifespan-extending measure, increases NADPH levels and delays each facet of the cascade.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that chronological aging might represent a physiological stress condition that utilizes and stresses the redox buffering capacity of the thiol proteome. The observed decrease in cellular NADPH levels and the concomitant increase in cellular redox potential might be shared with other aging organisms as their metabolism changes.

    **Conclusions:** This study reveals a time line of events leading up to the system-wide oxidation of the proteome days before cell death. Our results provide new insights into the mechanisms of aging and suggest that the redox collapse in chronologically aging yeast cells is a critical event in the aging process. Further studies are needed to uncover protein targets whose oxidative thiol modifications might contribute to the physiological alterations observed in aging organisms and to establish a clear correlation between onset and extent of oxidative stress and lifespan.

    **Title:** Mechanistic Basis of D-Aminoacyl-tRNA Deacylase Reveals a Chiral Enforcement Mechanism for Homochirality in Proteins

    **Research Question:** How does the enzyme D-aminoacyl-tRNA deacylase (DTD) selectively remove D-amino acids from tRNAs, ensuring the homochirality of the biological macromolecular world?

    **Methods:** We solved the crystal structure of the dimeric DTD from Plasmodium falciparum in complex with a substrate-mimicking analog, providing insights into the mechanistic basis of its configuration-specific activity.

    **Main Findings:** Our structural analysis reveals that DTD uses an invariant Gly-cisPro dipeptide to capture the chiral centre of incoming D-aminoacyl-tRNA, enabling the enzyme to selectively recognize and remove D-amino acids from tRNAs. Notably, this side chain-independent mode of substrate recognition allows DTD to act on multiple D-amino acids. The strict chiral specificity of DTD ensures that the cellular pool of L-aminoacyl-tRNAs is enriched, preventing the incorporation of D-amino acids into proteins.

    **Implications:** Our study provides a fundamental understanding of the enantioselective mechanism involved in the enforcement of homochirality in proteins. The unique recognition mechanism of DTD highlights the crucial role of weak hydrogen bonds in D-chirality selection, providing a basis for how nature has designed a single deacylase to deal with any D-aminoacyl-tRNA.

    **Conclusions:** This study elucidates a chiral enforcement mechanism with a critical role in preventing D-amino acid infiltration during the evolution of the translational apparatus. Our findings provide new insights into the molecular basis of homochirality in proteins and have implications for understanding the mechanisms of protein synthesis and the origins of life. The discovery of the Gly-cisPro dipeptide as a key component of the DTD recognition mechanism highlights the importance of cross-subunit interactions in protein function and provides a new perspective on the evolution of protein structure and function.
Title: Energetic Coupling and Spontaneous Gating in Cystic Fibrosis Transmembrane Conductance Regulator (CFTR) Channels

    Abstract:

    Background: Cystic fibrosis (CF) is a genetic disorder caused by mutations in the cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) gene, which encodes a chloride channel. The CFTR channel is a member of the ATP-binding cassette (ABC) protein superfamily and is regulated by cytosolic ATP. In wild-type CFTR, ATP-binding-induced dimerization of two cytosolic nucleotide-binding domains (NBDs) opens the pore, while dimer disruption following ATP hydrolysis closes it. However, in certain CF mutants, spontaneous openings without ATP are rare, but can be stimulated by ivacaftor, a clinically approved CFTR potentiator.

    Research Question: What are the molecular motions underlying spontaneous gating in CFTR channels, and how do they relate to NBD dimerization and ATP binding?

    Methods: We used a combination of biochemical and biophysical approaches to investigate the energetic coupling between residues across the dimer interface and spontaneous pore opening/closure in single CFTR channels.

    Main Findings: Our results show that spontaneous openings are strictly coupled to NBD dimerization, which may occur even without ATP. Coordinated NBD/pore movements are intrinsic to CFTR, and ATP alters the stability, but not the fundamental structural architecture, of open- and closed-pore conformations. This explains the correlated effects of phosphorylation, mutations, and drugs on ATP-driven and spontaneous activity.

    Implications: Our findings provide new insights into the mechanism of CFTR channel gating and the role of NBD dimerization in spontaneous openings. This understanding has important implications for understanding CF mutation and drug mechanisms, and may lead to the development of new therapeutic strategies for treating CF.

    Conclusions: Our study reveals that the molecular motions underlying spontaneous gating in CFTR channels are closely linked to NBD dimerization, and that ATP acts as a "molecular glue" to stabilize the transition state and the open state. These findings provide a new framework for understanding the complex interplay between ATP binding, NBD dimerization, and pore opening in CFTR channels, and have important implications for the development of new treatments for cystic fibrosis.
Title: Unveiling the Orderphobic Effect: A Novel Mechanism for Transmembrane Protein Assembly and Mobility in Lipid Bilayers

    Abstract:

    Background: Transmembrane proteins play a crucial role in various cellular processes, yet their assembly and mobility within lipid bilayers remain poorly understood. Here, we propose a novel mechanism, termed the orderphobic effect, which governs the assembly and mobility of transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers.

    Research Question: What is the underlying mechanism driving the assembly and mobility of transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers, and how does it relate to the phase transition between ordered and disordered phases?

    Methods: We employed large-scale molecular simulations to investigate the behavior of transmembrane proteins in model lipid bilayers. Our simulations revealed that a protein with hydrophobic thickness equal to that of the disordered phase embedded in an ordered bilayer stabilizes a microscopic order-disorder interface, which exhibits finite stiffness.

    Main Findings: We discovered that when two such proteins approach each other, they assemble due to the reduction in net interfacial energy. This phenomenon, analogous to the hydrophobic effect, is mediated by proximity to the order-disorder phase transition and the size and hydrophobic mismatch of the protein. The strength and range of forces arising from this effect are significantly larger than those that could arise from membrane elasticity.

    Implications: Our findings suggest that the orderphobic effect plays a crucial role in the assembly and mobility of transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers. This novel mechanism has significant implications for our understanding of cellular processes, such as protein-protein interactions, protein-lipid interactions, and membrane fluidity.

    Conclusions: The orderphobic effect provides a new paradigm for understanding the behavior of transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers. Our predictions of enhanced lipid mobility surrounding orderphobic proteins may be experimentally tested using single molecule tracking techniques. Furthermore, the dual nature of the orderphobic effect, where a transmembrane protein in the disordered phase can nucleate an ordered region and order-disorder interface, highlights the complexity of biological membranes and transmembrane proteins.

    **Title:** Characterizing the Action Potential-Fluorescence Transfer Function in Vivo: Implications for Calcium Imaging Studies

    **Research Question:** What is the relationship between action potentials (APs) and fluorescence signals in vivo, and how does this relationship impact the detection of APs in population imaging studies?

    **Methods:** We used simultaneous calcium imaging and cell-attached recordings from transgenic mice expressing GCaMP6s or GCaMP6f to investigate the AP-fluorescence transfer function in 48 layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons in primary visual cortex.

    **Main Findings:** Under optimal conditions, most APs were detected, but under conditions typical of population imaging studies, only a minority of 1 AP and 2 AP events were detected (often <10% and ~20-30%, respectively). This emphasizes the limits of AP detection under more realistic imaging conditions.

    **Implications:** Our findings highlight the challenges of inferring underlying AP trains or firing rates from calcium imaging data, particularly in population imaging studies. The large field of view required for population imaging generates fewer photons per neuron, compromising AP detection.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides insight into the relationship between spiking activity in vivo and fluorescence signals, which will aid the interpretation of existing and future calcium imaging datasets. By characterizing the AP-fluorescence transfer function, we can better understand the limitations of calcium imaging and develop more accurate spike inference models. Our results will facilitate the interpretation of datasets from mainstream transgenic mouse lines, such as the Allen Institute’s Brain Observatory Visual Coding dataset.

    **Future Directions:** Our work has implications for the development of more accurate spike inference models and the design of future calcium imaging experiments. By understanding the AP-fluorescence transfer function, researchers can optimize imaging conditions and improve the detection of APs in population imaging studies, ultimately enhancing our understanding of neural dynamics in vivo.

    **Title:** Functional Switching Valve in the Female Genitalia of Neotrogla: A Novel Mechanism for Seminal Gift Competition

    **Research Question:** How does the female genitalia of the Brazilian cave insect Neotrogla enable females to compete for seminal gifts in nutrient-poor cave habitats?

    **Methods:** We conducted a morphological and functional analysis of the female genitalia of Neotrogla, focusing on the switching valve complex and its role in seminal flow control.

    **Main Findings:** Our study reveals a functional switching valve within the female genitalia of Neotrogla, composed of two plate-like sclerites, a closure element, and in-and-outflow canals. The switching valve in Neotrogla's female genitalia is a remarkable adaptation that highlights the complexity and diversity of reproductive strategies in insects.

    **Title:** Rapid and Modular Self-Assembly of Cell-Matrix Adhesion Sites through Pre-Assembled Integrin Adhesome Building Blocks

    **Research Question:** How do integrin adhesomes self-assemble locally, rapidly, and correctly into diverse cell-matrix adhesion sites?

    **Methods:** We employed fluorescence cross-correlation spectroscopy (FCCS) and fluorescence recovery after photobleaching (FRAP) to investigate the cytosolic state of integrin-adhesome components and their dynamic exchange between adhesion sites and cytosol.

    **Main Findings:** Our results reveal that the integrin adhesome is extensively pre-assembled in the cytosol as multi-protein building blocks for adhesion sites. The building blocks are size-confined and predominantly correspond to functional layers across focal adhesions, including anchoring and integrin regulation, force transduction, and actin regulation.

    **Conclusions:** We propose a model in which multi-protein building blocks enable rapid and modular self-assembly of adhesion sites. The integrin adhesome is pre-assembled in the cytosol as multi-protein building blocks, which are then locally switched on to self-assemble adhesion sites by passing through checkpoints that induce additional protein interactions. This model ensures that the self-assembly process maintains control over the identity of co-recruited proteins, preventing the formation of aberrant structures. Our results provide new insights into the assembly logic of adhesion sites and highlight the importance of integrating interaction-dependencies across the integrin adhesome to understand this complex biological process.

    **Title:** Electrophysiological Markers of Reduced Arousal States: A Comparative Analysis of Wakefulness, Sleep, and Anesthesia

    **Research Question:** Can the 1/f spectral slope of the electrophysiological power spectrum be used as a common electrophysiological marker to track states of reduced arousal, including different sleep stages and anesthesia in humans?

    **Methods:** This study employed intracranial and surface EEG recordings from four independent data sets to investigate the 1/f spectral slope of the electrophysiological power spectrum in wakefulness, non-rapid eye movement (NREM) sleep, rapid eye movement (REM) sleep, and general anesthesia with propofol.

    **Main Findings:** Our results demonstrate that the 1/f spectral slope, which reflects the non-oscillatory, scale-free component of neural activity, reliably differentiates wakefulness from all three states of reduced arousal: NREM sleep, REM sleep, and general anesthesia with propofol. Specifically, the spectral slope becomes more negative (i.e., the power spectrum steeper) in sleep and under anesthesia compared to wakefulness. Moreover, the spectral slope discriminates wakefulness from REM sleep solely based on the neurophysiological brain state.

    **Implications:** These findings provide empirical evidence for the notion that the 1/f dynamics of the electrophysiological power spectrum can be used as a common electrophysiological marker to track states of reduced arousal, including different sleep stages and anesthesia in humans. This discovery has significant implications for the development of novel diagnostic and therapeutic strategies for sleep disorders and anesthesia-related complications.

    **Conclusions:** Our results from four independent studies provide a mechanistic link between the spectral slope and population synchrony during slow oscillations, and demonstrate that the non-oscillatory, aperiodic part of the power spectrum can be used to track changes in arousal levels in both propofol anesthesia and sleep in humans with high temporal precision and on a wide range of timescales. Collectively, our findings highlight the potential of the 1/f spectral slope as a universal electrophysiological marker for reduced arousal states.

    **Title:** Cytoskeletal Regulation of Stromule Dynamics and Perinuclear Chloroplast Clustering during Innate Immunity

    **Research Question:** How do microtubules (MTs) and actin filaments (AFs) regulate stromule dynamics and perinuclear chloroplast clustering during innate immunity in plant cells?

    **Title:** Predicting Protein-Protein Interactions across Interfaces using Residue Covariation

    **Research Question:** Do amino acid sequence identities of residues that make contact across protein interfaces covary during evolution, and can such covariance be used to predict contacts across interfaces and assemble models of biological complexes?

    **Methods:** We employed a pseudo-likelihood-based statistical method to examine residue-residue covariation across protein-protein interfaces in 50S ribosomal units and 28 additional bacterial protein complexes with known structure. We also applied this method to predict contacts across interfaces for 36 evolutionarily conserved complexes of unknown structure.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that residue pairs identified using the pseudo-likelihood-based method to covary across protein-protein interfaces are almost always in contact in the complex, provided that the number of aligned sequences is greater than the average length of the two proteins. Furthermore, we found that significant residue-residue covariance occurs frequently between physically interacting protein pairs but very rarely between non-interacting pairs. This suggests that residue covariation can be a useful predictor of protein-protein interactions.

    **Implications:** Our findings have significant implications for the prediction of protein-protein interactions and the assembly of models of biological complexes. By identifying covarying residue pairs, we can make accurate predictions of contacts across interfaces, which can contribute to the determination of the structures of biologically important complexes.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates that residue covariation can be a powerful tool for predicting protein-protein interactions and assembling models of biological complexes. The pseudo-likelihood-based method used in this study can be applied to a wide range of protein complexes, and our results suggest that it can be used to make accurate predictions of contacts across interfaces. The predicted residue-residue interactions for 36 evolutionarily conserved complexes of unknown structure presented in this study provide a valuable resource for structural biologists and can contribute to the determination of the structures of these biologically important complexes.

    **Title:** Active Sensing in Visual Categorization: Unveiling the Efficiency of Eye Movements

    **Research Question:** How do participants accumulate information from multiple locations in a scene to categorize visual patterns, and what is the efficiency of their eye movements in this process?

    **Methods:** We employed a novel gaze-contingent paradigm in a visual categorization task, where participants' eye movements were tracked and small apertures of the image were successively revealed at each fixation location. We used a combination of a Bayesian ideal observer and an active sensor algorithm to estimate the efficiency of eye movements.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that participants' scan paths follow an active sensing strategy that incorporates information already acquired about the scene and knowledge of the statistical structure of patterns. Categorization performance was significantly improved when locations were revealed to participants by an optimal Bayesian active sensor algorithm. We estimated that a major portion of the apparent suboptimality of fixation locations arises from prior biases, perceptual noise, and inaccuracies in eye movements. Our analysis revealed that the central process of selecting fixation locations is around 70% efficient in our task.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that participants select eye movements with the goal of maximizing information about abstract categories that require the integration of information from multiple locations. This active sensing strategy is more efficient than heuristic strategies that only employ a subset of the elements of a fully closed-loop active sensing strategy.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides the first fixation-by-fixation analysis of information gathering under active eye movements, allowing us to dissociate the contributions of the eye-movement selection process from those of perceptual, motor, and decision processes. Our results have implications for understanding the efficiency of eye movements in various perceptual domains and highlight the importance of considering the interplay between prior biases, perceptual noise, and eye movement inaccuracies in active sensing tasks.
Title: Elucidating the Molecular Determinants of Large Cargo Import through the Nuclear Pore Complex

    Abstract:
    The nuclear pore complex (NPC) plays a crucial role in regulating nucleocytoplasmic transport, allowing for the passage of thousands of molecules, including large cargoes such as pathogens, mRNAs, and pre-ribosomes. However, the quantitative understanding of the requirements for nuclear import of these large cargoes, particularly the role of multivalent binding to transport receptors via nuclear localization sequences (NLSs) and the effect of size on import efficiency, remains poorly understood. To address this knowledge gap, we investigated the nuclear import kinetics of 30 large cargo models based on four capsid-like particles in the size range of 17-36 nm, with tunable numbers of up to 240 NLSs. Our results demonstrate that the requirements for nuclear transport can be recapitulated by a simple two-parameter biophysical model that correlates the import flux with the energetics of large cargo transport through the NPC. We show that the import flux scales non-linearly with size and that the number of NLSs has a significant impact on import efficiency. Our findings provide key molecular determinants of large cargo import in cells and have implications for understanding the export of large cargoes, such as pre-ribosomal subunits and large RNA export complexes.

    Methods:
    We used a combination of biochemical and biophysical approaches to investigate the nuclear import kinetics of large cargo models. We designed and synthesized four capsid-like particles in the size range of 17-36 nm, with tunable numbers of up to 240 NLSs. We then assayed the nuclear import kinetics of these cargoes in HeLa cells using live-cell imaging and fluorescence microscopy.

    Main Findings:
    Our results show that the requirements for nuclear transport can be recapitulated by a simple two-parameter biophysical model that correlates the import flux with the energetics of large cargo transport through the NPC. We found that the import flux scales non-linearly with size and that the number of NLSs has a significant impact on import efficiency.

    Implications:
    Our findings provide key molecular determinants of large cargo import in cells and have implications for understanding the export of large cargoes, such as pre-ribosomal subunits and large RNA export complexes. The theoretical model used in this study assumes that the capsids do not interact with each other during transport through the pore, which may not be the case in vivo. However, our results suggest that the basic principles of our work could help in the future to better understand export of large cargoes.

    Conclusions:
    In conclusion, our study provides a quantitative understanding of the requirements for nuclear import of large cargoes and highlights the importance of multivalent binding to transport receptors via NLSs and the effect of size on import efficiency. Our findings have implications for understanding the export of large cargoes and provide a framework for future studies on nucleocytoplasmic transport.

    **Title:** Cdc48 Orchestrates Mitochondrial Dynamics through Ubiquitin-Dependent Regulation of E3 Ligases and Deubiquitylases

    **Research Question:** How does Cdc48, a ubiquitin-selective chaperone, regulate mitochondrial dynamics by integrating the activities of E3 ligases and deubiquitylases (DUBs)?

    **Methods:** Using a combination of biochemical and cell biological approaches, we investigated the role of Cdc48 in ubiquitin-dependent regulation of mitochondrial dynamics.     **Conclusions:** In conclusion, our study unravels a new cascade of ubiquitylation events, comprising Cdc48 and two DUBs, which ensures a tight control of Fzo1 ubiquitylation at levels sufficient to allow mitochondrial fusion while preventing unnecessary ubiquitylation that targets Fzo1 for proteasomal turnover. This work provides new insights into the regulation of mitochondrial dynamics and highlights the importance of Cdc48 in integrating the activities of E3 ligases and DUBs to maintain cellular homeostasis.

    **Title:** Categorical Organization of the Ventral Occipito-Temporal Cortex (VOTC) in Sighted and Blind Individuals: A Functional Magnetic Resonance Imaging (fMRI) Study

    **Research Question:** Is vision necessary for the development of the categorical organization of the Ventral Occipito-Temporal Cortex (VOTC)?

These results have significant implications for our understanding of cross-modal plasticity and the neural basis of categorical perception. By harnessing the opportunities provided by representational similarity analysis, we demonstrate that categorical membership is the main factor that predicts the representational structure of sounds in VOTC in blind people, rather than lower-level acoustical attributes of sounds.

    **Significance:** This study contributes to our understanding of the neural basis of categorical perception and the role of visual experience in shaping the organization of the VOTC. The findings have implications for the development of novel rehabilitation strategies for individuals with visual impairments and for our understanding of the neural mechanisms underlying cross-modal plasticity.

    **Title:** Systematic Analysis of RNA Degradation Pathways in Saccharomyces cerevisiae Reveals Insights into RNA Processing and Surveillance

    **Research Question:** How do RNA degradation factors interact with different classes of RNAs in Saccharomyces cerevisiae, and what are the implications for RNA processing and surveillance?

    **Methods:** We employed photoactivatable ribonucleoside-enhanced crosslinking and immunoprecipitation (PAR-CLIP) to generate transcriptome-wide protein binding profiles for 30 general RNA degradation factors in S. cerevisiae.

    **Main Findings:** Our results reveal the distribution of degradation factors between different RNA classes, consistent with the canonical degradation pathway for closed-loop forming mRNAs after deadenylation.     **Implications:** Our study provides a comprehensive understanding of RNA degradation pathways in S. cerevisiae, revealing how degradation factors interact with different classes of RNAs.     **Title:** Viral Modulation of Microbial Communities and Ecosystem Functions in Marine Oxygen Minimum Zones

    **Research Question:** How do viruses interact with uncultivated SUP05 bacteria in marine oxygen minimum zones (OMZs), and what are the implications for ecosystem functions?

    **Methods:** We employed single-cell amplified genomes (SAGs) from uncultivated SUP05 bacteria isolated from a model OMZ to identify viral contigs representing five new genera within dsDNA Caudovirales and ssDNA Microviridae. We analyzed 127 SAGs and 186 microbial and viral metagenomes from the Saanich Inlet water column over a 3-year time interval.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that ∼1/3 of SUP05 bacteria is viral-infected, with higher infection frequency where oxygen-deficiency was most severe.     **Conclusions:** This study provides new insights into the complex interactions between viruses and uncultivated SUP05 bacteria in OMZs. Our findings suggest that viruses play a crucial role in modulating biogeochemical cycling and energy metabolism in these ecosystems. The discovery of virus-encoded dissimilatory sulfite reductase highlights the potential for viruses to reprogram their host's metabolic pathways. These results have significant implications for our understanding of the role of viruses in shaping microbial communities and ecosystem functions in OMZs, and highlight the need for further research into the complex interactions between viruses and their hosts in these ecosystems.
Title: Elucidating the Role of TTC26/DYF13 in Intraflagellar Transport and Ciliary Motility

    Abstract:
    Intraflagellar transport (IFT) is a highly conserved mechanism responsible for the assembly and maintenance of cilia and flagella in eukaryotic cells. Despite its importance, the functions of individual IFT proteins remain poorly understood. This study aimed to investigate the role of TTC26/DYF13, a putative IFT complex B protein, in mammalian cells and Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. Using live imaging and biochemical approaches, we demonstrate that TTC26/DYF13 is an IFT complex B protein in both mammalian cells and C. reinhardtii. Knockdown of TTC26/DYF13 in zebrafish embryos or mutation of TTC26/DYF13 in C. reinhardtii resulted in short cilia with abnormal motility. Notably, IFT particle assembly and speed were normal in dyf13 mutant flagella, unlike in other IFT complex B mutants. Proteomic and biochemical analyses revealed a specific set of proteins involved in motility that were depleted in the dyf13 mutant. These findings suggest that different IFT proteins are responsible for transporting distinct cargo subsets, providing a possible explanation for the complexity of the IFT machinery.

    Our results indicate that TTC26/DYF13 plays a specialized role in transporting a specific set of motility-related ciliary proteins into cilia/flagella, rather than being essential for ciliogenesis or IFT particle assembly. This is in contrast to other IFT proteins, which are necessary for cilia/flagella assembly. The dyf13 mutant flagella were shorter and exhibited pronounced motility defects, while IFT particle movement was normal. Comparative proteomic analysis of flagellar proteins in the dyf13 mutant further supports a specific function for TTC26/DYF13 in transporting a subset of cargo proteins involved in flagellar motility.

    These findings have significant implications for our understanding of the IFT machinery and its role in ciliary motility. The discovery that different IFT proteins are responsible for transporting distinct cargo subsets highlights the complexity and specificity of the IFT process. Further analysis of TTC26/DYF13 and other IFT proteins will be necessary to clarify their roles in determining cilia and flagella length and motility.

    **Title:** Aneuploidy Facilitates Organismal Evolution through Gene-Dosage Compensation in Wild Yeast Isolates

    **Research Question:** How do cells overcome the deleterious effects of aneuploidy, a condition where cells have an abnormal number of chromosomes, until new phenotypes evolve?

    **Methods:** We investigated aneuploidy in wild yeast isolates and generated diploid strain panels with cells carrying two, three, or four copies of affected chromosomes to study gene-dosage compensation.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that aneuploidy is common in wild yeast isolates, which exhibit lower-than-expected expression at many amplified genes. We found that gene-dosage compensation functions at 10-30% of amplified genes, and genes subject to dosage compensation are under higher expression constraint in wild populations but show elevated rates of gene amplification. Furthermore, we discovered that aneuploidy provides a clear ecological advantage to oak strain YPS1009 by amplifying a causal gene that escapes dosage compensation.

    **Implications:** Our study presents a model in which dosage compensation buffers gene amplification through aneuploidy, providing a natural, but likely transient, route to rapid phenotypic evolution. This finding has significant implications for our understanding of how cells tolerate duplication of toxic genes long enough for new phenotypes to evolve.

    **Conclusions:** Our results provide new insight into the long-standing conundrum of how cells overcome the deleterious effects of aneuploidy. We show that up to 40% of amplified genes in naturally aneuploid strains exhibit lower-than-expected expression, which is in part due to heritable polymorphisms that down-regulate expression from amplified genes. Our study highlights the importance of gene-dosage compensation in buffering gene amplification and facilitating phenotypic evolution in wild yeast isolates. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of the evolution of aneuploidy and its role in organismal evolution.

    Abstract:

Our study provides new insights into the integration of spatial and temporal cues in neural progenitors and has implications for understanding the mechanisms of neuronal diversity in both Drosophila and mammals.

    Title: Unveiling the Gating Mechanism of P2X Receptors through Photo-Switchable Cross-Linkers

    Research Question: How do P2X receptors open their transmembrane pore in response to extracellular ATP, and what is the molecular mechanism underlying this process?

    Methods: We employed photo-switchable cross-linkers as "molecular tweezers" to monitor inter-residue distances in the transmembrane domain of the P2X2 receptor during activation.     Implications: Our study establishes a versatile strategy for investigating the function of membrane proteins using photo-switchable cross-linkers and molecular dynamics simulations. This approach can be applied to other membrane proteins to elucidate their gating mechanisms.

    Conclusions: Our data demonstrate that the P2X2 receptor contains a critical "hinge" in its TM2 helices, which facilitates their vertical bending and allows the inner and outer ends of adjacent TM2 helices to come closer during activation. This study provides the first application of photo-switchable derivatives to investigate the mechanism of pore gating in P2X receptors, and sheds light on the long-standing controversy surrounding the mechanism of P2X receptor activation. Our findings have significant implications for understanding the function of P2X receptors and other membrane proteins, and highlight the potential of photo-switchable cross-linkers as a powerful tool for structural biology research.

    **Title:** Oncogenic Ras Converts Apoptotic Caspases into Tumor Promoters through a Feedback Amplification Loop

    **Research Question:** How do apoptotic caspases, traditionally considered tumor suppressors, contribute to tumor growth and proliferation in the presence of oncogenic Ras mutations?

    **Methods:** We investigated the role of caspases in a well-studied neoplastic tumor model in Drosophila, specifically in scrib−/− RasV12 cells, which harbor oncogenic mutations of the proto-oncogene Ras.

    **Implications:** Our findings challenge the traditional view of caspases as tumor suppressors and reveal a novel mechanism by which oncogenic Ras converts apoptotic caspases into tumor promoters. This feedback amplification loop highlights the complex interplay between tumorous epithelial cells, macrophage-like immune cells, and ROS in promoting tumor growth and invasion.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying tumor progression and suggests that targeting the caspase-ROS-JNK feedback loop may be a promising therapeutic strategy to inhibit tumor growth and improve organismal survival. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of cancer biology and the development of novel cancer therapies.

    **Methods:** We conducted a series of experiments (Experiments 1-5) to investigate the relationship between social interactions, subjective time, and oxytocin. We also administered intranasal oxytocin or atosiban, a competitive antagonist of oxytocin, to participants to examine the effects on subjective time perception.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that motion sequences showing agents acting communicatively are perceived to be significantly shorter in duration compared to those acting non-communicatively. This effect, known as temporal compression, is negatively correlated with an individual's autistic-like tendency. Critically, intranasal oxytocin administration restored the temporal compression effect in socially less proficient individuals, whereas atosiban administration diminished the effect in socially proficient individuals.

    **Implications:** These findings suggest that perceived time is not a faithful representation of physical time, but is instead highly idiosyncratic and influenced by an individual's personality traits, such as social cognitive competency. Moreover, our results indicate that oxytocin plays a key role in mediating the subjective duration of social interactions, further supporting its involvement in human social cognition.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides novel insights into the complex relationship between social interactions, subjective time, and oxytocin. The findings have implications for our understanding of social cognition and the neural mechanisms underlying social behavior. Specifically, they suggest that oxytocin may be a potential therapeutic target for improving social skills in individuals with autism spectrum disorder (ASD). Further research is needed to fully elucidate the mechanisms underlying these effects and to explore the therapeutic potential of oxytocin in ASD.
Title: Elucidating the Impact of SARS-CoV-2 RBD and ACE2 Mutations on Viral Infection and Immune Evasion

    Abstract:
    The emergence of SARS-CoV-2 variants of concern (VOCs) has raised concerns about their potential to enhance viral transmission and immune evasion. We investigated the effects of five common RBD mutations (K417N, K417T, N501Y, E484K, and S477N) and two common ACE2 mutations (S19P and K26R) on the RBD/ACE2 interaction. Using a detailed affinity and kinetics analysis, we found that most RBD mutations increased the affinity of the RBD/ACE2 interaction, with the exceptions being mutations K417N/T, which decreased the affinity. Our results suggest that the N501Y and S477N mutations enhance transmission primarily by enhancing binding, while the K417N/T mutations facilitate immune escape, and the E484K mutation enhances binding and immune escape. Furthermore, we examined the effects of combinations of RBD mutations, including those present in VOC-202102–02, a subset of the Alpha lineage, and the Beta and Gamma variants. Our findings have important implications for understanding the mechanisms of SARS-CoV-2 transmission and immune evasion, and highlight the need for continued surveillance of RBD and ACE2 mutations.

    Methods:
    We performed a detailed affinity and kinetics analysis of the RBD/ACE2 interaction using surface plasmon resonance (SPR) and isothermal titration calorimetry (ITC) at a physiological temperature (37°C). We analyzed the effects of individual RBD mutations and combinations found in new SARS-CoV-2 Alpha (B.1.1.7), Beta (B.1.351), and Gamma (P1) variants.

    Main Findings:
    Our results show that most RBD mutations increased the affinity of the RBD/ACE2 interaction, with the exceptions being mutations K417N/T, which decreased the affinity. The N501Y and S477N mutations enhanced transmission primarily by enhancing binding, while the K417N/T mutations facilitated immune escape, and the E484K mutation enhanced binding and immune escape.

    Implications:
    Our findings have important implications for understanding the mechanisms of SARS-CoV-2 transmission and immune evasion. The increased affinity of RBD mutations for ACE2 may enhance viral transmission and disease severity, while the K417N/T mutations may facilitate immune escape. The E484K mutation may enhance both binding and immune evasion. Our results also highlight the need for continued surveillance of RBD and ACE2 mutations.

    Conclusions:
    In conclusion, our study provides a detailed analysis of the effects of RBD and ACE2 mutations on the RBD/ACE2 interaction. Our findings suggest that RBD mutations can enhance transmission and immune evasion, while ACE2 mutations may increase the risk of infection and/or severe disease in carriers of these variants. Further studies are needed to fully understand the impact of these mutations on SARS-CoV-2 transmission and disease severity.

    **Title:** Mapping Directional Gene-Gene Interactions to Reveal Regulatory Relationships in Drosophila Cells

    **Research Question:** How do gene-gene interactions shape complex phenotypes and modify the effects of mutations during development and disease?

    **Methods:** We employed combinatorial RNA interference (RNAi) and automated single-cell phenotyping to generate a large genetic interaction map for 21 phenotypic features of Drosophila cells. We selected 1367 genes implicated in key biological processes, including signalling, chromatin biology, cell cycle regulation, and protein turnover control.

Overall, this research provides a valuable resource for the scientific community and highlights the importance of gene-gene interactions in understanding complex biological processes.
Title: Elucidating the Genetic Basis of Heterostyly in Primula: Identification of the CYP734A50 Gene as the G Locus Determining Style-Length Dimorphism

    Abstract:
    Heterostyly, a widespread floral adaptation promoting outbreeding, remains poorly understood in terms of its genetic basis and evolutionary origin. In Primula (primroses), heterostyly is controlled by the S-locus supergene, which determines the reciprocal arrangement of reproductive organs and incompatibility between morphs. However, the identities of the component genes remain unknown. Here, we investigate the genetic basis of heterostyly in Primula and identify the CYP734A50 gene, encoding a putative brassinosteroid-degrading enzyme, as the G locus that determines the style-length dimorphism. We demonstrate that CYP734A50 is present only on the short-styled S-morph haplotype, specifically expressed in S-morph styles, and its loss or inactivation leads to long styles. Our results provide a mechanistic explanation for the Primula style-length dimorphism and shed light on the evolution of the S-locus as a prime model for a complex plant supergene. We propose that CYP734A50 arose via a lineage-specific gene duplication and gained style-specific expression in the ancestor of the Primulaceae, representing a dominant style-shortening mutation. Our findings open the way for understanding the evolutionary origin of the Primula S-locus and provide a rare example of an identified causal locus in a supergene underlying an adaptive polymorphism.

    Research Question: What is the genetic basis of heterostyly in Primula, and what is the identity of the G locus that determines the style-length dimorphism?

    Methods: We sequenced style transcriptomes of heterostylous and homostylous Primula species to identify the CYP734A50 gene and investigated its expression and function using VIGS (Virus-Induced Gene Silencing) experiments.

    Main Findings: We found that CYP734A50 is present only on the short-styled S-morph haplotype, specifically expressed in S-morph styles, and its loss or inactivation leads to long styles. We also demonstrated that CYP734A50 arose via a lineage-specific gene duplication and gained style-specific expression in the ancestor of the Primulaceae.

    Implications: Our results provide a mechanistic explanation for the Primula style-length dimorphism and shed light on the evolution of the S-locus as a prime model for a complex plant supergene. The identification of CYP734A50 as the G locus opens the way for understanding the evolutionary origin of the Primula S-locus and provides a rare example of an identified causal locus in a supergene underlying an adaptive polymorphism.

    Conclusions: Our study elucidates the genetic basis of heterostyly in Primula and identifies the CYP734A50 gene as the G locus determining the style-length dimorphism. Our findings have significant implications for our understanding of the evolution of the S-locus and the mechanisms underlying adaptive polymorphisms in plants.

    **Title:** Kisspeptin and Neurokinin B Neurocircuits in Pubertal Development and Fertility

    **Research Question:** How do Kisspeptin (Kiss1) and Neurokinin B (NKB) neurons in the hypothalamic arcuate nucleus (Kiss1ARH) regulate gonadotropin-releasing hormone 1 (GnRH) neurons during pubertal development and fertility?

    **Methods:** We employed optogenetics, whole-cell electrophysiology, molecular pharmacology, and single-cell RT-PCR in mice to characterize the cellular basis for synchronized Kiss1ARH neuronal activity.

These findings have significant implications for our understanding of reproductive physiology and may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies for reproductive disorders.

    **Title:** BMP Signalling Regulates Differentiation by Blocking Downregulation of Cdh1 and Priming Pluripotent Cells for Mesoderm Differentiation

    **Research Question:** How does Bone Morphogenetic Protein (BMP) signalling control differentiation in pluripotent cells, and what are the underlying mechanisms?

    **Methods:** We investigated the role of BMP signalling in regulating differentiation in pluripotent cells using a combination of molecular and cellular approaches, including gene expression analysis, cell culture experiments, and functional assays.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that BMP signalling maintains pluripotency by blocking the downregulation of Cdh1, an event that accompanies the earliest stages of neural and mesodermal differentiation. We found that loss of Cdh1 is a limiting requirement for differentiation of pluripotent cells, and that experimental suppression of Cdh1 activity rescues the BMP-imposed block to differentiation. Furthermore, we demonstrate that BMP acts prior to and independently of Cdh1 to prime pluripotent cells for mesoderm differentiation, thus reinforcing the block to neural differentiation.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that differentiation depends not only on exposure to appropriate extrinsic cues but also on morphogenetic events that control receptivity to those differentiation cues. We propose that BMP signalling feeds into this control mechanism by maintaining Cdh1 expression, which interferes with efficient neural and mesodermal differentiation.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the mechanisms by which BMP signalling controls differentiation in pluripotent cells. We conclude that BMP signalling maintains pluripotency by blocking the downregulation of Cdh1, and that BMP also primes pluripotent cells for mesoderm differentiation by imposing a posterior identity that is incompatible with a neural fate. These findings have important implications for our understanding of the transition from pluripotency to lineage commitment, and highlight the complex interplay between extrinsic cues and morphogenetic events in regulating cell fate specification.

    **Title:** Cep120 Regulates Centrosome Homeostasis and Ciliary Assembly during Quiescence

    **Research Question:** How are the asymmetric properties of centrioles in quiescent cells established and maintained, and what is the role of Cep120 in this process?

    **Methods:** We investigated the function of Cep120, a daughter centriole-associated ciliopathy protein, in quiescent mouse and human cells. We depleted Cep120 using RNA interference (RNAi) and examined the effects on centrosome homeostasis, microtubule-nucleation activity, and ciliary assembly and signaling.

    **Main Findings:** Depletion of Cep120 in quiescent cells led to the accumulation of pericentriolar material (PCM) components, including pericentrin, Cdk5Rap2, ninein, and Cep170, resulting in increased microtubule-nucleation activity at the centrosome.     **Implications:** Our results indicate that Cep120 plays a critical role in maintaining centrosome homeostasis by inhibiting the untimely maturation of the daughter centriole. Loss of Cep120 disrupts the localization of centriolar satellites, leading to defective ciliary assembly and signaling. These findings have important implications for our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying ciliopathies, such as Jeune Asphyxiating Thoracic Dystrophy and Joubert syndrome.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals that Cep120 is a key regulator of centrosome homeostasis and ciliary assembly during quiescence. The loss of Cep120 disrupts the asymmetric distribution of PCM proteins at the centrosome, leading to aberrant microtubule organization, dynein-dependent trafficking of centrosomal proteins, and defective ciliary assembly and signaling. These findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying ciliopathies and highlight the importance of Cep120 in maintaining centrosome homeostasis and ciliary function.

    **Title:** Neuron-Glia Interactions in the Developing Retina: Spatio-Temporal Characteristics and Molecular Pathways

    **Research Question:** How do neurons and Müller glial cells (MCs) communicate in the developing retina, and what are the molecular pathways involved in this interaction?

    **Methods:** We used a combination of whole-cell recordings of retinal ganglion cells (RGCs) and two-photon imaging of calcium indicators or glutamate sensors expressed specifically in MCs to investigate the spatio-temporal characteristics and molecular pathways of neuron-glia interactions in the developing retina.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that retinal waves induce calcium transients in a large proportion of MCs, demonstrating that spontaneous activity encompasses both neuronal and glial networks at this age. We found that the mechanism of interaction between neurons and MCs during cholinergic retinal waves is similar to previous findings in visual cortex and hippocampus, where cholinergic neuron stimulation leads to astrocyte calcium transients via activation of muscarinic acetylcholine receptors. However, during glutamatergic waves, MC calcium transients were inhibited by ionotropic glutamate receptor antagonists, indicating that the responsiveness of MCs changes to match the neurotransmitter used to support retinal waves.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that neurons and MCs exhibit correlated activity during a critical period of retinal maturation that is enabled by neurotransmitter spillover from retinal synapses. The proportion of MCs that responded to waves with calcium transients decreased significantly from postnatal day 9 (P9) to P11, despite the presence of retinal waves, indicating that MCs express multiple neurotransmitter receptor types throughout this period, allowing neuronal control of MC activity to be sustained as neuronal synchronization shifts from being dependent on cholinergic to glutamatergic signaling.

    **Conclusions:** Our study elucidates the spatio-temporal characteristics and molecular pathways of neuron-glia interactions in the developing retina, highlighting the importance of neurotransmitter spillover from retinal synapses in enabling correlated activity between neurons and MCs during a critical period of retinal maturation. These findings have implications for our understanding of the development of neural circuits and the role of glial cells in modulating neuronal activity.

    **Title:** Estimating Postmortem Interval (PMI) using Microbial Community Changes in Decomposing Corpses: A Novel Forensic Approach

    **Research Question:** Can microbial community changes in decomposing corpses be used to estimate the postmortem interval (PMI) with high accuracy and precision?

    **Methods:** We conducted a laboratory experiment to investigate the temporal changes in microbial communities associated with mouse corpses as they decomposed on soil under controlled conditions for 48 days. We characterized bacteria, archaea, and microbial eukaryotes using a combination of culture-independent, high-throughput DNA sequencing approaches for each sample.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that postmortem microbial community changes are dramatic, measurable, and repeatable in a mouse model system, allowing PMI to be estimated within approximately 3 days over 48 days. We demonstrate that microbial community data can be developed into a forensic tool for estimating PMI, providing a novel method that avoids many of the limitations associated with existing techniques, such as forensic entomology.

    **Implications:** Our findings have significant implications for the field of forensic science, as they provide a detailed understanding of bacterial and microbial eukaryotic ecology within a decomposing corpse system. This knowledge can be used to develop a reliable and accurate method for estimating PMI, which is critical in death investigations.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides a proof-of-principle demonstration that microbial community ecology of decomposing corpses has potential to be developed into a complementary forensics tool for estimation of PMI. We rigorously tested hypotheses long-held by the forensic community about the role of microbes in corpse decomposition and demonstrated that microbial community change is sufficiently measurable and directional during decomposition to allow accurate estimates of past events, such as PMI. Our results have the potential to revolutionize the field of forensic science and provide a new tool for investigators to estimate PMI with high accuracy and precision.

    **Title:** Investigating the Dynamics of AMPA Receptor Trafficking in Synapses using Super-Resolution Microscopy

    **Research Question:** How do fluorophore size and photostability influence the trafficking of AMPA receptors (AMPARs) outside of and within post-synaptic densities (PSDs) in rat synapses?

    **Methods:** We employed super-resolution microscopy to examine the diffusion of AMPARs labeled with organic fluorescent dyes (≈4 nm), small quantum dots (sQDs, ≈10 nm diameter), and big quantum dots (bQDs, >20 nm) in rat synapses. AMPARs were coupled to these fluorophores via different-sized linkers.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that >90% of AMPARs labeled with fluorescent dyes or sQDs were diffusing in confined nanodomains within PSDs, which were stable for 15 min or longer. In contrast, <10% of sQD-AMPARs were extrasynaptic and highly mobile, whereas 5-10% of bQD-AMPARs were in PSDs and 90-95% were extrasynaptic. These findings suggest that AMPARs rapidly enter stable nanodomains in PSDs with a lifetime >15 min and do not accumulate in extrasynaptic membranes.

    **Implications:** Our study challenges the hypothesis that AMPAR entry is limited by the occupancy of open PSD "slots." Instead, our results indicate that AMPARs can rapidly enter stable nanodomains within PSDs, which may have important implications for our understanding of synaptic plasticity and neurotransmission.

    **Conclusions:** Using super-resolution microscopy and sQDs, we were able to study the dynamics of AMPAR nanodomains in synapses for extended periods (up to 60 min). Our findings suggest that the localization of AMPA receptors is predominantly at synaptic regions, with typically one nanodomain per synapse. The use of smaller fluorophores (sQDs or organic dyes) revealed that AMPARs are largely confined to PSD nanodomains, whereas larger fluorophores (bQDs) resulted in extrasynaptic accumulation. These results highlight the importance of fluorophore size and photostability in studying AMPAR trafficking and synaptic function.

    **Title:** SUMOylation of NaV1.2 Channels Mediates the Early Response of Central Neurons to Hypoxia

    **Research Question:** What is the mechanism underlying the earliest response of central neurons to hypoxia, characterized by an increase in voltage-gated sodium current (INa)?

    **Methods:** We investigated the role of the Small Ubiquitin-like Modifier (SUMO) pathway in rat cerebellar granule neurons (CGN) subjected to acute hypoxia. We used electrophysiology and biochemical techniques to examine the SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels and its effects on INa.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that hypoxia activates the SUMO pathway in CGN, leading to the SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels at the cell surface. This SUMOylation increases INa by shifting the voltage-dependence of channel activation, allowing depolarizing steps to evoke larger sodium currents.     **Implications:** Our findings suggest that the SUMO pathway and NaV1.2 channels are potential targets for neuroprotective interventions to reduce damage due to acute hypoxic insults to central neurons. The rapid SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels at the plasma membrane in response to hypoxia provides a novel mechanism for the early response of central neurons to hypoxia.

    **Conclusions:** In summary, our study reveals that SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels at the plasma membrane underlies the immediate increase in INa in response to acute hypoxia in cultured neurons and CHO cells. The SUMO pathway and NaV1.2 channels may be valuable therapeutic targets for reducing damage due to acute hypoxic insults to central neurons. Our findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying the early response of central neurons to hypoxia and highlight potential strategies for neuroprotection.
