    **Title:** Unveiling the Thermal Optima and Limits of Ross River Virus Transmission: A Mechanistic Modeling Approach

    **Research Question:** What are the thermal optima and limits of Ross River virus (RRV) transmission, and how do they influence the geographic, seasonal, and interannual variation in disease dynamics?

    **Methods:** We developed a mechanistic model for the thermal response of RRV, an important mosquito-borne pathogen in Australia, Pacific Islands, and potentially at risk of emerging worldwide. We parameterized the model using data from two key vector species, Cx. annulirostris and Ae. vigilax.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that RRV transmission peaks at moderate temperatures (26.4°C) and declines to zero at thermal limits (17.0 and 31.5°C). The model accurately predicts that transmission is year-round endemic in the tropics but seasonal in temperate areas, resulting in a nationwide seasonal peak in human cases. Climate warming is likely to increase transmission in temperate areas (where most Australians live) but decrease transmission in tropical areas where mean temperatures are already near the thermal optimum.

    **Implications:** Our findings highlight the importance of nonlinear models for inferring the role of temperature in disease dynamics and predicting responses to climate change. Identifying transmission optima and limits by characterizing nonlinear thermal responses, rather than simply assuming that transmission increases with temperature, can more accurately predict geographic, seasonal, and interannual variation in disease.

    **Conclusions:** This study demonstrates the value of mechanistic modeling in understanding the thermal biology of vector-borne diseases. Our results have important implications for predicting the impact of climate change on RRV transmission and highlight the need for further research on the thermal responses of other vector-borne pathogens. By characterizing nonlinear thermal responses, we can improve our understanding of disease dynamics and develop more effective strategies for disease control and prevention.

**Title:** Deciphering the Behavioral Correlates of Motor Cortical Beta Oscillations: A Neurofeedback Study in Macaques

**Research Question:** How are changes in population neural activity, beta oscillations, and behavior linked during motor tasks?

**Methods:** We recorded multi-scale neural activity from the motor cortex of three macaques while they performed a novel neurofeedback task, in which they volitionally brought their beta oscillatory power to an instructed state and subsequently executed an arm reach.

**Main Findings:** Our results show that reaches preceded by a reduction in beta power exhibited significantly faster movement onset times than reaches preceded by an increase in beta power. Furthermore, population neural activity was found to shift farther from a movement onset state during beta oscillations that were neurofeedback-induced or naturally occurring during reaching tasks.

**Implications:** These findings establish a population neural basis for slowed movement onset following periods of beta oscillatory activity, supporting the hypothesis that beta oscillations are linked to neural patterns that slow the onset of new movements (Gilbertson et al., 2005). Our results also add to previously reported findings of elevated beta power prior to and after well-trained movements (Donoghue et al., 1998; Murthy and Fetz, 1992; Sanes and Donoghue, 1993).

**Conclusions:** Our study ties together existing works on behavioral correlates of beta oscillations with hypotheses of how the motor cortex encodes movement onset through the lens of population-level neural activity. The behavioral effect from neurofeedback-induced beta oscillations matches well with hypotheses claiming a movement-slowing role of beta oscillations during natural movements (Gilbertson et al., 2005). Notably, individual unit responses during beta activity were different across tasks, but population-level analyses show that beta oscillations promote a consistent movement-slowing state during both the CO and NR tasks, matching what is observed behaviorally.

    **Title:** TRAF2 Regulates Keratinocyte Homeostasis and Prevents Psoriasis-like Skin Inflammation

    **Research Question:** What is the role of TRAF2 in regulating keratinocyte homeostasis and preventing psoriasis-like skin inflammation?

    **Methods:** To investigate the role of TRAF2 in keratinocytes, we generated mice with keratinocyte-specific deletion of Traf2 (Traf2EKO) and compared them to mice with keratinocyte-specific deletion of Sphk1 (Sphk1-/-). We also examined the effects of concomitant deletion of Tnf and Nfkb2 genes in Traf2EKO mice.

    **Implications:** Our results highlight the essential role of TRAF2 in regulating keratinocyte homeostasis and preventing psoriasis-like skin inflammation. TRAF2 protects keratinocytes from cell death and down-regulates inflammatory responses, suggesting that intrinsic defects in keratinocytes can initiate psoriasis-like skin inflammation.

    **Conclusions:** TRAF2-deficient keratinocytes do not require bacterial stimulation to produce TNF, and TNF-induced death sets up a vicious cycle of inflammation. Concomitant deletion of Tnf in mice with Traf2-deficient keratinocytes breaks this cycle and prevents apoptosis of keratinocytes and early onset of the psoriasis-like phenotype. Our findings support the idea that TRAF2 plays a crucial role in regulating keratinocyte homeostasis and preventing psoriasis-like skin inflammation, and suggest that TRAF2 may be a potential therapeutic target for the treatment of psoriasis.

    **Title:** Dynamin 1 and 3 are dispensable for bulk endocytosis and SV reformation from bulk endosomes

    **Research Question:** What is the role of dynamin 1 and 3 in the process of bulk endocytosis and synaptic vesicle (SV) reformation from bulk endosomes during intense synaptic activity?

    **Methods:** We used synapses of dynamin 1 and 3 double knock-out (DKO) neurons, where clathrin-mediated endocytosis (CME) is dramatically impaired, to investigate the mechanisms underlying bulk endocytosis and SV reformation.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that massive formation of bulk endosomes was not defective, but rather enhanced, in the absence of dynamin 1 and 3.     **Conclusions:** Our study reveals that dynamin 1 and 3 are dispensable for bulk endocytosis and SV reformation from bulk endosomes, challenging the prevailing view that clathrin and dynamin 1/3 are essential for these processes. Instead, our results suggest the existence of a clathrin- and dynamin-independent pathway for SV reformation, which is activated during intense synaptic activity. These findings have important implications for our understanding of synaptic function and plasticity, and highlight the need for further investigation into the mechanisms underlying SV reformation and recycling.

    **Title:** Identification of Circulating Biomarkers of Glucocorticoid Action in Humans

    **Research Question:** Can we identify and validate circulating biomarkers of glucocorticoid action in humans?

    **Methods:** In a randomized, crossover, single-blind study, 10 subjects with primary adrenal insufficiency were admitted to an in-patient clinic and studied during physiological glucocorticoid exposure and withdrawal.     **Title:** Dynamics of mTORC1 Activation in Response to Amino Acids Revealed by Live Imaging

    **Research Question:** How do amino acids regulate the activation of the mechanistic target of rapamycin complex 1 (mTORC1) in cells, and what is the role of lysosomes in this process?

    **Methods:** We used live imaging of the mTORC1 component RAPTOR and a cell-permeant fluorescent analogue of di-leucine methyl ester to examine the spatial and temporal dynamics of mTORC1 translocation to lysosomes in response to amino acid addition. We also generated a series of leucine analogues to test their ability to activate mTORC1.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that mTORC1 translocates to lysosomes within 2 minutes of amino acid addition and peaks within 5 minutes. This translocation is temporally coupled with the appearance of fluorescent leucine in lysosomes and is sustained compared to amino acid stimulation. We also found that Sestrin2 and the vacuolar ATPase are negative and positive regulators of mTORC1 activity, respectively. Notably, phosphorylation of canonical mTORC1 targets is delayed compared to lysosomal translocation, suggesting a dynamic and transient passage of mTORC1 from the lysosomal surface before targeting its substrates elsewhere.

    **Implications:** Our findings provide new insights into the dynamics of mTORC1 activation in response to amino acids and suggest that lysosomes play a crucial role in this process. The results also highlight the importance of Sestrin2 and the vacuolar ATPase in regulating mTORC1 activity.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides a dynamic view of mTORC1 activation in response to amino acids, revealing a complex interplay between lysosomes, Sestrin2, and the vacuolar ATPase. These findings have important implications for our understanding of the regulation of mTORC1 and its role in cellular processes such as growth, metabolism, and autophagy.

    **Title:** Modulating Antigen Uptake and Immune Response through Controlled Glycosylation of Antigens

Our findings have implications for understanding the evolution of cooperation and conflict in bacterial populations, and highlight the importance of cell-cell contact-dependent mechanisms in regulating bacterial behavior.

    **Title:** Post-transcriptional regulation of satellite cell quiescence by Tristetraprolin (TTP)

    **Research Question:** How do skeletal muscle satellite cells (SCs) maintain quiescence in their niche, and what mechanisms are involved in their activation and self-renewal following muscle injury?

    **Methods:** To investigate the mechanisms of satellite cell quiescence, we identified gene transcripts that were differentially expressed during satellite cell activation following muscle injury. We found that transcripts encoding RNA binding proteins, including Tristetraprolin (TTP), were among the most significantly changed.

    **Main Findings:** We discovered that TTP promotes the decay of MyoD mRNA, a transcriptional regulator of myogenic commitment, via binding to the MyoD mRNA 3' untranslated region. Satellite cell-specific knockdown of TTP precociously activates satellite cells in vivo, enabling MyoD accumulation, differentiation, and cell fusion into myofibers.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that regulation of mRNAs by TTP is a critical post-transcriptional regulatory mechanism controlling satellite cell homeostasis. Furthermore, TTP inactivation preceding MyoD up-regulation is consistent with the hypothesis that TTP actively suppresses pro-myogenic mRNA targets.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals a novel mechanism of post-transcriptional regulation of satellite cell quiescence by TTP. We propose that TTP suppresses transcripts for SC activation as a mechanism for immediate exit from quiescence, and that loss of TTP from skeletal muscle tissue should precociously activate SCs, promoting inappropriate MyoD expression and aberrant cell cycle entry. These findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms controlling satellite cell homeostasis and muscle regeneration, and highlight the importance of post-transcriptional regulation of RNA in skeletal muscle diseases.

    **Title:** Macrophage-Intrinsic Interleukin 10 Receptor Signaling Prevents Infantile-Onset Colitis in Mice

    **Research Question:** Does interleukin 10 receptor (IL10R) signaling regulate lamina propria macrophage function during infant development in mice, and is macrophage-intrinsic IL10R signaling required to prevent colitis in infancy?

The results also indicate that IL10R signaling does not directly regulate the microbial ecology during infant development.

**Title:** Bistability in Purkinje Neurons of Larval Zebrafish: A Conserved Feature of Cerebellar Function

**Research Question:** Do Purkinje neurons exhibit bistability in vivo in unanesthetized larval zebrafish, and if so, what are the mechanisms underlying this phenomenon?

**Methods:** We recorded intracellularly from Purkinje neurons in unanesthetized larval zebrafish (Danio rerio) from 4 to 19 days post-fertilization (dpf) to investigate the membrane potential dynamics of these neurons in vivo.

**Main Findings:** Our results demonstrate that Purkinje neurons in larval zebrafish exhibit bistability, characterized by tonic firing at depolarized membrane potentials and bursting at hyperpolarized membrane potentials. This bistability is dependent on AMPA receptor (AMPAR)-mediated inputs from climbing fibers (CFs). We also show that the initiation of motor neuron spiking is correlated with increased incidence of CF excitatory postsynaptic potentials (EPSPs) and membrane depolarization. Furthermore, bistability is observed soon after Purkinje neuron specification and persists at least until late larval stages.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that bistability is a conserved feature of Purkinje neurons across species, from fish to mammals. This is supported by the conservation of other structural and functional properties of the cerebellum. The ability of Purkinje neurons to switch between tonic and bursting modes in vivo has important implications for our understanding of cerebellar function and motor control.

**Conclusions:** This study provides the first direct evidence of bistability in Purkinje neurons in vivo in an unanesthetized animal model. Our results demonstrate that AMPAR-mediated CF inputs are sufficient to trigger bursts in Purkinje neurons, and that bistability is a conserved feature of Purkinje neurons from fish to mammals. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of cerebellar function and motor control, and highlight the importance of further investigation into the mechanisms underlying bistability in Purkinje neurons.

    **Title:** Force Generation by Actin Assembly Shapes Cellular Membranes: A Multiscale Model of Endocytic Internalization

    **Research Question:** Can a minimal branched actin network generate sufficient force to internalize endocytic pits against mammalian plasma membrane tension?

    **Methods:** We combined a continuum membrane mechanics model with filament-based simulations of actin polymerization coupled to endocytic pit internalization to develop a multiscale model of actin in mammalian endocytosis. We used a newly developed molecule-counting method to determine the number of Arp2/3 complexes (~200) that accumulate at clathrin-mediated endocytosis sites over time.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that a minimal branched actin network is sufficient to create sustained internalization of an endocytic pit against physiological membrane tension. Actin filament self-organization and bending, which arise from the spatial distribution of actin-coat attachments around the curved endocytic pit, allow the actin network to adapt to changing loads. Simulations predict that actin self-organizes into a radial branched array with growing ends oriented toward the base of the pit. Long actin filaments bend between attachment sites in the coat and the base of the pit, storing elastic energy that contributes to endocytic internalization.

    **Implications:** Elevated membrane tension directs more growing filaments toward the base of the pit, increasing actin nucleation and bending for increased force production. Our findings suggest that spatially constrained actin filament assembly utilizes an adaptive mechanism enabling endocytosis under varying physical constraints.

    **Conclusions:** We demonstrate that a minimal actin network composed of actin, the Arp2/3 complex, and capping protein, with linker attachments in the clathrin coat and rates constrained by cellular and biochemical measurements, is able to generate sufficient force to internalize endocytic pits against mammalian plasma membrane tension. Our multiscale model provides a mechanistic understanding of how actin assembly shapes cellular membranes during endocytosis, highlighting the importance of actin filament self-organization and bending in generating force for endocytic internalization.

    **Title:** Self-Organizing Signaling Patches Drive Macropinocytic Cup Formation in Dictyostelium

    **Conclusions:** Our study proposes a new and general hypothesis for the formation of cups from the plasma membrane, highlighting the importance of self-organizing signaling patches in driving macropinocytic cup formation.     **Title:** Growth, Fattening, and Hibernation Patterns in Early-Born and Late-Born Garden Dormice (Eliomys quercinus)

    Title: Mechanisms of Influenza A Virus-Induced Host Shutoff Revealed by RNA-Sequencing and Ribosome Profiling

    **Title:** Unexpected Formation of a Novel α/β Coiled Coil Structure through Minor Mutations to a Known Fold

    **Research Question:** How do insertions of two or six residues into the heptad repeat of a parallel, trimeric coiled coil affect the backbone structure of the protein?

    **Methods:** Using parametric equations to describe the backbone structure of coiled coils, we inserted two or six residues into the heptad repeat of a parallel, trimeric coiled coil and analyzed the resulting structures.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that the insertion of two or six residues into the heptad repeat of a parallel, trimeric coiled coil strains the supercoil to the breaking point, causing the local formation of short β-strands. These β-strands move the path of the chain by 120° around the trimer axis, resulting in the formation of a novel α/β coiled coil structure. This structure retains only one backbone hydrogen bond per repeat unit from the parent coiled coil.

    **Implications:** Our findings demonstrate that a substantially novel backbone structure is possible within the allowed regions of the Ramachandran space with only minor mutations to a known fold. This has significant implications for protein design efforts, as it suggests that even small changes to a protein structure can lead to the formation of new and unexpected structures.

    **Conclusions:** The discovery of the α/β coiled coil structure expands our understanding of coiled coil periodicities and highlights the importance of considering the effects of insertions on protein structure. The α/β coiled coil is a new class of protein fiber based on a novel supersecondary structure element, characterized by alternating α- and β-layers. This finding opens up new avenues for protein design and engineering, and highlights the complexity and diversity of protein structures that can arise from minor mutations to a known fold.

    Background: Asthma aetiology in Africa remains poorly understood, with limited data on risk factors among schoolchildren. This study aimed to investigate the main risk factors for asthma among schoolchildren (5–17 years) in urban Uganda.

    Methods: We conducted a case-control study among 555 cases and 1115 controls. Asthma was diagnosed by study clinicians. We analyzed the association between asthma and various risk factors, including parental education, area of residence at birth, parental history of asthma, children’s own allergic conditions, atopy, and cooking methods.

    Main Findings: Our results show that asthma was strongly associated with a rural-town-city risk gradient, higher parental socio-economic status, and urbanicity. Specifically, children born in small towns or cities had an increased risk of asthma compared to those born in rural areas (adjusted OR: 2.16, 95% CI: 1.60–2.92 and 2.79, 95% CI: 1.79–4.35, respectively). Parental education, particularly tertiary education, was also a significant risk factor (adjusted OR: 2.32, 95% CI: 1.71–3.16 for fathers and 1.85, 95% CI: 1.38–2.48 for mothers). Additionally, children’s own allergic conditions, atopy, and cooking on gas/electricity were associated with an increased risk of asthma.

    Implications: This study highlights the importance of early life environmental and lifestyle factors in the development of asthma among schoolchildren in urban Uganda. The rapid urbanization in Africa and other low- and middle-income countries (LMICs) is likely to increase the prevalence of asthma. Our findings provide a basis for future studies to investigate specific environmental and lifestyle factors contributing to asthma risk in urban areas of LMICs.

    Conclusions: This case-control study demonstrates that asthma among schoolchildren in urban Uganda is associated with a strong rural-town-city risk gradient, higher parental socio-economic status, and urbanicity. Our results underscore the need for further research to identify specific environmental and lifestyle factors responsible for increasing asthma risk among children in urban areas of LMICs.

    **Title:** Heteromeric Gap Junctions Formed by Connexin 41.8 and Connexin 39.4 are Required for Stripe Pattern Formation in Zebrafish

    **Research Question:** What is the molecular nature of the interactions between pigment cell types that form the stripe pattern of adult zebrafish?

    **Methods:** We used a combination of genetic and molecular approaches to investigate the role of Connexin 41.8 (Cx41.8) and Connexin 39.4 (Cx39.4) in stripe pattern formation in zebrafish. We identified a new dominant allele, leotK3, which leads to a complete loss of the pattern, suggesting a dominant negative impact on another component of gap junctions. We performed a genetic screen to identify this component and found that Cx39.4 is required for stripe formation in zebrafish.

    **Main Findings:** Double mutants and chimeras showed that Cx41.8 and Cx39.4 are only required in xanthophores and melanophores, but not in iridophores, suggesting that both connexins form heteromeric gap junctions. The phenotypes indicate that these channels promote homotypic interactions between melanophores and xanthophores, respectively, and instruct the patterning of the iridophores.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that heteromeric gap junctions formed by Cx41.8 and Cx39.4 play a crucial role in stripe pattern formation in zebrafish. These channels are responsible for some of the interactions that take place among chromatophores while the pigment pattern is established.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the molecular nature of the interactions between pigment cell types that form the stripe pattern of adult zebrafish. The identification of Cx39.4 as a component of heteromeric gap junctions with Cx41.8 sheds light on the complex mechanisms underlying pigment pattern formation in zebrafish. These findings have implications for our understanding of the development and evolution of pigment patterns in vertebrates.

    **Title:** Evolutionary Origins of Olfactory Receptors in Insects: A Comparative Study of Apterogote and Neopteran Insects

    **Research Question:** What is the evolutionary history of olfactory receptors in insects, and how did they adapt to terrestrial environments?

    **Methods:** We investigated the olfactory systems of three insect species: Lepismachilis y-signata (Archaeognatha), Thermobia domestica (Zygentoma), and Phyllium siccifolium (Phasmatodea). We used molecular biology techniques to identify and characterize olfactory receptors (ORs) and the olfactory coreceptor (Orco) in these species.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that Lepismachilis lacks ORs and Orco, while Thermobia has three Orco candidates, and Phyllium has a fully developed OR/Orco-based system. These findings suggest that ORs did not evolve as an adaptation to a terrestrial lifestyle, but rather emerged later in insect evolution, with Orco being present before the appearance of ORs.

    **Implications:** Our study provides new insights into the evolution of the insect olfactory system and challenges the hypothesis that ORs arose as an adaptation to a terrestrial lifestyle. Instead, our results suggest that ORs evolved after the emergence of wings, and that Orco was present before the appearance of ORs. This has significant implications for our understanding of the evolutionary history of insect olfaction.

    **Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates that the evolution of the insect olfactory system is more complex than previously thought. The presence of Orco before the appearance of ORs suggests that Orco played a key role in the evolution of the insect olfactory system. Our findings also highlight the importance of studying basal insects to understand the evolution of olfaction in insects and other animals. Further research is needed to fully elucidate the evolutionary history of the insect olfactory system and its adaptations to terrestrial environments.
Title: Spatial and Temporal Analysis of Malaria Episodes and Asymptomatic Infections in Myanmar: Evidence for a Herd Effect in Mass Drug Administration

    Abstract:

    Background: Malaria elimination efforts are hindered by asymptomatic infections, which are not diagnosed or treated. Mass drug administration (MDA) has been proposed as a solution, but its effectiveness depends on adequate population participation. We investigated the spatial and temporal patterns of malaria episodes and asymptomatic infections in four villages undergoing MDA in Myanmar.

    Methods: We conducted a detailed analysis of micro-epidemiological and spatial data from an MDA trial in Kayin State, Myanmar. We examined the associations between individual- and group-level participation in MDA, subclinical infections, and clinical episodes of Plasmodium falciparum post-MDA.

    Main Findings: Our results show that individuals from neighborhoods with low MDA adherence had 2.85 times the odds of having a malaria episode post-MDA compared to those from high adherence neighborhoods, suggesting a herd effect. High mosquito biting rates, living in a house with someone else with malaria, or having an asymptomatic malaria infection were also predictors of clinical episodes. Spatial clustering of non-adherence to MDA, even in villages with high overall participation, may frustrate elimination efforts.

    Implications: Our study provides evidence for a herd effect conferred by MDA for P. falciparum malaria, which has important implications for malaria elimination efforts. The findings suggest that MDA can provide additional levels of population protection beyond individual participation. However, spatial clustering of non-adherence to MDA can undermine the effectiveness of MDA, highlighting the need for targeted interventions to improve adherence.

    Conclusions: This study provides the first empirical evidence for a herd effect in MDA for P. falciparum malaria. The results have significant implications for malaria elimination efforts and highlight the importance of addressing spatial clustering of non-adherence to MDA. Further research is needed to explore the mechanisms underlying the herd effect and to develop effective strategies to improve MDA adherence and ultimately achieve malaria elimination.

    Abstract:

    Background: The evolution of divergent morphology in species has been attributed to genetic differences in tissue-specific expression of proteins, which can arise from divergence in cis-regulatory elements or altered binding specificity of transcription factors (TFs). However, the relative importance of the latter has been difficult to assess.

    Research Question: What is the extent of conservation and divergence of TF binding specificities between Drosophila and mammals, and how do these differences contribute to the emergence of novel cell types?

    Methods: We used high-throughput SELEX (HT-SELEX) to determine the binding specificities of 242 Drosophila TFs and compared them to human and mouse data.

    Main Findings: Our analysis revealed a striking level of conservation of TF binding specificity between Drosophila and mammals, with almost identical core DNA binding profiles, flanking sequences, and secondary modes of binding for orthologous TFs. However, a few human TFs with divergent specificities function in cell types not found in fruit flies, suggesting that evolution of TF specificities contributes to the emergence of novel types of differentiated cells.

    Implications: Our findings suggest that TF binding specificities are highly conserved between Drosophila and mammals, and that subtle changes in these specificities can lead to the emergence of novel cell types. This has important implications for our understanding of the evolution of animal diversity and the development of novel cell types.

    Conclusions: Our study provides a comprehensive analysis of TF binding specificities in Drosophila and sheds light on the evolution of novel cell types. The conservation of TF binding specificities between Drosophila and mammals highlights the importance of these specificities in maintaining cellular identity and function. Our findings also suggest that subtle changes in TF binding specificities can lead to the emergence of novel cell types, providing new insights into the evolution of animal diversity.
Title: Simultaneous Induction of Cardiac and Pulmonary Lineages from Human-Induced Pluripotent Stem Cells via Temporal Tuning of WNT and Nodal Signaling

    Abstract:
    During human embryogenesis, the developing heart and lung engage in extensive crosstalk to ensure proper morphogenesis and maturation. However, a lack of models investigating this critical cardio-pulmonary interaction has hindered our understanding of this process. Here, we report a novel stepwise strategy for directing the simultaneous induction of cardiac and pulmonary lineages from human-induced pluripotent stem cells (hiPSCs) via temporal-specific tuning of WNT and Nodal signaling in the absence of exogenous growth factors. Using 3D suspension culture, we established concentric cardio-pulmonary micro-Tissues (μTs) and expedited alveolar maturation in the presence of cardiac accompaniment. Upon withdrawal of WNT agonist, the cardiac and pulmonary components within each dual-lineage μT effectively segregated from each other with concurrent initiation of cardiac contraction. Our multilineage differentiation model offers an experimentally tractable system for investigating human cardio-pulmonary interaction and tissue boundary formation during embryogenesis.

    Research Question:
    Can human-induced pluripotent stem cells (hiPSCs) be directed to simultaneously differentiate into cardiac and pulmonary lineages via temporal-specific tuning of WNT and Nodal signaling?

    Methods:
    We employed a stepwise strategy to induce cardiac and pulmonary lineages from hiPSCs using small molecule cocktails modulating WNT, Nodal, and TGF-β signaling in a temporal-specific manner. 3D suspension culture was used to establish concentric cardio-pulmonary micro-Tissues (μTs).

    Main Findings:
    We demonstrated the simultaneous induction of cardiac and pulmonary lineages from hiPSCs via temporal-specific tuning of WNT and Nodal signaling. In 3D suspension culture, each cardio-pulmonary μT effectively segregated into separate cardiac and pulmonary μTs, which was partially inhibited by WNT activation. Upon withdrawal of WNT agonist, each concentric cardio-pulmonary μT reorganized and ultimately segregated into cardiac-only and pulmonary-only μTs.

    Implications:
    Our multilineage differentiation model offers an experimentally tractable system for investigating human cardio-pulmonary interaction and tissue boundary formation during embryogenesis. This model has the potential to elucidate the molecular and cellular mechanisms underlying human cardio-pulmonary co-development and tissue boundary formation.

    Conclusions:
    Our study provides a novel stepwise strategy for directing the simultaneous induction of cardiac and pulmonary lineages from hiPSCs via temporal-specific tuning of WNT and Nodal signaling. Our multilineage differentiation model offers a powerful tool for investigating human cardio-pulmonary interaction and tissue boundary formation during embryogenesis, and has the potential to shed light on the molecular and cellular mechanisms underlying human cardio-pulmonary co-development.

    **Title:** Unexpected Flexibility in Cellular Mutation Rates as a Response to Changes in Selective Pressure

    **Research Question:** How do organisms regulate their mutation rates in response to changes in selective pressure, particularly under near-lethal stress conditions?

    **Methods:** We employed a model system of Escherichia coli (E. coli) exposed to high ethanol stress to investigate the dynamics of mutation rate evolution. We used a defined collection of mutators with distinct mutation rates to identify the range of optimal mutation rates for rapid growth under high ethanol stress. Experimental evolution revealed the essential role of hypermutation in de novo adaptation to high ethanol stress.

    **Main Findings:** Our results demonstrate an unexpected flexibility in cellular mutation rates as a response to changes in selective pressure. We show that hypermutation independently evolves when E. coli cultures adapt to high ethanol stress, and that hypermutator states are transitory, repeatedly alternating with decreases in mutation rate. Specifically, population mutation rates rise when cells experience higher stress and decline again once cells are adapted. We identified cellular mortality as the major force driving the quick evolution of mutation rates.

    **Implications:** These findings provide new insights into the dynamics of mutation rate evolution and help explain the prevalence of hypermutation in various settings, including the emergence of antibiotic resistance in microbes and cancer relapses upon chemotherapy. Our results suggest that organisms balance robustness and evolvability by regulating their mutation rates in response to changes in selective pressure.

    **Conclusions:** While an organism's mutation rate is generally considered a slowly-evolving parameter, our study reveals an unexpected flexibility in cellular mutation rates that matches changes in selective pressure to avoid extinction under near-lethal stress. Our findings shed new light on the mechanisms underlying the evolution of mutation rates and highlight the importance of cellular mortality in driving the quick evolution of mutation rates. These results have significant implications for our understanding of the evolution of antibiotic resistance and cancer relapse.

    **Title:** The Arabidopsis Transcription Factor bZIP63 as a Direct Target of the SnRK1 Kinase in the Transcriptional Energy Deprivation Response

    **Research Question:** What is the role of the Arabidopsis transcription factor bZIP63 in the transcriptional energy deprivation response, and how is it regulated by the SnRK1 kinase?

    **Methods:** We used a combination of genetic and biochemical approaches to investigate the function of bZIP63 in Arabidopsis thaliana. We generated a bzip63 knock-out mutant and complemented it with wild-type bZIP63 and a version harboring point mutations in the identified SnRK1 target sites. We also overexpressed bZIP63-GFP or -YFP in the wild-type and akin10 backgrounds to verify the role of SnRK1 in bZIP63 phosphorylation.

    **Main Findings:** We identified bZIP63 as a key regulator of the starvation response in Arabidopsis and found that it is a direct target of the SnRK1 kinase. Phosphorylation of bZIP63 by SnRK1 changed its dimerization preference, affecting target gene expression and ultimately primary metabolism. The bzip63 knock-out mutant exhibited starvation-related phenotypes, which could be functionally complemented by wild-type bZIP63 but not by a version harboring point mutations in the identified SnRK1 target sites. We also found that the SnRK1 kinase AKIN10 is the major kinase responsible for the starvation-induced hyper-phosphorylation of bZIP63.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that bZIP63 plays a crucial role in the transcriptional energy deprivation response in Arabidopsis and that its phosphorylation by SnRK1 is essential for its function. We propose a molecular model for a phosphorylation-triggered switch of bZIP63 dimerization partners, which ultimately regulates metabolic reprogramming.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the regulation of the transcriptional energy deprivation response in plants and highlights the importance of bZIP63 as a key regulator of primary metabolism under stress/starvation conditions. The identification of SnRK1 as a major kinase responsible for bZIP63 phosphorylation opens up new avenues for understanding the molecular mechanisms underlying metabolic adjustment to changing environmental conditions.

    **Title:** RNF10: A Novel Synaptonuclear Protein Messenger Linking Synaptic Activity to Nuclear Gene Expression

    **Research Question:** What is the role of RNF10 in bidirectional communication between synapses and the nucleus, and how does it contribute to long-term potentiation (LTP) and structural modifications of dendritic spines?

    **Methods:** This study employed a combination of biochemical, cellular, and molecular biology techniques to investigate the role of RNF10 in synaptonuclear communication. Immunofluorescence, Western blotting, and co-immunoprecipitation were used to examine the subcellular localization and interactions of RNF10 with the GluN2A subunit of NMDA receptors (NMDARs).

    **Main Findings:** We identified RNF10 as a novel synaptonuclear protein messenger that is activated by calcium signals at the postsynaptic compartment and elicits discrete changes at the transcriptional level. RNF10 is enriched at excitatory synapses, where it associates with the GluN2A subunit of NMDARs. Activation of synaptic GluN2A-containing NMDARs and induction of LTP lead to the translocation of RNF10 from dendritic segments and dendritic spines to the nucleus via importin-dependent long-distance transport. Notably, RNF10 silencing prevents the maintenance of LTP and LTP-dependent structural modifications of dendritic spines.

    **Implications:** Our findings provide evidence for a novel synapse-to-nucleus signaling pathway that specifically links activation of synaptic GluN2A-containing NMDARs to nuclear gene expression. This pathway is essential for the long-lasting re-shaping of dendritic spines and the induction of LTP.

    **Conclusions:** RNF10 is a novel synaptonuclear protein messenger that plays a critical role in bidirectional communication between synapses and the nucleus. Its activation by calcium signals at the postsynaptic compartment and subsequent translocation to the nucleus via importin-dependent transport enables the encoding and transduction of synaptic and extrasynaptic origin of GluN2B NMDAR signals to the nucleus. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of synaptic plasticity and the molecular mechanisms underlying learning and memory.

    **Title:** Structural Insights into Swi2/Snf2 ATPase-Mediated DNA Remodeling: A Pseudoatomic View of Mot1 in Complex with TBP, DNA, and NC2

    **Research Question:** How do Swi2/Snf2 ATPases, such as Mot1, remodel protein-DNA complexes to control DNA-associated processes?

    **Methods:** We employed X-ray crystallography, electron microscopy, and cross-linking studies to determine the structure of the N-terminal domain of Mot1 in complex with TATA box-binding protein (TBP), DNA, and the transcription regulator negative cofactor 2 (NC2) at 3.8 Å resolution.

    **Main Findings:** Our data reveal that Mot1 reduces DNA-NC2 interactions and unbends DNA compared to the TBP-DNA-NC2 state, suggesting that Mot1 primes TBP-NC2 displacement in an ATP-independent manner. Electron microscopy and cross-linking studies suggest that the Swi2/Snf2 domain of Mot1 associates with the upstream DNA and the histone fold of NC2, revealing parallels to some nucleosome remodelers.

    **Implications:** Our findings provide a structural framework for understanding how a Swi2/Snf2 ATPase interacts with its substrate DNA-protein complex. The association of Mot1 with the TBP-DNA-NC2 complex results in conformational changes that may have implications for understanding the ATP-dependent dissociation mechanism.

    **Conclusions:** This study provides the first pseudoatomic view of a Swi2/Snf2 ATPase in complex with a DNA-protein substrate complex, shedding light on the structural mechanisms of Swi2/Snf2 ATPases. Our results suggest that Mot1 primes TBP-NC2 displacement in an ATP-independent manner, and the Swi2/Snf2 domain of Mot1 associates with the upstream DNA and the histone fold of NC2, revealing parallels to some nucleosome remodelers. These findings contribute to our understanding of how Swi2/Snf2 ATPases regulate the structure and epigenetic state of chromatin regions during transcription, DNA replication, and DNA repair.

    **Title:** Elucidating the Molecular Mode of Action of Cytotoxic Cyclic Peptides: Targeting the Eukaryotic Elongation Factor-1A Ternary Complex

    **Research Question:** What is the molecular target of the cytotoxic natural product ternatin, and how do its synthetic variants interact with this target to inhibit cellular processes?

    **Methods:** We designed and synthesized structural variants of ternatin, a cytotoxic and anti-adipogenic natural product, and evaluated their potency against cancer cells. Using a photo-affinity probe, we identified the translation elongation factor-1A ternary complex (eEF1A·GTP·aminoacyl-tRNA) as a specific target of ternatin and its variants.     **Main Findings:** Our results show that ternatin and its variants inhibit cellular protein synthesis with potencies that correlate with their ability to block cell proliferation. Mutations in domain III of eEF1A prevent ternatin binding and confer resistance to its cytotoxic effects, implicating the adjacent hydrophobic surface as a functional hot spot for eEF1A modulation.

    **Implications:** Our study reveals that the eukaryotic elongation factor-1A and its ternary complex with GTP and aminoacyl-tRNA are common targets for the evolution of cytotoxic natural products. This finding has significant implications for the development of new cancer therapies and highlights the potential of cyclic peptides as leads for medicinal chemistry efforts.

    **Conclusions:** We conclude that the eEF1A ternary complex is a specific target of ternatin and its variants, and that mutations in domain III of eEF1A prevent ternatin binding and confer resistance to its cytotoxic effects. Our study provides new insights into the molecular mode of action of cytotoxic cyclic peptides and highlights the importance of the eEF1A ternary complex as a target for the development of new cancer therapies.

    **Title:** Loss of OCRL Function Disrupts Clathrin-Mediated Endocytosis in Lowe Syndrome Patient Fibroblasts

    **Research Question:** What is the role of OCRL in clathrin-mediated endocytosis, and how do mutations in OCRL contribute to the pathology of Lowe syndrome?

    **Methods:** We investigated the impact of OCRL loss on clathrin-mediated endocytosis in Lowe syndrome patient fibroblasts using time-lapse microscopy, immunofluorescence, and biochemical assays.

Our findings suggest that defects in clathrin-mediated endocytosis are a key contributor to the pathology of Lowe syndrome, and that the loss of OCRL function is a critical factor in this process. These results have significant implications for our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying Lowe syndrome and may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies for the treatment of this disease.

    **Title:** Disengagement of Vestibular Balance Stabilizing Mechanisms during Motor State Transitions in Humans

    **Research Question:** Do humans interrupt vestibular balance stabilizing mechanisms to transition between motor states, as predicted by optimal feedback theory?

    **Methods:** We investigated the continuity of vestibular control of balance during transitions between quiet standing and locomotion and between two standing postures in healthy subjects. Participants initiated and terminated locomotion or shifted the distribution of their weight between their feet while exposed to electrical vestibular stimuli (EVS). The relationship between EVS and ground reaction forces was quantified using time-frequency analyses.

    **Main Findings:** Discontinuities corresponding to null coherence periods were observed preceding the onset of movement initiation and during the step preceding locomotion termination. These results suggest that humans interrupt the vestibular balance stabilizing mechanisms to transition between motor states.

    **Implications:** Our findings support the optimal feedback theory, which proposes that transitions between motor states require the disengagement of one motor control policy before the implementation of another. The suspension of vestibular-evoked responses at movement initiation starts while subjects are still maintaining quiet upright stance, indicating a general control process that occurs during transitions between standing balance and other motor states.

    **Conclusions:** Our results demonstrate that humans "stop balancing" before they start moving and "stop moving" before they start balancing again. The period of suspension of vestibular-evoked responses appears related to the different relative sensory feedback gains needed to control whole-body movements by the quiet standing and locomotor control policies. This study provides new insights into the neural control of motor state transitions and has implications for understanding the neural mechanisms underlying balance control in humans. The findings suggest that the vestibular system plays a critical role in regulating balance during transitions between motor states, and that the disengagement of vestibular balance stabilizing mechanisms is a general control process that occurs during transitions between standing balance and other motor states.

    **Title:** Circadian Regulation of Carbon Metabolism in Cyanobacteria: A Critical Role for RpaA in Anticipating Periodic Energy Limitations

    **Research Question:** How does the circadian output pathway, mediated by the transcription factor RpaA, control and temporally coordinate essential pathways in carbon metabolism to maximize fitness of cyanobacteria facing periodic energy limitations?

    **Methods:** We investigated the mechanisms underlying the viability defect of a cyanobacterial strain deleted for the rpaA gene, which encodes the master regulator of circadian transcription. We analyzed the effects of rpaA deletion on energy status, carbon reserve accumulation, and transcription of genes crucial for carbohydrate utilization at night.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that the rpaA- strain cannot maintain appropriate energy status at night, does not accumulate carbon reserves during the day, and is defective in transcription of genes crucial for utilization of carbohydrate stores at night. Reconstruction of carbon utilization pathways combined with provision of an external carbon source restores energy charge and viability of the rpaA- strain in light/dark cycling conditions.

    **Implications:** Our findings provide insight into the role of the circadian output pathway in enhancing fitness of cyanobacteria through coordination of central carbon metabolism with the metabolic demands imposed by periodic changes in the external environment. We demonstrate that functional circadian output mediated by RpaA is required for the anticipatory accumulation of carbon reserves during the day, as well as metabolic switching to carbon catabolism at dusk.

    **Conclusions:** Our results strongly suggest that RpaA plays a critical role in coordinating carbon anabolism and catabolism to prepare cells for the metabolic demands of periods of darkness. The deletion of rpaA prevents glycogen accumulation in light and renders cells unable to utilize existing glycogen at dusk, leading to alterations in cellular energy charge and reduced fitness. Our data support a model in which anticipation of periodic dark-induced resource limitation is one essential role of the cyanobacterial circadian system.

    **Title:** Structural and Functional Insights into the PIB-4-ATPase sCoaT from Sulfitobacter sp. NAS14-1

    **Research Question:** What is the structure and mechanism of the PIB-4-ATPase sCoaT from Sulfitobacter sp. NAS14-1, and how does it transport heavy metals across cellular membranes?

Our results also have implications for drug discovery, as PIB-4-ATPases function as virulence factors in many pathogens.

Our findings open up new avenues for the development of tools to manipulate heavy-metal accumulation in plants and provide promising leads for drug-discovery efforts to combat pathogens that rely on PIB-4-ATPases for virulence.

    Title: Identification of a Chromatin Signature for Mitotically Stable Monoallelic Expression (MAE) in Human Cells

    Background: Mitotically stable monoallelic expression (MAE) is a phenomenon where a single allele of an autosomal gene is expressed in a cell, while the other allele is silenced. Despite its importance in various human diseases, the regulation of MAE remains poorly understood due to its clonal nature.

    Research Question: Can we identify a chromatin signature that distinguishes MAE genes in human cells and sheds light on its regulation?

    Methods: We developed a novel approach to detect MAE genes by analyzing chromatin marks associated with active transcription (H3K36me3) and silencing (H3K27me3) simultaneously occurring in the gene body. We applied this approach to a variety of cell types and estimated that up to 20% of ubiquitously expressed genes and more than one-third of tissue-specific genes showed the chromatin signature of MAE.

    Main Findings: Our results reveal that the MAE signature is enriched among key developmental genes that have bivalent chromatin structure in pluripotent cells. Notably, we found that a remarkably large fraction of known bivalent genes (>80%) acquire the MAE chromatin signature in at least one of the differentiated cell types. This suggests that upon activation, these master regulator genes preferentially resolve into a state with the characteristic MAE chromatin signature.

    Implications: Our findings open a new approach to the study of MAE that is independent of polymorphisms, making it feasible to analyze MAE in primary samples, including systematic comparisons of normal and diseased tissue. This has significant implications for understanding the regulation of MAE and its role in human diseases.

    Conclusions: Our study identifies a chromatin signature that distinguishes MAE genes in human cells, providing new insights into the regulation of this phenomenon. The MAE signature is linked to cell differentiation and is enriched among key developmental genes. These findings have important implications for understanding the molecular mechanisms underlying MAE and its role in human diseases.

    **Title:** Coiled-Coil Middle Domains Regulate AAA+ Chaperone Activity in Bacteria

    **Research Question:** How do coiled-coil middle domains (MDs) control the activity of AAA+ chaperones, such as ClpC, in bacteria?

    **Methods:** We used cryo-electron microscopy (cryo-EM) to determine the structures of ClpC with and without the adaptor protein MecA. We also generated MD mutants of ClpC and ClpE to investigate the role of MDs in regulating AAA+ chaperone activity.

    **Main Findings:** We discovered that ClpC forms large two-helical assemblies via head-to-head contacts between coiled-coil MDs, which sequester ClpC molecules in an inactive resting state. MecA binding to MD interaction sites converts this resting state to an active planar ring structure, stimulating ClpC ATPase activity. Loss of ClpC repression in MD mutants causes constitutive activation and severe cellular toxicity.

    **Implications:** Our findings reveal a novel regulatory mechanism executed by coiled-coil MDs to tightly control AAA+ chaperone activity in bacteria. This mechanism is distinct from other AAA+ chaperones, such as ClpB/Hsp104, where MDs form a repressing belt around the AAA ring. Our results suggest that coiled-coil MDs are a general mechanism for controlling bacterial AAA+ chaperone systems.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the regulation of AAA+ chaperones in bacteria and highlights the importance of coiled-coil MDs in controlling their activity. The severe toxicity of ClpC and ClpE MD mutants suggests that these AAA+ chaperones are potential targets for antimicrobial therapies. Our findings have significant implications for understanding the molecular mechanisms of bacterial stress resistance and virulence, and for the development of novel antimicrobial strategies.

    **Title:** Niche-Driven Insulin-Notch-dMyc Cascade Regulates the Reactivation of Adult Muscle Precursors in Drosophila

    **Research Question:** How do adult muscle precursors (AMPs), the transient fruit fly muscle stem cells, maintain their non-differentiated quiescent state and how are they reactivated during development?

    **Methods:** We employed a Drosophila model to investigate the in vivo behavior of AMPs, focusing on their interactions with neighboring muscles. We used genetic analyses to examine the role of muscles in AMP reactivation and proliferation.

    **Main Findings:** Our study reveals that emerging AMPs send out thin filopodia that make contact with neighboring muscles, suggesting that muscles could play a role in AMP reactivation. We found that muscles send inductive dIlp6 signals that switch the Insulin pathway ON in closely associated AMPs, leading to the activation of Notch, which regulates AMP proliferation via dMyc. Notably, dMyc acts as a positive regulator of AMP reactivation, indicating that the proliferation of muscle stem cells is regulated by the same set of genes in both Drosophila and vertebrates.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that muscles behave as an AMP niche, providing local inductive Insulin signals that reactivate AMPs and induce their proliferation in larval stages. The Insulin-Notch-dMyc cascade plays a key role in setting the activated state of AMPs, highlighting the importance of the muscle niche in regulating muscle stem cell behavior.

    **Conclusions:** This study provides new insights into the mechanisms governing the reactivation of adult muscle precursors in Drosophila. Our results demonstrate that the muscle niche plays a driving role in AMP reactivation and proliferation, and that the Insulin-Notch-dMyc cascade is a key regulatory pathway in this process. These findings have implications for our understanding of muscle stem cell biology and muscle regeneration in both Drosophila and vertebrates.

    **Title:** Identification of the Sodium Leak Channel NALCN as a Critical Regulator of Tonic Firing in Substantia Nigra Pars Reticulata Neurons

    **Research Question:** What is the molecular basis of the depolarizing leak current that supports the spontaneous firing of substantia nigra pars reticulata (SNr) neurons, and how does it contribute to basal ganglia circuit function?

    **Methods:** We employed single-cell RNA sequencing to determine the transcriptome of individual mouse SNr neurons and identified the sodium leak channel NALCN as the highest expressed nonselective cation channel (NSCC) in these cells.

    **Main Findings:** We found that SNr neurons lacking NALCN have impaired spontaneous firing, suggesting that NALCN is essential for the maintenance of tonic activity in these neurons. Furthermore, we discovered that NALCN is involved in the modulation of excitability by changes in glycolysis and by activation of muscarinic acetylcholine receptors.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that disruption of NALCN could impair the basal ganglia circuit, which may underlie the severe motor deficits observed in humans carrying mutations in NALCN. The identification of NALCN as a critical regulator of SNr neuron firing provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying basal ganglia function and dysfunction.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals that NALCN is the molecular basis of the depolarizing leak current that supports the spontaneous firing of SNr neurons. The expression of NALCN in SNr neurons is essential for the maintenance of tonic activity, and its disruption may contribute to basal ganglia-related motor disorders. These findings have important implications for our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying basal ganglia function and dysfunction, and highlight the potential of NALCN as a therapeutic target for the treatment of motor disorders associated with basal ganglia dysfunction.

    **Title:** Microfluidic Droplet-Based Cultivation and High-Throughput Screening for the Discovery of Novel Antibiotics

    **Research Question:** Can a microfluidic droplet-based cultivation system be used to increase the throughput and sensitivity of microbiological cultivation, allowing for the discovery of novel antibiotics from uncultured microorganisms?

    **Methods:** We developed a droplet-based microscale cultivation system coupled to a high-throughput screening for antimicrobial activity prior to strain isolation. The system consisted of a modular droplet-microfluidic platform that mimicked environmental conditions in situ. A miniaturized droplet volume of 200 pL facilitated high-density long-term cultivation of 2 million compartmentalized single cells in a total volume of 1.5 mL. Reporter strains were added to the cultivation compartments to screen for antibiotic production.

    **Main Findings:** We demonstrated that highly parallelized in-droplet cultivation starting from single cells resulted in the cultivation of yet uncultured species and a significantly higher bacterial diversity than standard agar plate cultivation. Strains able to inhibit intact reporter strains were isolated from the system, and a variety of antimicrobial compounds were detected for a selected potent antibiotic producer.

    **Implications:** The combination of bacterial strain discovery during the in-droplet cultivation phase with subsequent selection of the most auspicious candidates for natural product biosynthesis through a highly robust reporter assay will streamline efforts towards new scaffold structures for the development of antibiotics.

    **Conclusions:** Our microfluidic droplet-based cultivation system has the potential to revolutionize the discovery of novel antibiotics by increasing the throughput and sensitivity of microbiological cultivation. The ability to culture uncultured microorganisms and detect antimicrobial compounds in a high-throughput manner will accelerate the development of new antibiotics to combat the growing problem of antibiotic resistance. Furthermore, the system provides a genotype-phenotype linkage for secreted products, allowing for the identification and characterization of novel antimicrobial compounds.

    **Title:** Genotype-Directed Adjunctive Glucocorticoid Therapy for Tuberculous Meningitis: A Bayesian Analysis of LTA4H Genotype and Disease Severity

    **Background:** Tuberculous meningitis (TBM) is a devastating disease with high mortality rates, despite adjunctive anti-inflammatory corticosteroids reducing mortality by only 30%. Recent studies have suggested that the LTA4H TT genotype, which regulates inflammatory eicosanoids, may be a critical determinant of inflammation and response to corticosteroid treatment.

    **Research Question:** Does the LTA4H TT genotype confer a survival benefit in patients with TBM, and is this benefit influenced by disease severity?

    **Methods:** We conducted a Bayesian analysis of two studies, one from Vietnam and one from Indonesia, to investigate the association between LTA4H genotype and survival in patients with TBM.

    **Main Findings:** Our analysis revealed that the LTA4H TT genotype confers a survival benefit that begins early and continues long-term in both populations. However, this benefit is nullified in the most severe cases with high early mortality.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that LTA4H genotyping, combined with disease severity assessment, may be used to target glucocorticoid therapy to patients most likely to benefit from it. This personalized approach may improve treatment outcomes for patients with TBM.

    **Conclusions:** Our study provides evidence that the LTA4H TT genotype is a critical determinant of inflammation and response to corticosteroid treatment in TBM. The nullification of the LTA4H genotype benefit in severe cases may be due to the overriding effect of disease severity, rather than an unknown genetic phenomenon. These findings have important implications for the development of genotype-directed adjunctive glucocorticoid therapy for TBM.

    **Title:** Spatially Regulated Phosphorylation of Shootin1 Mediates Netrin-1 Gradient-Induced Axon Guidance

    **Research Question:** How do growth cones navigate axonal projection in response to guidance cues, and what is the molecular mechanism underlying the transduction of local spatial cues into protrusive forces?

    **Methods:** We used knockout mice of Shootin1 and Netrin-1 to investigate the role of Shootin1 in axon guidance. We examined the expression of Shootin1 in developing forebrain commissural axons and its interaction with the cell adhesion molecule L1-CAM. We also analyzed the effects of Shootin1 phosphorylation on F-actin-adhesion coupling and growth cone migration.

    **Main Findings:** We found that knockout mice of Shootin1 display abnormal projection of forebrain commissural axons, a phenotype similar to that of Netrin-1 knockout mice. Shallow gradients of Netrin-1 elicited highly polarized Pak1-mediated phosphorylation of Shootin1 within growth cones. This phosphorylation increased the interaction between Shootin1 and L1-CAM, promoting F-actin-adhesion coupling and concomitant generation of forces for growth cone migration. Moreover, spatially regulated Shootin1 phosphorylation within growth cones is required for axon turning induced by Netrin-1 gradients.

    **Implications:** Our study defines a mechano-effector for Netrin-1 signaling and demonstrates that Shootin1 phosphorylation is a critical readout for Netrin-1 gradients that results in a directional mechanoresponse for axon guidance. The spatially regulated phosphorylation of Shootin1 within growth cones mediates the gradient reading and mechanoresponse for Netrin-1-induced axon guidance.

    **Conclusions:** Our findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanism of axon guidance and the role of Shootin1 in transducing local spatial cues into protrusive forces. The spatially regulated phosphorylation of Shootin1 within growth cones is essential for axon turning induced by Netrin-1 gradients, and its interaction with L1-CAM promotes F-actin-adhesion coupling and growth cone migration. These results have significant implications for our understanding of axon guidance and the development of novel therapeutic strategies for neurological disorders.

    **Title:** Unveiling the Biophysical Origin of Hummingbird Hum: A Multidisciplinary Approach

    **Research Question:** What is the biophysical origin of the humming sound produced by hummingbirds, and how do oscillating aerodynamic forces contribute to this phenomenon?

    **Methods:** We conducted an in vivo study on six freely hovering Anna’s hummingbirds using a 2176 microphone array for acoustic nearfield holography and a new aerodynamic force platform to directly measure 3D aerodynamic forces. We also developed an idealized acoustic model that integrates wing kinematics and aerodynamic forces to predict the sound radiated by the flapping wings.

    **Main Findings:** Our results show that the hummingbird’s hum arises from the oscillating lift and drag forces on each wing, which generate higher harmonics that contribute to the characteristic timbre and radiated power of their flapping wings. We found that larger birds are quieter, while elongated flies are louder, due to allometric deviation. Our model analysis across insects and birds clarifies complex bioacoustic behavior.

    **Implications:** This study provides new insights into the biophysical origin of hummingbird hum, shedding light on the role of oscillating aerodynamic forces in generating sound. Our findings have implications for understanding the evolution of bioacoustic behavior in birds and insects, and may inform the development of novel biomimetic technologies.

    **Conclusions:** Our multidisciplinary approach, combining aerodynamic force platform measurements and acoustic nearfield holography with an idealized acoustic model, provides a comprehensive understanding of the biophysical origin of hummingbird hum. Our results demonstrate that oscillating aerodynamic forces play a crucial role in generating the characteristic sound of flapping wings, and highlight the importance of allometric deviation in shaping bioacoustic behavior. This study contributes to our understanding of the intricate relationships between wing kinematics, aerodynamics, and sound production in birds and insects.

    **Title:** Unraveling the Interplay of Chance, Contingency, and Necessity in Protein Evolution

    **Research Question:** To what extent do chance, contingency, and necessity contribute to the evolution of protein-protein interaction (PPI) specificities in the B-cell lymphoma-2 (BCL-2) family?

    **Methods:** We employed a novel combination of ancestral protein reconstruction and continuous evolution technology to mutate and select proteins in the BCL-2 family, simulating evolutionary trajectories from multiple ancestral proteins under identical selection pressures.

    **Main Findings:** Our results demonstrate that, over long historical timescales, contingency steadily erases necessity and overwhelms chance as the primary cause of acquired sequence variation. We found that trajectories launched from phylogenetically distant proteins yielded virtually no common mutations, even under strong and identical selection pressures. Chance arose due to the numerous sets of mutations that could alter specificity at any timepoint, while contingency arose from historical substitutions that changed these sets.

    **Implications:** Our findings suggest that present-day BCL-2 family proteins, and potentially many others, are largely idiosyncratic products of their particular and unpredictable histories, shaped by the interaction of accumulated chance events during descent from common ancestors with necessity imposed by physics, chemistry, and natural selection.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals that contingency plays a profound role in BCL-2 family protein sequence evolution under laboratory selection for new functions. The results indicate that the paths through sequence space leading to historical PPI specificities changed repeatedly during the BCL-2 family's history, even during intervals when the proteins' PPI binding profiles did not evolve. This suggests that chance can strongly influence the outcomes of adaptive evolution only when multiple paths to a selected phenotype are accessible with similar probabilities of being taken. Our study highlights the importance of considering contingency as a primary factor shaping the outcomes of evolution, in addition to selection, drift, and mutation.

    **Title:** Unraveling the Complex History of Genetic Diversity in Sub-Saharan Africa: A Genome-Wide Analysis of Haplotype Sharing and Gene-Flow

    **Research Question:** What are the patterns of genetic diversity and gene-flow in sub-Saharan Africa, and how have they been shaped by historical events such as admixture and population expansions?

    **Methods:** We conducted a genome-wide, haplotype-based analysis of a collection of 48 sub-Saharan African groups to characterize the structure of genetic diversity and gene-flow in the region.

    **Main Findings:** Our analysis reveals that coastal populations in sub-Saharan Africa experienced an influx of Eurasian haplotypes over the last 7000 years, while Eastern and Southern Niger-Congo speaking groups share ancestry with Central West Africans as a result of recent population expansions. Furthermore, we found that most sub-Saharan populations share ancestry with groups from outside of their current geographic region as a result of gene-flow within the last 4000 years.

    **Implications:** Our study provides new insights into the complex history of genetic diversity in sub-Saharan Africa, highlighting the importance of admixture and gene-flow in shaping the genetic landscape of the continent. These findings have significant implications for studies of genetic epidemiology, as they provide a quantitative measure of the coancestry between groups and identify the dominant events that have shaped current genetic diversity in sub-Saharan Africa.

    **Conclusions:** Our in-depth analysis of haplotype sharing across different ethno-linguistic groups and the recent movement of alleles into new environments provides a nuanced understanding of the genetic history of sub-Saharan Africa. These observations are relevant to studying genotype-phenotype associations in Africa and highlight the importance of considering the complex history of genetic diversity in the region when interpreting genetic data. Our study contributes to the growing body of evidence that admixture and gene-flow have played a significant role in shaping the genetic diversity of human populations across the globe.

    **Title:** Mechanisms of Spindle Scaling during Xenopus laevis Embryogenesis: A Ratiometric Control of Microtubule Destabilization

    **Research Question:** How does the mitotic spindle adapt to changing cell dimensions during early development in Xenopus laevis embryos?

    **Methods:** We established an in vitro system using cytoplasmic extracts from Xenopus laevis embryos to recapitulate in vivo spindle size differences between stage 3 (4 cells, 37 µm) and stage 8 (∼4000 cells, 18 µm). We identified the kinesin-13 kif2a as a driver of developmental spindle scaling.

    **Main Findings:** We found that kif2a's microtubule-destabilizing activity is inhibited in stage 3 spindles by the transport receptor importin α, and activated in stage 8 when importin α partitions to a membrane pool. Altering spindle size in developing embryos impaired spindle orientation during metaphase, but chromosome segregation remained robust.

    **Implications:** Our results suggest that spindle size in Xenopus development is coupled to cell size through a ratiometric mechanism controlling microtubule destabilization. This mechanism is consistent with the gradual change from meiotic to mitotic spindle morphology observed during early mouse development.

    **Conclusions:** We have elucidated a mechanism that coordinates spindle size with cell size during Xenopus laevis development through differential activation of the kinesin-13 kif2a. This work highlights the importance of spindle size in maintaining spindle orientation in large cells of early Xenopus development. Our findings suggest that the changing physical features of the cell can drive intracellular scaling through biochemical partitioning and steady-state redistribution of activities. This study provides new insights into the mechanisms of spindle scaling during vertebrate embryogenesis.

    **Title:** Discovery of Tandem Guanine-Phosphoribosyl Pyrophosphate (PRPP) Riboswitch Aptamers Approximating an IMPLY Boolean Logic Gate in Bacteria

    **Research Question:** Can tandem arrangements of ligand-binding riboswitch aptamers approximate the function of specific two-input Boolean logic gates to regulate gene expression in bacteria?

    **Methods:** In this study, we identified and characterized riboswitch aptamers for phosphoribosyl pyrophosphate (PRPP) that exist either in singlet arrangements or in tandem with guanine aptamers. We used in-line probing experiments to examine the aptamer regions of the RNA and assessed the logic gate function of the tandem riboswitch through interactions between the two aptamers and the adjoining expression platform.

    **Main Findings:** We discovered that tandem guanine-PRPP aptamers can bind target ligands, either independently or in combination, to approximate the function expected for an IMPLY Boolean logic gate. This allows for the regulation of transcription of messenger RNAs for de novo purine biosynthesis in bacteria. Our results demonstrate that these tandem riboswitch systems can sense two ancient ribonucleotide derivatives to control synthesis of RNA molecules, supporting the hypothesis that RNA World organisms could have managed a complex metabolic state without the assistance of protein regulatory factors.

    **Implications:** The existence of sophisticated all-RNA regulatory systems that sense two ancient ribonucleotide derivatives has significant implications for our understanding of the complexity of systems used for metabolite monitoring and biological regulation in ancient RNA World organisms. Furthermore, our findings suggest that additional variations of two-input Boolean logic gates may be represented by tandem riboswitches that have yet to be discovered in modern cells.

    **Conclusions:** The discovery of tandem guanine-PRPP riboswitches serves as additional demonstrations of how complex molecular devices can be formed purely from RNA. Our results support the idea that RNA aptamers that selectively bound biosynthetic precursors in ancient times might have persisted to serve as riboswitch aptamers in modern cells. This study highlights the potential for RNA to play a central role in the regulation of gene expression and provides new insights into the evolution of complex biological systems.

    **Title:** Regulation of Multivesicular Release by the Readily-Releasable Pool: A Novel Mechanism for Synaptic Diversity

    **Research Question:** What is the source of variability in the number of neurotransmitter-filled vesicles released into the synaptic cleft with each action potential, and how does this variability contribute to the diversity of synaptic function across brain regions?

    **Methods:** We used mouse brain slices to investigate the regulation of multivesicular release (MVR) and its relationship to the readily-releasable pool (RRP) of vesicles.

    **Main Findings:** Our results demonstrate that the number of vesicles released is regulated by the size of the RRP, upstream of vesicle release probability. We found that protein kinase A (PKA) and its vesicle-associated target, synapsin, dynamically control release site occupancy to dictate the number of vesicles released without altering release probability. This suggests that the RRP is a functional measure of the number of release-competent vesicles per active zone, which is under the control of synapsins and constrains the number of vesicles released.

    **Implications:** These findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms that control MVR and contribute to the functional diversity of synaptic signaling. Our results suggest that the size of the RRP, rather than vesicle release probability, is a key determinant of the number of vesicles released. This has important implications for understanding synaptic diversity and developing unifying models of presynaptic function that account for multiple presynaptic parameters.

    **Conclusions:** Our study reveals a novel mechanism for regulating MVR, which is distinct from the prevailing view that release mode is determined by vesicle release probability. These findings highlight the importance of the RRP in controlling the number of vesicles released and suggest that synapsin and PKA play a critical role in regulating release site occupancy. Our results provide a new framework for understanding the diversity of synaptic function across brain regions and have important implications for the development of novel therapeutic strategies for neurological disorders.
Here is a 300-400 word abstract for the given biology research article:

    **Title:** Mechanistic Insights into HIV-1 Genome Selection and Packaging: A Minimal System Approach

    **Research Question:** How does HIV-1 Gag selectively package its dimeric, unspliced viral RNA genome in the presence of a large excess of cytosolic human RNAs?

    **Methods:** We employed a minimal system consisting of purified RNAs, recombinant HIV-1 Gag, and giant unilamellar vesicles (GUVs) to recapitulate the selective packaging of the 5' untranslated region (UTR) of the HIV-1 genome in the presence of excess competitor RNA.

    **Main Findings:** Our results demonstrate that mutations in the CA-CTD domain of Gag, which subtly affect its self-assembly on membranes, abrogate RNA selectivity. Furthermore, we found that tRNA suppresses Gag membrane binding less when Gag has bound viral RNA. These findings suggest that the ability of HIV-1 Gag to selectively package its RNA genome and its self-assembly on membranes are interdependent on one another.

    **Implications:** Our study provides new insights into the mechanism of HIV-1 genome selection and packaging. We propose a model in which a viral RNA with multiple high-affinity binding sites for Gag can recruit several Gag molecules to the membrane if only one of them dissociates from its membrane-occluding tRNA. This model highlights the importance of membrane-localized Gag assembly events in HIV-1 genome selection and packaging.

    **Conclusions:** Our results demonstrate that the NC domain of Gag is necessary but not sufficient for the selective packaging of the HIV-1 genomic RNA. Lattice contacts conferring Gag's ability to multimerize on a membrane are crucial for the selective packaging of viral RNA. The 5'UTR is recruited to membranes by multimerized Gag without any gross changes to its secondary structure. Moreover, the association of Gag with tRNA serves to inhibit genome-less virus assembly. Overall, our study provides a mechanistic understanding of HIV-1 genome selection and packaging, which has important implications for the development of novel antiretroviral therapies.
